Rational Judgments
intermediate
12 min
Razi's First Argument: Actions Are Compelled or Accidental
Razi argues human actions are either compulsory or accidental, refuting rational good/evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that human actions are either compelled or accidental, thereby refuting the existence of rationally inherent good and evil.

Core Idea
Human actions are either compelled or accidental; neither allows free choice.

Razi begins his proof against rational good and evil by analyzing the nature of human action. He argues that every action, when examined, falls into one of two categories: either it is compelled by its causes, or it occurs by mere accident. In both cases, genuine choice is absent, and without choice, moral categories like good and evil lose their foundation. This passage walks through the logical steps of this argument and answers an important objection about God's actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: a premise, a detailed proof, and an objection with response.
The argument is highly logical and proceeds through conditional steps.
2
Identify the two categories of action
Razi states that actions are either compulsory (اضطرارية) or accidental (اتفاقية). Understand what each means in context.
Compulsory means the act necessarily occurs when its causes are present; accidental means it occurs without a cause.
3
Trace the logical chain
Follow the reasoning: capacity + motive → either act is necessary or not. If necessary, it's compulsory. If not necessary, then either preponderator makes it necessary (compulsory) or no preponderator (accidental).
This establishes that all actions fall into one of the two categories.
4
Understand the objection and response
The objection asks: doesn't this argument apply to God? Razi responds by distinguishing between human and divine will: human will is created and dependent, divine will is eternal and self-sufficient.
This preserves God's freedom while denying human rational moral qualities.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Since choice is eliminated in both categories, rational good and evil cannot exist. Consider how this refutes the Mu'tazila position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الأولى : إن أفعال العباد إما اضطرارية وإما اتفاقية. وعلى التقديرين، فالقول بالحسن والقبح العقليين باطل بيان المقدمة الأولى : إن صدور الفعل عند حصول القدرة والداعي . إما أن يكون واجبا أو لا. فإن كان واجبا كان فعل العبد اضطراريا، لأن حصول القدرة والداعي ليس بالعبد وإلا لزم التسلسل وإذا كان كذلك فعند حصولهما يكون الفعل واجبا، وعندما لا يكونان حاصلين كان الفعل ممتنعا فكان الاضطرار لازما لا محالة. وأما إن لم يكن حصول الفعل عند حصول القدرة والداعي واجبا، فأما أن يتوقف رجحان الفعل على الترك على مرجح أو لا يتوقف . فإن توقف كان حصول ذلك الفعل عند حصول ذلك المرجح واجبا . وإلا عاد الكلام الأول ولزم التسلسل . وإذا كان واجبا عاد القول بأن حصول الفعل عند حصول القدرة والداعي اضطراري. وأما إن لم يتوقف رجحان الفعل على الترك على مرجح كان رجحان الفعل اتفاقيا، أنه اتفق حصول هذا الرجحان، لا لمؤثر أصلا، فلا يكون ذلك الرجحان من العبد . فثبت أن أفعال العباد إما اضطرارية وإما اتفاقية وإذا كان كذلك وجب أن يكون القول بالحسن والقبح العقلي باطلا. أما على قولنا فظاهر، وأما على قول المعتزلة فلأن كل واحدة من هاتين الحالتين تنافي الاختيار، وعند فقدان الاختيار لا يبقى الحسن والقبح. فإن قيل: فهذا الكلام بتمامه قائم في الغائب، فينبغي أن لا يصدر من الله تعالى فعل حسن ولا فعل قبيح قلنا : قد ذكرنا في مسألة خلق الأفعال أن صدور الفعل عن القادر موقوف على المرجح، وذلك المرجح هو الإرادة والإرادة في حق العبد محدثة فافتقرت إلى الخالق والموجد، فكان هذا المعنى لازما في حق العبد بخلاف الباري تعالى فإن إرادته قديمة أزلية فاستغنت عن المؤثر . فلم يلزم الجبر في حقه.
The first proof: The actions of servants are either compulsory or accidental. On both suppositions, the assertion of rational good and evil is false. Explanation of the first premise: The occurrence of the act when capacity and motive are present — either it is necessary or not. If it is necessary, the servant's action is compulsory, because the presence of capacity and motive is not from the servant (otherwise infinite regress). If that is so, when they are present the act is necessary, and when they are not present the act is impossible, so compulsion is unavoidable. But if the occurrence of the act when capacity and motive are present is not necessary, then either the preponderance of the act over omission depends on a preponderator or not. If it depends, then the occurrence of that act when that preponderator is present is necessary, otherwise the first argument returns and infinite regress follows. If it is necessary, this returns to saying that the occurrence of the act when capacity and motive are present is compulsory. But if the preponderance of the act over omission does not depend on a preponderator, then the preponderance of the act is accidental, because it happens to occur with no effect at all, so that preponderance is not from the servant. So it is established that the actions of servants are either compulsory or accidental. If that is so, the assertion of rational good and evil must be false. As for our school, it is obvious; as for the Mu'tazila, each of these two states precludes choice, and when choice is absent good and evil do not remain. If it is said: This entire argument applies to the Unseen, so it should follow that no good or evil act issues from Allah, may He be exalted. We say: We have mentioned in the question of the creation of acts that the issuance of an act by a powerful agent depends on a preponderator, and that preponderator is will, and will in the case of the servant is originated, so it needs a Creator and Existentiator, thus this meaning is necessary for the servant, unlike the Blessed and Exalted, for His will is eternal, so it is independent of an effect, so compulsion does not apply to Him.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof against rational good and evil. Razi argues that every human action is either necessary (compulsory) or accidental (without cause). He proves this by analyzing the conditions for action: when capacity and motive are present, either the act necessarily occurs (compulsory) or it does not. If it does not, then either a preponderator makes it occur (which again makes it necessary) or it occurs without a preponderator (making it accidental). In both cases, there is no genuine free choice. Since choice is a precondition for moral responsibility (good/evil), rational good/evil cannot exist. An objection is raised: this argument would apply to God, making His acts also without moral quality. Razi answers by distinguishing: for God, His will is eternal and self-sufficient, so no external compulsion; for humans, their will is created and dependent, so the argument holds.

Key Terms
اضطرارية
اتفاقية
القدرة
الداعي
المرجح
الإرادة
الاختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Razi's argument denies any human responsibility for actions.
Correction: Razi is not denying responsibility but refuting the Mu'tazili claim that actions have inherent moral qualities independent of divine decree. He still affirms that actions are created by God and attributed to humans in a secondary sense.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's first argument, when capacity and motive are present and the act is not necessary, what happens if the preponderance of act over omission depends on a preponderator?
A) The act is accidental.
B) The act becomes necessary, hence compulsory.
C) The act is voluntary.
D) The act is impossible.

Key Takeaway
Razi's first argument reduces all human actions to either compulsory or accidental, eliminating the possibility of free choice and thus undermining rational good and evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a decision you made today that you considered a 'free choice.' Now trace the capacity and motive behind it. Can you honestly say you had any real control over their presence? How does Razi's argument unsettle your sense of agency?
?
If all your actions are either compelled or accidental, what becomes of your responsibility towards Allah? Are you willing to let go of the pride that comes from thinking you deserve credit for good deeds?
?
Razi distinguishes human will (created) from divine will (eternal). When you sin, do you secretly resent your lack of control? Or can you accept that even your rebellion is enclosed within Allah's will, and that true humility begins with acknowledging your utter dependence?

Evil Cannot Be Caused by a Negative Attribute
Razi argues that evil cannot be intrinsic because its cause would be negative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that if injustice were evil due to its essence, then a positive quality (evil) would be caused by a non‑existent cause, which is impossible.

Core Idea
A positive quality cannot be caused by a non-being.

Razi now presents his second argument against the view that moral evil is intrinsic to actions. He carefully analyzes the concept of 'injustice' (ظلم) and shows that its definition includes a negative condition—the harm being undeserved. Since non-being cannot cause an existing attribute, the evil of injustice cannot be due to its essence. This reinforces the Ash'ari position that moral values depend on revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it, then read the literal translation. Let the logical flow of Razi's argument become familiar.
Pay attention to the key terms: الظلم, القبح, العلة العدمية.
2
Understand how 'evil' is established as existing
Razi argues that 'evil' (القبح) is an existing attribute because it is the opposite of 'not-evil', which is non-being. Make sure you follow this step.
3
Analyze the composite nature of injustice
Injustice is defined as a composite essence that includes the condition that the harm is undeserved (غير مستحق). This condition is a negation, a 'قيد عدمي'.
This negative component is key to the argument.
4
See why non-being cannot be a cause
A non-existent cannot cause something existent. So if the essence of injustice includes a non-existent component, that essence cannot be the cause of the positive attribute 'evil'.
Razi explains that non-being is 'pure negation and absolute privation'.
5
Connect to the conclusion
Therefore, the evil of injustice cannot be due to an intrinsic quality; it must be determined by divine command. This is the second proof against the Mu'tazilite view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : لو كان الظلم قبيحا لوجه عائد إليه لزم تعليل الحكم الوجودي بالعلة العدمية. وهذا محال فذلك محال بيان الملازمة : إن القبيح نقيض اللا قبيح واللا قبيح محمول على العدم والمحمول على العدم عدم فلا قبيح عدم فوجب أن يكون القبح صفة موجودة. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : الظلم ماهية مركبة من أمور: منها كون ذلك الإضرار غير مستحق . ومعلوم أن كونه غير مستحق قيد عدمي، فإذا عللنا قبح الظلم بكونه ظلما، وكونه ظلما ماهية مركبة من قيود أحدها قيد عدمي، فحينئذ لم يكن مجموع القيود المعتبرة في كونه ظلما أمرا وجوديا فيلزم تعليل القبح الذي صفة موجودة بالعدم، وهو محال وإنما قلنا إن تعليل ذلك الموجود بالعدم محال، وذلك لأن العدم نفي محض وسلب صرف وإيجاد الغير يعتمد كونه موجودا وإذا لم يكن له ثبوت وتحقق أصلا، امتنع كونه علة لغيره.
The second proof: If injustice were evil due to a quality returning to it, this would require explaining a positive attribute (the evil) by a negative cause, which is impossible; thus the premise is impossible. Explanation of the implication: Evil is the opposite of not-evil, and not-evil is predicated of non-being, and what is predicated of non-being is non-being, so not-evil is non-being. Therefore it must be that evil is an existing attribute. If this is established, we say: Injustice is a composite essence: among its components is that the harm is undeserved. It is known that its being undeserved is a negative qualification. So if we explain the evil of injustice by its being injustice, and its being injustice is a composite essence one of whose components is a negative qualification, then the totality of the qualifications considered in its being injustice is not something existent, thus we would be explaining evil (which is an existing attribute) by non-being, which is impossible. We said that explaining an existent by non-being is impossible because non-being is pure negation and absolute privation, and causing something else depends on it being existent. If it has no subsistence and reality at all, it cannot be a cause for another.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof. Razi argues that if we say injustice is evil because of its essence (i.e., a quality returning to it), then we must explain the existence of evil (a positive quality) by something that is non-existent, because the essence of injustice includes a negative component (the harm is not deserved). Since a non-existent cannot be a cause for an existent, this is impossible. Therefore, the evil of injustice cannot be due to an intrinsic quality; it must be a divine ruling. Razi first sets up that 'evil' is an existing attribute (since it is the opposite of 'not-evil' which is non-being). Then he analyzes the definition of injustice: it includes the negative condition 'not being deserved'. Hence the composite essence of injustice is partly non-existential, so it cannot be the cause of the positive attribute of evil.

Key Terms
الظلم
القبح
العلة العدمية
الماهية المركبة
القيد العدمي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The evil of injustice is a property inherent in the action itself, independent of divine command.
Correction: Razi shows that the definition of injustice includes a negation (undeserved harm), which is non-being. Since non-being cannot be a cause, the evil cannot be inherent; it must depend on divine ruling.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi consider it impossible for injustice to be evil due to an intrinsic quality?
A) Because injustice is not a real action
B) Because the essence of injustice includes a negative component (harm being undeserved), making it a non-being unable to cause the positive quality of evil
C) Because injustice is not morally relevant
D) Because evil must be a negative attribute itself

Key Takeaway
Evil cannot be an intrinsic quality of an action because its definition includes a negative component, and a non-being cannot cause an existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see injustice in the world, do you instinctively attribute its evil to the act itself or to God's command? How does this lesson challenge you to reconsider the source of moral judgments?
?
If the evil of injustice is not intrinsic but a divine ruling, how does that affect your understanding of God's wisdom in allowing harm? Are you willing to submit to His judgment even when you do not see the intrinsic reason?
?
Razi argues that a non-being cannot cause an existent. Reflect on your own life: have you ever let a 'lack' (like absence of resources, knowledge, or recognition) define your identity or actions? How can you shift your focus from what you lack to the positive attributes God has given you?

The Third Proof: Lying Is Not Evil by Essence
Razi argues that lying's evil is not essential by showing exceptions like saving prophets.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam al-Razi argues that the evil of lying cannot be due to its essence, using the example of saving prophets from harm.

Core Idea
If lying were evil by essence, saving a prophet by lying would be evil—but it is not.

In this passage, al-Razi presents a decisive argument against the view that lying is inherently evil. He uses a powerful counterexample: a lie that saves a prophet from murder. If the evil were intrinsic, such a lie would still be evil, but we know it is not. He then anticipates and responds to objections, showing the sophistication of his method. By studying this argument, you will see how rational theologians test claims about moral values by considering necessary consequences.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow: argument, then objection, then the beginning of an answer. Then read the literal translation to confirm your understanding.
Notice the structure: a 'law' statement, then a counterexample, then an objection with two parts.
2
Identify the core argument
Identify Razi's claim: 'If the evil of lying were because of its being lying, then every lie would have to be evil.' Find the specific counterexample: a lie that saves prophets from being killed.
The argument is a conditional followed by a denial of the consequent.
3
Analyze the objection
The opponent says lying is always evil, but in such cases one should use equivocation (mu'arid) instead of lying. Alternatively, the opponent argues that the cause (lying) can be overridden by a stronger factor (mani' wa mu'arid). Understand both parts.
The opponent is trying to preserve the claim that lying is inherently evil.
4
Examine Razi's answer (as given)
Razi begins his answer by assuming the discussion is about situations where equivocation is not possible. This sidesteps the first objection. Consider why this is a strong move.
The answer is incomplete in the text, but the logic is clear: if equivocation is impossible, the opponent must rely on the override claim.
5
Reflect on the implication
This argument does not justify lying in all cases. It only shows that the evil of lying is not an essential property; it can depend on context and intention. Consider how this affects our understanding of moral rules.
This is a key insight in Islamic theology: moral values are often determined by reasons and circumstances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : لو كان قبح الكذب لكونه كذبا لوجب أن يقبح كل ما كان كذبا، وكان يلزم أن يكون الكذب الذي يكون سببا لخلاص الأنبياء والرسل صلوات الله وسلامه عليهم عند إقدام الظلم عليهم بالقتل وأنواع الإيذاء قبيحا . ومعلوم أنه ليس كذلك فدل هذا على أن كونه كذبا ليس علة للقبح. فإن قيل: الكذب في جميع المواضع قبيح. والواجب في هذه الصور إنما هو ذكر المعاريض . كما قيل : (إن) في المعاريض لمندوحة عن الكذب) وأيضا : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن كونه كذبا علة للقبح . إلا أن الحكم تخلف عن العلة في هذه الصورة لقيام مانع ومعارض. والجواب عن السؤال الأول: إنا نفرض أن الكلام فيما إذا لم يكن التعريض
The third proof: If the evil of lying were because of its being lying, then every lie would have to be evil, and it would follow that the lie which is a cause of deliverance of the prophets and messengers (peace be upon them) when tyrants proceed to kill them and various forms of harm would be evil. It is known that it is not so, and this indicates that its being lying is not the cause of evil. If it is said: Lying in all situations is evil, and the obligation in these cases is only to use ambiguous statements (mu'arid), as it is said: 'Indeed in ambiguous statements there is a way out from lying.' Also, why may it not be said that its being lying is the cause of evil, but the ruling is absent from the cause in this case due to the presence of an obstacle and countervailing factor? The answer to the first question: We assume that the discussion concerns when there is no ambiguous statement (mu'arid)
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof. Razi argues that if lying were evil because it is lying (i.e., the essence of lying is the cause of its evil), then all instances of lying would necessarily be evil. But we know that lying to save a prophet from murder is not evil; indeed it might be obligatory. Thus, the essence of lying is not the cause of its evil. An objection is raised: the opponent may claim that lying is always evil, but in such cases one should use equivocation (ta'rid) to avoid lying. Also, the opponent may say that the cause (lying) can be overridden by a stronger factor (مانع ومعارض). Razi begins his answer by assuming that the discussion concerns cases where equivocation is not possible, but the text cuts off before completing the response.

Key Terms
الكذب
القبح
العلة
المعاريض
المانع
المعارض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument proves that lying is always permissible or never evil.
Correction: Razi only argues that lying is not evil because of its essence; he allows that it can be evil for other reasons (e.g., harm). The example of saving prophets is a specific exception, not a blanket permission.

Checkpoint
What does Razi claim would follow if lying were evil because it is lying?
A) Lying would be forbidden only when it harms someone
B) Every lie, including those that save prophets, would be evil
C) Lying would be permissible in dire situations
D) The evil of lying would be a matter of opinion

Key Takeaway
This argument shows that moral judgments like 'lying is evil' can have exceptions based on context, meaning the evil is not part of the essence of the act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever told a lie that you believed was justified because of a greater good? How does this lesson's argument that the evil of lying is not absolute confront your personal moral assumptions?
?
If lying is not evil by its essence, then what makes it wrong in most situations? Does this lesson make you uncomfortable with the idea that there are no inviolable rules? Or does it free you to reason more deeply about morality?
?
The proof hinges on a hypothetical where you could save a prophet by lying. But in your own life, when you face a situation where truth-telling would harm someone, do you honestly rely on rational proofs like this, or do you follow your gut? What would it take for you to revise a deeply held moral rule?

Opening Argument Against Rational Good and Evil
Razi opens the 25th question with an initial argument and objection-answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument against rational good and evil and his response to the objection about God's acts.

Core Idea
Good and evil depend on free choice; God's eternal will avoids compulsion.

In this lesson, Imam Razi opens the 25th question of his Forty Principles by targeting the Mu'tazilite doctrine of rational good and evil. He presents a concise argument that these moral categories require free choice, which is absent in the Mu'tazilite framework. Then he answers an objection concerning God's own actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully, then the English translation. Try to grasp the overall structure: claim, objection, answer.
Notice the phrase 'أما على قول المعتزلة' which signals the target.
2
Identify the core argument
Razi argues that rational good and evil are false because they negate free choice (ikhtiyar). Understand how he connects good/evil to choice and why he says the Mu'tazilite position is self-defeating.
The phrase 'كل واحدة من هاتين الحالتين تنافي الاختيار' is key.
3
Analyze the objection and answer
The objector says Razi's reasoning applies to God as well. Follow Razi's answer: he distinguishes human will (created, needs a preponderating factor) from God's will (eternal, self-sufficient). See how this preserves God's freedom from compulsion.
The key concept is 'المرجح' (preponderating factor).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الخامسة والعشرون
يكون القول بالحسن والقبح العقلي باطلا. أما على قولنا فظاهر، وأما على قول المعتزلة فلأن كل واحدة من هاتين الحالتين تنافي الاختيار، وعند فقدان الاختيار لا يبقى الحسن والقبح.
فإن قيل: فهذا الكلام بتمامه قائم في الغائب، فينبغي أن لا يصدر من الله تعالى فعل حسن ولا فعل قبيح قلنا: قد ذكرنا في مسألة خلق الأفعال أن صدور الفعل عن القادر موقوف على المرجح، وذلك المرجح هو الإرادة والإرادة في حق العبد محدثة فافتقرت إلى الخالق والموجد، فكان هذا المعنى لازما في حق العبد بخلاف الباري تعالى فإن إرادته قديمة أزلية فاستغنت عن المؤثر. فلم يلزم الجبر في حقه.
The twenty-fifth question: The doctrine of rational good and evil is false. As for our view, it is apparent; as for the view of the Mu'tazila, because each one of these two states contradicts free choice, and when free choice is absent, good and evil do not remain. If it is said: This entire discourse applies to the unseen (al-gha'ib), so it should be that from God Almighty no good act and no evil act proceeds. We say: We have mentioned in the question of the creation of acts (khalq al-af'al) that the occurrence of an act from the powerful (al-qadir) is dependent upon a preponderating factor (al-murajjih), and that preponderating factor is the will (al-irada). The will in the case of the servant is originated (muhdatha), so it is in need of the Creator and the Giver of existence. Thus this meaning is necessary in the case of the servant, unlike the Blessed and Exalted, for His will is eternal (qadima azaliyya), so it is independent of any effect. Hence compulsion (al-jabr) does not apply in His case.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage introduces the 25th question of the book. Razi states his position: the doctrine of rational good and evil (al-husn wa al-qubh al-'aqli) is false. He first notes that for his own school (Ash'ari) this is obvious, but he aims to refute the Mu'tazila. He provides a concise argument: good and evil depend on free choice (ikhtiyar), but for the Mu'tazila, the two states (good and evil) are mutually exclusive and negate choice. When choice is absent, good and evil cease to apply. An objection is raised: if this argument is applied to God (the unseen), then no act of God would be good or evil, which seems problematic. Razi answers by referencing his earlier discussion on the creation of acts: every act from a capable agent requires a preponderating factor (murajjih), which is the will. For humans, the will is created and dependent on a creator, so the argument would imply compulsion (jabr). For God, however, His will is eternal and self-sufficient, thus no compulsion arises. Therefore, the objection is invalid because the analogy between God and humans fails in this respect.

Key Terms
الحسن والقبح العقلي
الاختيار
الإرادة
الجبر
خلق الأفعال
المرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Razi is denying any moral assessment of God's acts, claiming God's actions are neither good nor evil.
Correction: Razi is only denying the Mu'tazilite rational basis for moral categories; he does not deny that God's acts are good in a theological sense, as established by revelation.

Checkpoint
What is the key difference Razi identifies between God and humans in the context of free choice?
A) Humans have free will but God does not.
B) God's will is eternal and self-sufficient, while human will is created and dependent.
C) Humans are compelled in their actions, while God is not.
D) God's actions are subject to rational good and evil, but humans' are not.

Key Takeaway
Razi argues that rational good and evil are incompatible with free choice, and God's eternal will exempts Him from the implications of this argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a moral judgment, do you subconsciously rely on your own reason as the ultimate arbiter of good and evil, or do you actively submit to God's revealed commands? How does Razi's argument challenge your default tendency?
?
Your will is originated (muhdatha) and dependent on God. In your daily decisions, do you acknowledge this dependence by seeking God's guidance, or do you act as if your will is self-sufficient? What concrete change can you make today to align your decision-making with this truth?
?
If free choice is essential for moral accountability, are you genuinely exercising your free will in obedience to God, or are you passively following desires and social pressures? How can you assert your free choice in a way that honors your Creator?

Razi's Third Proof: Lying to Save Prophets
Razi presents a proof, two objections, and two responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that lying cannot be inherently evil because saving prophets through lies is not considered evil, and how he defends this against objections.

Core Idea
If lying were evil inherently, saving prophets by lying would be evil—but it isn't.

Razi presents his third proof against the idea that lying is evil because it is a lie. He argues that if lying were inherently evil, then any lie, including one that saves a prophet's life, would be evil. But we know saving prophets is good, so lying cannot be inherently evil. He then addresses two objections: that prophets use equivocation instead of lying, and that an impediment might suspend the judgment. His responses aim to show that the opponent's position leads to skepticism about moral judgments.

Guided Path
1
Read Razi's main argument
Read the Arabic and translation of the third proof carefully. Note the logical structure: if A then B, but not B, so not A.
2
Identify the two objections
The opponent raises two points: (1) prophets use equivocation, not lies; (2) an impediment or countervailing factor can remove evil from a lie. Find these in the passage.
3
Analyze Razi's responses
Razi answers the first by giving a scenario where equivocation is impossible. He answers the second by showing that if impediments are allowed, we can never be certain about any lie being evil, leading to mere probability.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this argument matters for theology: if lying is not inherently evil, then God's actions (which never involve lies) are evaluated differently.
This connects to broader discussions of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : لو كان قبح الكذب لكونه كذبا لوجب أن يقبح كل ما كان كذبا، وكان يلزم أن يكون الكذب الذي يكون سببا لخلاص الأنبياء والرسل عليهم السلام عند إقدام الظلم عليهم بالقتل وأنواع الإيذاء قبيحا . ومعلوم أنه ليس كذلك فدل هذا على أن كونه كذبا ليس علة للقبح.
فإن قيل: الكذب في جميع المواضع قبيح. والواجب في هذه الصور إنما هو ذكر المعاريض . كما قيل : إن في المعاريض لمندوحة عن الكذب وأيضا : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن كونه كذبا علة للقبح . إلا أن الحكم تخلف عن العلة في هذه الصورة لقيام مانع ومعارض.
والجواب عن السؤال الأول: إنا نفرض أن الكلام فيما إذا لم يكن التعريض كافيا، كما إذا قال: هل رأيت فلانا في هذه الساعة؟ وهل تعرف مكانه؟ فإن قلت نعم طالبك به وقتله لا محالة وإن قلت : لا . فقد كذبت.
وأما السؤال الثاني فجوابه : إنه لو جاز تخلف القبح عن الكذب لمانع، فلا كذب إلا ويجوز أن يقال له لعله وجد مانع من الموانع يمنعه من قبحه. وعلى هذا التقدير لا يمكنك الحكم القطعي بقبح شيء من الأكاذيب أقصى ما في الباب أن يكون الحاصل هو الظن. وعند هذا لا يمكنك الحكم بقبح الكذب في حق الله تعالى.
The third proof: If lying were evil because it is a lie, then it would be necessary that everything that is a lie be evil. And it would follow that the lie which is a cause for the deliverance of the prophets and messengers, peace be upon them, when oppressors advance upon them with killing and various kinds of harm, would be evil. But it is known that it is not so. So this indicates that its being a lie is not the cause of evil.
If it is said: Lying in all places is evil. What is obligatory in these cases is only the use of equivocations (al-ma'arid), as it is said: 'In equivocations there is an escape from lying.' Also, why is it not permissible to say that its being a lie is the cause of evil, except that the judgment is absent from the cause in this case due to the existence of an impediment (man') and a countervailing factor (mu'arid)?
The answer to the first question: We assume that the discussion is when equivocation is not sufficient, such as when he says: 'Did you see so-and-so at this hour?' and 'Do you know his location?' If you say 'yes', he will demand him from you and kill him without doubt; and if you say 'no', you have lied.
As for the second question, its answer is: If it were permissible for evil to be absent from lying due to an impediment, then there is no lie except that it is permissible to say about it: 'Perhaps an impediment among impediments has occurred preventing its evil.' On this basis, you cannot make a categorical judgment about the evil of any lie; at most, what is obtained is probability (al-zann). And with this, you cannot judge the evil of lying with respect to God Almighty.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage is the third proof. Razi argues that if lying were evil due to its essence (being a lie), then all lies would be evil. Yet lies that save prophets from harm are not considered evil, so lying cannot be inherently evil. Two objections are raised. First, the prophets use equivocations (ta'rid), not actual lies. Razi responds by giving a scenario where equivocation is impossible, such as being questioned about a person's exact whereabouts at that moment, leaving only the option to lie or cause death. Second, the opponent suggests that an impediment (man') might suspend the judgment of evil. Razi counters that if impediments are allowed, then any lie could potentially be excused by an unknown impediment, leading to skepticism (zann) rather than certainty, making it impossible to assert that lying is absolutely evil, especially regarding God.

Key Terms
الكذب
التعريض
المانع
المعارض
الظن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Razi is arguing that lying is always permissible.
Correction: Razi is only arguing that the rational grounding for the evil of lying cannot be its essence because exceptions exist; he is not endorsing lying as always permissible.

Checkpoint

A) He admits that equivocation is always possible.
B) He provides a scenario where equivocation is not possible.
C) He argues that equivocation is also a form of lying.
D) He says the prophets never face such situations.

Key Takeaway
Razi shows that if lying were inherently evil, saving prophets by lying would be evil, which is absurd, and the opponent's exceptions lead to skepticism.

The Tyrant's Threat Dilemma
Razi presents a logical dilemma against the absolute rational evil of lying.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses a logical dilemma to challenge the claim that lying is absolutely evil by reason alone.

Core Idea
A dilemma showing that absolute evil of lying leads to absurdity.

Razi's fourth proof is a clever logical trap. He constructs a scenario where a tyrant threatens to kill, then asks: what is the good action? The answer leads to a contradiction if we assume lying is always evil. This lesson walks you through the dilemma and its devastating conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the concise logical structure.
The argument is a dilemma with two horns.
2
Identify the two options
The dilemma: either the tyrant kills (which is evil) or he does not kill (which makes his statement a lie). Which option is supposedly good?
Razi states that killing is absolutely false as the good option.
3
Follow the chain of reasoning
If lying is evil, then refraining from killing (the only remaining option) leads to a lie, so refraining from killing would entail evil. But what entails evil is itself evil, so refraining from evil would be evil – a contradiction.
This shows the premise 'lying is evil' leads to absurdity.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Since the conclusion is absurd, we cannot judge that lying is absolutely evil by reason. Reason cannot make such a universal moral judgment.
This does not mean lying is good; it means reason alone cannot declare it evil in all cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : لو أن ظالما قال لشخص : إني سأقتلك غدا. فههنا الحسن إما أن يقتله. وهو باطل قطعا . أو أن لا يقتله إلا أنه إذا لم يقتله صار الخبر بأنه سيقتله غدا : كذبا . فلو كان الكذب قبيحا، لكان ترك القتل يلزمه القبيح، وما يلزمه القبيح فهو قبيح أن يكون ترك القبيح قبيحا . ولما كان ذلك باطلا علمنا أنه لا يمكن الحكم فكان يجب يقبح الكذب مطلقا. وبالله التوفيق.
The fourth proof: If a tyrant said to a person: 'I will kill you tomorrow.' Then the good is either that he kills him – which is absolutely false – or that he does not kill him, but when he does not kill him, the report that he will kill him tomorrow becomes a lie. So if lying were evil, then refraining from killing would entail evil, and that which entails evil is evil, so refraining from evil would be evil. Since that is false, we know that it is not possible to judge that lying is absolutely evil. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This is the fourth proof, presented as a dilemma. Razi assumes a tyrant who says, 'I will kill you tomorrow.' The morally good option (hasan) is either to kill (which is evil) or not to kill (which makes the statement a lie). If lying is evil, then refraining from killing (a good act) leads to evil, meaning that avoiding evil would itself be evil – a contradiction. Since this conclusion is absurd, the premise that lying is absolutely evil must be false. The argument shows that the rational judgment of evil cannot be universal.

Key Terms
الظالم
الكذب
القتل
الحسن والقبح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument proves that lying is sometimes good or permissible.
Correction: No, the argument only shows that reason cannot universally declare lying evil; it does not establish any positive moral ruling.

Checkpoint
What conclusion does Razi draw from the tyrant's threat scenario?
A) That killing is always evil.
B) That refraining from evil can be evil.
C) That lying cannot be judged as absolutely evil by reason alone.
D) That the tyrant should be killed.

Key Takeaway
Reason alone cannot declare lying absolutely evil, as shown by the tyrant's dilemma.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you ever justified a small lie because it seemed to produce a better outcome? How does this dilemma challenge you to examine whether you rely on your own reason to decide right from wrong, rather than seeking divine guidance?
?
If reason alone cannot declare lying absolutely evil, on what basis do you decide your own honesty? Are you willing to examine your conscience for the times you have used 'good intentions' to excuse deceit?
?

Distinguishing Natural Inclination from Moral Obligation
Razi responds to the claim that intuitive knowledge of good and evil proves rational moral values.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's key distinction between intuitive moral feelings (natural inclination/aversion) and the objective moral quality that makes acts deserving of praise or blame.

Core Idea
Intuitive moral feelings are not the same as objective moral worth.

This lesson presents a classic objection in Islamic theology: that we intuitively know good and evil independently of revelation. Imam Razi responds by distinguishing between natural inclination and true moral obligation. The opponent's argument, he shows, only proves that we have natural preferences, not that acts have objective moral qualities.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Start by reading the opponent's argument carefully. They claim that rational intuition tells us injustice is evil and beneficence is good, and that this knowledge is not from revelation.
Notice that the opponent appeals to universal human experience.
2
Identify Razi's distinction
Razi's answer hinges on a crucial distinction. He says 'good' and 'evil' here refer to natural inclination and aversion—not to moral praiseworthiness or blameworthiness. Understand this difference.
3
Apply the lesson
Reflect: Can we deduce moral obligation from mere preference? Razi says no. The object of dispute is whether acts deserve reward or punishment in themselves.
This distinction is foundational in the debate over rational ethics in Islam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شبهة للخصم : إنا نعلم ببداهة العقل أن الظلم قبيح وأن الإحسان حسن. وهذا العلم غير مستفاد من الشرع . فإن من ينكر الشرع حصل له هذا العلم. فدل ذلك على أن هذا مستفاد من العقل.
والجواب : إن هذا الحسن والقبح عبارتان عن رغبة الطبع ونفرته . ولا نزاع في أن هذا معلوم بالعقل وإنما النزاع في أن كون الفعل متعلق الذم والعقاب، أو متعلق المدح والثواب. هل هو لأجل صفة قائمة بالفعل؟ وما ذكرتموه لا يدل على ذلك وتمام الكلام فيه مذكور في كتاب المحصول في علم الأصول والله أعلم.
A doubt of the opponent: We know by rational intuition (badahat al-'aql) that injustice is evil and beneficence is good. This knowledge is not derived from revelation (al-shar'). For one who denies revelation still attains this knowledge. So that indicates that this is derived from reason.
The answer: Indeed, these 'good' and 'evil' are expressions for inclination of nature (raghbat al-tab') and its aversion (nafratuhu). There is no dispute that this is known by reason; rather, the dispute is about whether the act's being an object of blame and punishment, or an object of praise and reward, is due to a quality subsisting in the act. What you have mentioned does not indicate that. The full discussion of this is mentioned in the book al-Mahsul fi 'Ilm al-Usul. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage presents an objection (shubhah) from the opponent and Razi's answer. The opponent argues that rational intuition tells us injustice is evil and beneficence is good, independently of revelation, proving that good and evil are rational. Razi's answer distinguishes two meanings of 'good' and 'evil': one is natural inclination and aversion (raghbat al-tab' wa nafratuhu), which is indeed known by reason and not disputed. The other is the moral quality that makes an act deserving of praise/thawab or blame/dham and punishment/'iqab. The opponent's argument only shows the former, not the latter. Therefore, it does not prove that moral values inhere in acts as objective qualities. Razi refers to his work al-Mahsul for a fuller treatment.

Key Terms
البداهة
العقل
الشرع
الحسن والقبح
رغبة الطبع
نفرته
الذم والعقاب
المدح والثواب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that our natural aversion to injustice and approval of beneficence proves that acts have objective moral qualities that deserve reward or punishment.
Correction: Razi clarifies that such feelings are merely natural inclinations, not indicators of inherent moral worth. Moral obligation is a separate matter.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's answer, what does the intuitive knowledge of good and evil actually refer to?
A) Objective moral qualities inherent in acts.
B) Divine commands and prohibitions.
C) Natural inclination and aversion of the soul.
D) Rational proofs for the existence of God.

Key Takeaway
Intuitive moral feelings are not proof of objective moral values; they are merely natural preferences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Think of a moral judgment you hold strongly—something you feel is obviously wrong. Have you ever considered that this feeling might be just a natural aversion, not an objective truth? How would it change your conviction if you realized that without revelation, you couldn't be sure it is truly blameworthy before God?
?
Razi distinguishes between what feels good and what is morally praiseworthy. In your daily life, where do you rely on your natural inclinations to decide right and wrong? Are you willing to submit those feelings to the authority of revelation, even when your nature pulls the other way?
?
If your intuitive sense of injustice is only a natural preference, then what is the foundation for your moral responsibility? Does this lesson unsettle you? It should—because it means your conscience is not an infallible guide. Will you let the Qur'an and Sunnah shape your conscience, or will you continue trusting your own feelings?

Answer to the Second Objection: The Impact of Deterrents on Judging Lying
Imam Razi answers a second objection about lying and potential deterrents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's argument that if a deterrent can suspend the badness of lying, then we cannot have certainty about any lie's badness—only conjecture—and thus cannot judge God's actions.

Core Idea
A potential deterrent turns certainty about a lie's badness into mere conjecture.

Imam Razi addresses a second objection that challenges the absolute badness of lying. He argues that if a deterrent can prevent the badness of a lie from actualizing, then we can never be certain that any particular lie is bad; at best we have probability. This has profound implications for judging whether lying can ever be attributed to God.

Guided Path
1
Read the illustrative example
Start with the example of being asked about someone's whereabouts: answering truthfully leads to murder, lying saves a life. This sets up the dilemma that a deterrent (saving life) might affect the judgment of the lie.
Notice that the example is not about God but about human actions, used to illustrate the logical point.
2
Understand the conditional argument
Imam Razi argues: If it were possible for the badness (qubh) of a lie to be delayed by a deterrent, then for any lie, one could say 'perhaps a deterrent exists.' This destroys certainty about the lie's badness.
The key move is that the possibility of a deterrent, not its actual existence, is enough to undermine certainty.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is that without certainty, we are left with only conjecture (zann). Consequently, we cannot make a definite judgment about the badness of lying with respect to God, since we cannot rule out a deterrent in His case.
This does not deny that lying is generally bad; it only denies that we can be absolutely certain in every instance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كافيا، كما إذا قال: هل رأيت فلانا في هذه الساعة؟ وهل تعرف مكانه؟ فإن قلت نعم طالبك به وقتله لا محالة وإن قلت: لا. فقد كذبت. وأما السؤال الثاني فجوابه: إنه لو جاز تخلف القبح عن الكذب لمانع، فلا كذب إلا ويجوز أن يقال له لعله وجد مانع من الموانع يمنعه من قبحه. وعلى هذا التقدير لا يمكنك الحكم القطعي بقبح شيء من الأكاذيب أقصى ما في الباب أن يكون الحاصل هو الظن. وعند هذا لا يمكنك الحكم بقبح الكذب في حق الله تعالى.
Sufficiently, like when he says: Did you see so-and-so at this hour? And do you know his place? If you say yes, he will demand him from you and kill him inevitably, and if you say no, you have lied. As for the second question, its answer is: If it were permissible for the badness (qubh) of lying to be delayed due to a deterrent, then there is no lie except that it is permissible to say to him: perhaps a deterrent among deterrents exists preventing its badness. On this basis, you cannot make a definite judgment about the badness of any lie; the utmost in the matter is that the result is conjecture (zann). And in that case, you cannot judge the badness of lying with respect to God Almighty.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage is an answer to a second objection concerning the badness of lying. The author first gives an example: if asked about someone's location, answering truthfully leads to murder, answering falsely is a lie. Then he argues: if it were possible for the badness of lying to be suspended by a deterrent, then no lie can be certainly judged as bad; at best we have conjecture. Consequently, one cannot assert the absolute badness of lying in God's case.

Misconception: The passage denies that lying is inherently bad.
Correction: The passage does not deny inherent badness; it argues only that we cannot be certain of a lie's badness in any specific instance because a deterrent might exist. Certainty is replaced by conjecture, but the lie may still be bad in itself.

Key Takeaway
The possibility of a deterrent undermines certainty about the badness of any lie, limiting our judgment to conjecture and preventing a definitive ruling on God's actions.

Fourth Proof: The Tyrant's Dilemma Against Absolute Badness of Lying
Imam Razi presents a fourth proof using a tyrant's threat dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand a fourth proof from Imam Razi against the claim that lying is absolutely bad, using a tyrant's dilemma.

Core Idea
If lying is absolutely bad, then a tyrant's choice leads to a contradiction.

Imam Razi presents a clever argument that challenges the doctrine of absolute badness of lying. By considering a scenario where a tyrant threatens to kill someone, he shows that the claim leads to an impossible conclusion. Let's examine this proof carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage slowly, then read the English translation to get the gist of the argument.
Pay attention to the logical structure: if...then...either...or...
2
Understand the scenario
The tyrant says he will kill the person tomorrow. This sets up a dilemma: either he kills (which is bad) or he doesn't kill (which makes his statement a lie).
3
Follow the logical chain
Assume lying is absolutely bad. If the tyrant spares the person, he tells a lie. The lie is bad, so sparing leads to a bad consequence. But sparing itself is not bad (it's good to spare). This is a contradiction.
The contradiction shows that the assumption (absolute badness of lying) cannot be true.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Therefore, it is impossible to judge that lying is absolutely bad. The proof does not say lying is permissible; it only refutes the absolute claim.
This leaves room for lying to be bad in some contexts but not absolutely.
5
Reflect on the method
Notice the use of reductio ad absurdum: assume the opponent's position and derive a contradiction. This is a common kalam argument.
The argument is independent of any specific ethical theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة: لو أن ظالما قال لشخص: إني سأقتلك غدا. فههنا الحسن إما أن يقتله. وهو باطل قطعا. أو أن لا يقتله إلا أنه إذا لم يقتله صار الخبر بأنه سيقتله غدا: كذبا. فلو كان الكذب قبيحا، لكان ترك القتل يلزمه القبيح، وما يلزمه القبيح فهو قبيح أن يكون ترك القبيح قبيحا. ولما كان ذلك باطلا علمنا أنه لا يمكن الحكم فكان يجب [؟] يقبح الكذب مطلقا. وبالله التوفيق.
The fourth proof: If a tyrant said to a person: I will kill you tomorrow. Then here the situation is either that he kills him – and that is absolutely false – or that he does not kill him, but if he does not kill him, the report that he will kill him tomorrow becomes a lie. So if lying were bad (qabih), then refraining from killing would entail the bad, and whatever entails the bad is bad, [and] that refraining from the bad is bad. And since that is false, we know that it is not possible to judge that lying must be absolutely bad. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This is a fourth proof against the absolute badness of lying. The scenario: a tyrant promises to kill someone. If he kills, that is bad; if he does not kill, his statement becomes a lie. If lying is absolutely bad, then his omitting the killing (to avoid bad) would entail the bad (the lie), so omitting would be bad, but omitting a bad thing (killing) is not bad – contradiction. Hence lying cannot be judged absolutely bad.

Key Terms
ظالم
كذب
قبيح
ترك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof argues that lying is permissible or not bad at all.
Correction: The proof only shows that the claim of absolute badness leads to contradiction; it does not state that lying is permissible or never bad. Lying may still be bad in specific contexts.

Checkpoint
In the fourth proof, what does the author conclude?
A) Lying is always bad
B) Not killing is bad
C) It is impossible to judge that lying is absolutely bad
D) The tyrant must kill

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi shows that the claim of absolute badness of lying leads to a contradiction in a specific dilemma scenario, thereby refuting the claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider a moral rule like 'lying is always wrong,' what does Razi's tyrant dilemma reveal about the danger of absolute moral judgments? Can you think of a situation in your own life where you assumed a rule was absolute, only to find it leads to an impossible choice? How will you approach such rules now?
?
The tyrant's dilemma forces us to weigh the badness of a lie against the goodness of saving a life. Have you ever faced a decision where any choice would involve some wrong? How does this proof challenge you to reconsider what makes an action truly good or bad in Allah's sight? How will you seek guidance in such complex situations?

Objection and Answer on Rational Knowledge of Good and Bad
Imam Razi presents an opponent's objection and his response regarding rational knowledge of good and bad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Razi responds to the objection that good and bad are known by reason, distinguishing between natural inclination and inherent moral qualities.

Core Idea
Good and bad are known by reason, but moral desert depends on inherent qualities, not just inclination.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi presents an opponent's objection that we intuitively know injustice is bad and benevolence good, independent of revelation. He then offers a subtle response: while reason does know these, the real dispute is about whether actions have inherent qualities that make them deserving of praise or blame. This lesson unpacks that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The opponent claims that we know by intuition that injustice is bad and benevolence good, and this knowledge is not derived from revelation. They argue that even those who deny revelation possess this knowledge, so it must come from reason.
Notice the opponent's use of the example of injustice and benevolence.
2
Identify the opponent's reasoning
The opponent's argument is that since knowledge of good and bad is universal and not dependent on revelation, it must be rational.
3
Read the response
Al-Razi responds that 'good' and 'bad' are expressions of natural inclination (raghbah) and aversion (nifrah). He agrees that such judgments are rational, but the real dispute is whether actions have an inherent quality (sifah qa'imah bi al-fi'l) that makes them deserving of praise or blame.
The response does not deny rational knowledge; it redefines the terms of the debate.
4
Understand the real point of disagreement
The opponent's argument does not prove that actions have inherent qualities that determine moral desert. The dispute is about the basis for reward and punishment, not about the ability of reason to recognize good and bad.
This is a classic Ash'ari position.
5
Reflect on the implications
This distinction clarifies that while reason can discern beneficial and harmful actions, ultimate moral obligation and desert are determined by revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شبهة للخصم: إنا نعلم ببداهة العقل أن الظلم قبيح وأن الإحسان حسن. وهذا العلم غير مستفاد من الشرع. فإن من ينكر الشرع حصل له هذا العلم. فدل ذلك على أن هذا مستفاد من العقل. والجواب: إن هذا الحسن والقبح عبارتان عن رغبة الطبع ونفرته. ولا نزاع في أن هذا معلوم بالعقل وإنما النزاع في أن كون الفعل متعلق الذم والعقاب، أو متعلق المدح والثواب. هل هو لأجل صفة قائمة بالفعل؟ وما ذكرتموه لا يدل على ذلك وتمام الكلام فيه مذكور في كتاب المحصول في علم الأصول" والله أعلم.
An objection of the opponent: We know by the intuition of the intellect that injustice is bad and benevolence is good. This knowledge is not derived from the Law. For one who denies the Law still has this knowledge. So that indicates that this is derived from the intellect. The answer: Indeed, this good and bad are expressions of natural inclination and aversion. There is no dispute that this is known by the intellect; rather the dispute is whether an action being deserving of blame and punishment, or deserving of praise and reward, is due to a quality inherent in the action. What you have mentioned does not indicate that. The full discussion of this is mentioned in the book al-Mahsul fi Usul al-Fiqh. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The opponent argues that we intuitively know injustice is bad and benevolence good, independent of revelation, proving that good and bad are rational. The response distinguishes: these terms refer to natural inclination (raghbah) and aversion (nifrah). There is no dispute that such judgments are rational. The real dispute is whether an action has an inherent quality (sifah qa'imah bi al-fi'l) that makes it deserve blame or punishment, or praise or reward. The opponent's argument does not prove that inherent quality.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The passage denies that reason can know good and bad at all.
Correction: The passage affirms that reason knows good and bad as natural inclinations, but denies that this knowledge implies inherent moral qualities that determine divine reward or punishment.

Checkpoint

A) Whether good and bad are known by reason
B) Whether good and bad are determined by natural inclination
C) Whether an action has an inherent quality that makes it deserving of praise or blame
D) Whether revelation is needed to know good and bad

Key Takeaway
The dispute is not whether reason can know good and bad, but whether actions have inherent qualities that make them deserving of reward or punishment.

The First Proof: God's Actions Cannot Be for a Purpose
Razi states the problem and gives the first proof against purposive divine acts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's first proof that God's actions cannot be motivated by any purpose, and the logical dilemma it presents.

Core Idea
God cannot act for a purpose because that would imply deficiency or indifference.

In this passage, Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents the first proof against the view that God's actions are motivated by consideration of creatures' interests. This proof exposes a logical contradiction: if God acts for a purpose, either the purpose benefits Him (implying imperfection) or it does not (making the action arbitrary). The argument is foundational for understanding divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Learn the Mu'tazilite position that God's acts are for the benefit of creation. Razi declares this false and will provide five proofs.
This is the first of five proofs.
2
Read the first proof
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the structure: a dilemma between the purpose benefiting God or not.
If the purpose benefits God, He is deficient; if not, there is no reason to act.
3
Examine the objection
The objection tries to shift the benefit to the servant. Notice how the objector attempts to avoid the dilemma.
4
Follow the response
Razi shows the same dilemma applies when considering God's relation to the servant's benefit: either equal or not equal, leading to the same impossibility.
5
Recognize the conclusion
The proof decisively rules out any purpose in divine actions, establishing God's absolute transcendence.
This is a key argument in Ash'ari theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة السادسة والعشرون في أنه لا يجوز أن تكون أفعال الله تعالى وأحكامه معللة بعلة البتة اتفقت المعتزلة : على أن أفعال الله تعالى وأحكامه معللة برعاية مصالح العباد وهو اختيار أكثر المتأخرين من الفقهاء. وهذا عندنا باطل . ويدل عليه وجوه خمسة: الحجة الأولى : إن كل من فعل فعلا لأجل تحصيل مصلحة أو لدفع مفسدة، فإن كان تحصيل تلك المصلحة أولى له من عدم تحصيلها، كان ذلك الفاعل قد استفاد بذلك الفعل تحصيل تلك الأولوية. وكل من كان كذلك، كان ناقصا بذاته، مستكملا بغيره وهو في حق الله تعالى محال وإن تحصيلها وعدم تحصيلها بالنسبة إليه سيان فمع الاستواء لا يحصل الرجحان، فامتنع الترجيح. لايقال : حصولها ولها حصولها بالنسبة إليه، وإن كانا على التساوي، إلا أن حصولها أولى للعبد من عدم حصولها ،له، فلأجل الأولوية العائدة إلى العبد ترجح الوجود على العدم لأنا نقول : تحصيل مصلحة العبد وعدم تحصيلها له إما أن يكونا متساويين بالنسبة إلى الله تعالى أو لا يستويان. وحينئذ يعود التقسيم المذكور.
The twenty-sixth question: That it is not permissible that the actions of God Most High and His judgments be motivated by any cause at all. The Mu'tazilites agreed that the actions of God Most High and His judgments are motivated by consideration of the interests of the servants, and this is the choice of most of the later jurists. This is false in our view. And it is indicated by five proofs: The first proof: Every one who does an act for the sake of obtaining a benefit or repelling a harm, if obtaining that benefit is more preferable for him than not obtaining it, then that agent has gained by that act the attainment of that preference. And everyone who is like that is deficient in himself, completing himself by another, and that is impossible with respect to God Most High. And if obtaining it and not obtaining it are equal in relation to Him, then with equality no preponderance occurs, so weighting is impossible. It cannot be said: Its occurrence and not-occurrence are equal in relation to Him, but its occurrence is more preferable for the servant than its non-occurrence, so because of the preference returning to the servant, existence is weighted over non-existence. For we say: The obtaining of the servant's interest and its not obtaining are either equal in relation to God Most High or not equal. Then the aforementioned division recurs.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage defines the problem and presents the first proof against the Mu'tazilite view that God's actions are motivated by consideration of creatures' interests. The author first states the Mu'tazilite position and then declares it false. He then introduces five proofs, of which this is the first. The proof proceeds as follows: If an agent acts to obtain a benefit for himself, then that action gives him something he did not have, implying he was incomplete before and became complete through the action. This is impossible for God, who is perfect in Himself. Alternatively, if obtaining the benefit is equal to not obtaining it for God (since He is self-sufficient), then there is no reason to prefer one over the other, so the action cannot occur. The objection attempts to shift the benefit to the servant, but the author answers that even then, the benefit to the servant is either equal or not equal for God; if equal, no preference; if not equal, then God would be gaining something by the servant's benefit, which again implies deficiency. Thus the argument returns to the same dilemma.

Key Terms
معللة
مصلحة
أولوية
ناقص بذاته
مستكمل بغيره

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this proof shows God is selfish or uncaring.
Correction: The proof is a logical argument, not a moral judgment. It shows that attributing any purpose to God that could imply need or arbitrary choice is impossible, preserving divine transcendence.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, why is it impossible for God to act for a purpose?
A) Because God does not care about creation.
B) Because God would be either deficient or acting without preference.
C) Because purposes only apply to human actions.
D) Because the Mu'tazilites are wrong.

Key Takeaway
God's actions cannot be purposive because purpose implies either need or indifference, both impossible for God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial, do you instinctively demand to know the 'wisdom' or 'purpose' behind it? How does this proof challenge you to instead submit to God's decree without expecting to understand a hidden purpose? What would it take for you to trust that God's actions are not dependent on your benefit?
?
Do you ever assume that God's actions must be for your good, and that if something bad happens, there must be a deeper good? This lesson argues that God's actions cannot be purposive at all. How does that confront your need to see a silver lining? Can you still have gratitude and patience without believing that everything happens 'for a reason' in relation to you?
?
Think about your prayers: when you ask for something, do you feel more confident if you believe that granting it is 'good' for you? According to this proof, God does not act for your benefit. Does that make prayer meaningless? How might this understanding actually deepen your worship, freeing you from a transactional view of God? Journal your honest reaction.

Self-Awareness Proves Soul Is Not the Body
Razi argues that one can be aware of oneself while oblivious to all body parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's second rational proof that the soul is not identical to the body, based on the experience of self-awareness in the absence of awareness of bodily organs.

Core Idea
Self-awareness persists even when we are unaware of our body—proving the self is not the body.

Razi continues his critique of the view that the soul is a body by presenting a simple yet profound argument from self-knowledge. The argument appeals to a universal human experience: we can be directly aware of our own self without being aware of any bodily parts. Razi uses this to prove that the self is distinct from the body.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Notice how Razi structures his argument concisely.
The passage is short but dense; each word matters.
2
Identify the key terms
Focus on three key terms: ضرورة (necessary knowledge), ذات مخصوصة (specific self), and غفلة (heedlessness). Understand their role in the argument.
The term 'ضرورة' indicates that this knowledge is immediate and indubitable.
3
Unpack the logical structure
Break the argument into premises and conclusion. The first premise: I know my self necessarily when heedless of organs. The second premise: what is known is different from what is not known. Conclusion: Therefore, my self is other than all organs.
This is a deductive argument; if premises are true, conclusion must follow.
4
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself how this argument strengthens the case against the soul being material. Consider whether you have ever experienced self-awareness while oblivious to your body.
Razi's argument relies on a common human experience available to everyone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني: أني أعلم بالضرورة ذاتي المخصوصة، حال ما أكون غافلا عن جميع ما لي من الأعضاء الظاهرة والباطنة والمعلوم مغاير لما ليس بمعلوم، فوجب القطع بأن ما هو المشار إليه بقولي أنا مغاير لجميع الأعضاء الظاهرة والباطنة.
The second proof: that I necessarily know my specific self (dhātī al-makhṣūṣa) at a time when I am heedless of all the external and internal organs I have, and the known is other than what is not known, so it must be decisively concluded that that which is referred to by my saying 'I' is other than all external and internal organs.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This is the second proof against the soul being the body. Razi appeals to immediate self-knowledge: a person can be aware of their own self (dhāt) even while completely oblivious to all their bodily parts, both external (like hands) and internal (like the heart). Since the self is known directly while the organs are not known at that moment, the known self must be distinct from the unknown organs. Therefore the 'I' is not the body or any part of it.

Key Terms
ضرورة (ḍarūra)
ذات مخصوصة (dhāt makhṣūṣa)
غفلة (ghafla)
أعضاء ظاهرة وباطنة (aʿḍāʾ ẓāhira wa bāṭina)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Self-awareness always includes awareness of one's body parts.
Correction: Razi shows that one can be aware of one's self even while completely heedless of all external and internal organs, proving the self is distinct from the body.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's second proof, how do we know the self is distinct from bodily organs?
A) Because the body changes over time
B) Because we know our self even when unaware of our organs
C) Because the soul is immortal
D) Because God revealed it

Key Takeaway
Razi's second proof shows that self-awareness independently of bodily awareness demonstrates the soul's immateriality and distinction from the body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you were completely unaware of your body—maybe lost in thought or deep in prayer? In that moment, were you still aware of your 'self'? What does that tell you about where your true identity lies?
?
If your soul is not your body, then what part of you will stand before Allah after death? Are you nurturing that part now, or are you consumed with that which will perish?
?
You say 'I' every day without thinking. Has this lesson changed what you mean when you say 'I'? How should that shift how you live starting today?
