Philosophers' Proof for the Immateriality of the Knower
Razi presents the philosophers' four-premise argument for the immaterial soul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the philosophers' proof that the knowing subject (the soul) is immaterial, based on the indivisibility of knowledge and its objects.

Core Idea
If knowledge is indivisible, its subject must be indivisible and therefore immaterial.

This passage from Imam Razi's Al-Arba'een presents the central proof used by philosophers (al-falāsifa) to argue that the soul is not a body or a bodily accident. The argument proceeds through four carefully constructed premises, each supported by rational demonstration. By tracing the steps, you will see how the indivisibility of what we know leads to the conclusion that the knower itself cannot be extended or divisible.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Razi notes that this view is held by many philosophers and some theologians. He presents the proof even though he has objected elsewhere.
Razi is a critical reporter; he does not necessarily endorse the proof.
2
Follow the overall argument
The proof is summarized concisely at the beginning: indivisible objects → indivisible knowledge → indivisible subject → not extended.
3
Examine Premise 1
Why must there be indivisible objects of knowledge? Two proofs: direct knowledge of God and unity, and knowledge of simples underlying composites.
4
Examine Premise 2
If the object is indivisible, knowledge of it must be indivisible. Razi offers a four-case analysis to show that any division of such knowledge leads to contradiction.
This is the most detailed step; pay attention to the case where a new property emerges.
5
Examine Premises 3 and 4 and Conclusion
Premise 3: Indivisible knowledge implies an indivisible subject (again a four-case analysis of modes in a locus). Premise 4: Everything extended is divisible (based on atomism). Conclusion: The knower is neither extended nor a mode in an extended thing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذا القول اختيار جمهور الفلاسفة. ومن قدماء المعتزلة مذهب معمر بن عباد السلمي ومذهب أكثر الأجناس من الإمامية ومن المتأخرين الغزالي وأبي القاسم الراغب. واعلم أن الفلاسفة ذكروا في إثبات هذا وجوها كثيرة واعترضنا عليها في كتبنا الحكمية إلا أن اعتمادهم على وجه واحد ونحن نذكره ههنا: قالوا: لا شك في وجود معلومات غير منقسمة فيكون العلم بها غير منقسم، فيكون الموصوف بتلك العلوم غير منقسم، وكل متحيز فهو منقسم، فإذن الموصوف بتلك العلوم لا متحيز ولا حال في المتحيز. أما المقدمة الأولى: وهي إثبات معلومات غير قابلة للقسمة فيدل عليه وجهان: الأول: أن نعرف ذات الله تعالى وثبت أن ذاته تعالى غير قابلة للقسمة بوجه من الوجوه، وأيضا: نعرف الوحدة، والوحدة غير قابلة للقسمة بوجه من الوجوه. الثاني: لا شك أن نعرف شيئا، وذلك الشيء إما مفرد وإما مركب، فإن كان مفردا فقد حصل المطلوب، وإن كان مركبا فكل مركب فهو مركب من المفردات والعلم بالمركب مسبوق بالعلم بمفرداته، فثبت أنا نعلم أمورا مفردة. وأما المقدمة الثانية: وهي أن المعلوم إذا كان غير منقسم كان العلم به غير قابل للانقسام فالذي يدل عليه: أنه لو كان ذلك العلم قابلا للانقسام لكان ما يفرض جزءا لذلك العلم إما أن يكون علما بذلك المعلوم، أو يكون علما بجزء من أجزاء ذلك المعلوم، أو لا يكون علما بذلك المعلوم ولا بشيء من أجزاء ذلك المعلوم. فإن كان جزء العلم علما بذلك المعلوم لزم أن يكون الجزء مساويا للكل في تمام الماهية وذلك محال. وإن كان متعلقا بجزء من أجزاء ذلك المعلوم لزم كون ذلك المعلوم منقسما وهو محال. وإن لم يكن علما لا بذلك المعلوم ولا بشيء من أجزائه، فعند اجتماع تلك الأجزاء إما أن يحدث أمر زائد بسبب ذلك الاجتماع، أو لا يحدث، فإن لم يحدث البتة أمر زائد لزم أن لا يكون ذلك المجموع علما بذلك المعلوم. والعلم بالشيء لا يكون علما بذلك الشيء هذا خلف. وإن حدثت حالة زائدة بسبب ذلك الاجتماع، فتلك الحالة الزائدة إن كانت قابلة للقسمة، عاد التقسيم المذكور فيه ولزم التسلسل. وإن لم تقبل القسمة فالعلم بهذا المعلوم هو هذه الحالة الزائدة الحادثة بسبب الاجتماع، وأنه غير قابل للقسمة. فحينئذ يحصل بما ذكرنا أن المعلوم إذا لم يقبل القسمة، كان العلم به غير قابل للقسمة. وأما المقدمة الثالثة: وهي أن العلم إذا لم يكن قابلا للقسمة، وجب أن يكون الموصوف به غير قابل للقسمة فالذي يدل عليه أن كل ما كان قابلا للقسمة افترض فيه الجزءان فالفرض الحاصل فيه إما أن يكون بتمامه حاصلا في كل واحد من النصفين، أو يكون بتمامه حاصلا في أحد النصفين دون الثاني، أو يكون نصفه حاصلا في أحد نصفيه والنصف الآخر منه حاصلا في النصف الثاني من المحل، أو لا يكون شيء من ذلك. أما الأول: وهو أن يحصل بتمامه في هذا النصف وبتمامه في النصف الثاني فهذا يقتضي حصول العرض الواحد في محلين دفعة واحدة هو محال. وأما الثاني: وهو أن يحصل بتمامه في أحد النصفين منه دون الثاني فحينئذ تنقل الكلام إلى ذلك النصف، فإن ذلك النصف إن كان منقسما، عاد الكلام فيه، فيلزم أن يكون حاصلا في نصف ذلك النصف وبالجملة فكل ما يكون منقسما فإن ذلك العرض لا يكون حالا فيه وهذا يلزمه من باب عكس النقيض: أن ما يكون ذلك العرض حالا فيه فإنه لا يكون منقسما. وأما الثالث: وهو أن يقال بتوزع الحال على المحل فهذا يقتضي انقسام الحال وقد بينا أنه محال. وأما الرابع: وهو أن لا يكون شيء من هذه الأقسام فهذا محال لأنه إذا كان كل واحد من أجزاء المحل خاليا عن الحال بالكلية وعن جملة أجزاء الحال، كان كل واحد من أجزاء المحل خاليا عن الحال بالكلية ومتى كان الأمر كذلك، امتنع كون المحل موصوفا بالحال والعلم به ضروي فثبت أن الحال إذا كان غير منقسم، كان المحل أيضا غير منقسم. المقدمة الرابعة: وهي قولنا: إن كل متحيز منقسم فهذا بناء على مسألة نفي الجوهر الفرد وقد تقدم القول فيه. وحينئذ يلزم القطع من مجموع هذه المقدمات الأربع أن الشيء الذي هو الموصوف منا بالمعارف والعلوم موجود ليس بمتحيز ولا حال في المتحيز وهو المطلوب.
This is the view chosen by the majority of philosophers. And among the early Mu'tazila, the doctrine of Mu'ammar ibn 'Abbad al-Sulami and the doctrine of most sects of the Imamiyya, and among the later ones, al-Ghazali and Abu al-Qasim al-Raghib. Know that the philosophers mentioned many proofs for establishing this, and we have objected to them in our philosophical books, but their reliance is on one proof, and we will mention it here. They said: There is no doubt about the existence of indivisible objects of knowledge, so knowledge of them is indivisible, so the subject characterized by those knowledges is indivisible, and every extended thing is divisible, so the subject characterized by those knowledges is neither extended nor a mode in an extended thing. As for the first premise, which is establishing objects of knowledge not divisible, two proofs indicate it: The first is that we know the essence of God most high, and it is established that His essence is indivisible in any way, and also we know unity, and unity is indivisible in any way. The second is: There is no doubt that we know something, and that thing is either simple or composite; if it is simple, the required is obtained; and if it is composite, every composite is composed of simples, and knowledge of the composite is preceded by knowledge of its simples, so it is established that we know simple things. As for the second premise, which is that when the object is indivisible, knowledge of it is indivisible, what indicates it is that if that knowledge were divisible, then what is assumed as a part of that knowledge would either be knowledge of that object, or be knowledge of a part of the parts of that object, or be knowledge neither of that object nor of any part of that object. If the part of knowledge is knowledge of that object, it would necessitate that the part equals the whole in complete essence, and that is impossible. And if it is related to a part of the parts of that object, it would necessitate that object being divisible, which is impossible. And if it is not knowledge of that object nor of any part of it, then when those parts are combined, either an additional thing occurs due to that combination, or it does not occur. If no additional thing occurs at all, it would necessitate that the aggregate is not knowledge of that object; but knowledge of a thing is not knowledge of that thing—this is a contradiction. And if an additional state occurs due to that combination, then that additional state, if it is divisible, the mentioned division would recur in it and infinite regress would follow. And if it is not divisible, then knowledge of this object is this additional state arising due to the combination, and it is indivisible. Then from what we have mentioned it is obtained that when the object does not accept division, knowledge of it is not divisible. As for the third premise, which is that when knowledge is not divisible, the subject characterized by it must be indivisible, what indicates it is that everything that is divisible, two parts are assumed in it, and the state present in it is either wholly present in each of the halves, or wholly present in one of the halves without the second, or half of it is present in one of its halves and the other half of it is present in the second half of the locus, or none of that is the case. As for the first, which is that it occurs wholly in this half and wholly in the second half, this necessitates a single accident being in two loci at once, which is impossible. As for the second, which is that it occurs wholly in one of the halves without the second, then the discourse is transferred to that half; for if that half is divisible, the discourse recurs in it, so it would necessitate that it occurs in half of that half; and in summary, everything that is divisible, that accident is not a mode in it; and this entails by contrapositive that what has that accident as a mode is not divisible. As for the third, which is said to be distributing the mode in the locus, this necessitates division of the mode, and we have shown that it is impossible. As for the fourth, which is that it is none of these cases, this is impossible, because if every part of the locus is devoid of the mode entirely and of the totality of the parts of the mode, then every part of the locus is devoid of the mode entirely, and when this is the case, it is impossible for the locus to be characterized by the mode, and knowledge of it is necessary; so it is established that when the mode is indivisible, the locus is also indivisible. The fourth premise, which is our statement that every extended thing is divisible, is based on the issue of denying the indivisible atom, which has preceded in discussion. Then from the totality of these four premises the certain conclusion follows that the thing which is the subject in us of intellects and knowledges exists, is not extended, nor a mode in an extended thing; and that is the required.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' main proof (al-falāsifa) for the immateriality of the knowing subject (the soul). The proof is structured as four premises leading to a conclusion. Razi first notes that this view – that the soul is immaterial – is held by most philosophers, some Mu'tazila (like Mu'ammar ibn 'Abbad), most Imamiyya, and later scholars like al-Ghazali and al-Raghib. He states that he has objected to their many proofs in his philosophical works, but here he will present the one they rely on most. 

The argument begins with a concise summary: there exist indivisible objects of knowledge; therefore knowledge of them is indivisible; therefore the subject possessing that knowledge is indivisible; and since everything extended (mutahayyiz) is divisible, the subject is neither extended nor a mode (ḥāl) in an extended thing.

Premise one: There are indivisible objects of knowledge. Two proofs are given. First, we know God's essence (dhāt Allāh) and unity (al-waḥda), both of which are indivisible. Second, all our knowledge of composites presupposes knowledge of simple, indivisible constituents; otherwise there would be an infinite regress. So we know simple, indivisible things.

Premise two: If the object is indivisible, the knowledge of it is also indivisible. A proof by cases: suppose the knowledge could be divided. Then a part of that knowledge would either (1) still be knowledge of the whole indivisible object – making the part equal to the whole, impossible; or (2) be knowledge of a part of the object – implying the object is divisible, contradiction; or (3) be knowledge of neither the whole nor any part. In this third case, when parts are combined, either a new property emerges or not. If not, the aggregate is not knowledge at all – which contradicts the fact that it was knowledge. If a new property emerges, it must itself be indivisible (otherwise we repeat the argument ad infinitum). So the knowledge is indivisible.

Premise three: If knowledge is indivisible, then its subject (the knower) is indivisible. Again a proof by cases: assume the subject is divisible. Then the knowledge (as a mode or accident) must inhere in it. There are four possibilities: (a) wholly in each half – one accident in two loci, impossible; (b) wholly in one half only – then that half is the true subject, and if it is divisible the same issue recurs, forcing indivisibility of the subject; (c) split across halves – this would divide the knowledge itself, which is already proven indivisible; (d) not present in either half – then no part contains it, so the whole cannot contain it, impossible. Hence the subject must be indivisible.

Premise four: Everything extended is divisible. This is the atomist thesis that space is composed of indivisible parts (al-jawhar al-fard), which Razi says has been treated earlier. Accepting that conclusion, the subject of knowledge – the soul – is not extended, and thus not a body or a bodily accident. That is the desired proof.

Throughout, Razi is reporting the philosophers' argument, not necessarily endorsing it. The passage is a careful logical demonstration that the immateriality of the soul follows from the fact that we have indivisible concepts.

Key Terms
غير منقسم (ghayr munqasim)
متحيز (mutahayyiz)
حال (hāl)
عرض ('araḍ)
جوهر فرد (jawhar fard)
معلوم (ma'lūm)
علم ('ilm)
مقدمة (muqaddima)
محل (maḥall)
موصوف (mawṣūf)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument attempts to prove that the soul is a separate substance different from the body.
Correction: Actually, it only proves that the subject of knowledge (the knower) is indivisible and not extended, which is consistent with the soul being an immaterial substance, but the argument does not speculate on its separateness from the body; it only establishes its indivisibility.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers' proof presented by Razi, why must the subject of knowledge be indivisible?
A) Because knowledge is an accident that cannot exist in a divisible locus (maḥall).
B) Because the objects of knowledge are indivisible, requiring an indivisible knower.
C) Because the soul is a simple substance proven by revelation.
D) Because every extended thing is divisible, and the subject is not extended.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' proof for the immateriality of the soul rests on the logical chain that indivisible objects of knowledge require an indivisible knower, which cannot be extended.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you contemplate a simple concept like 'unity' or 'God'—something indivisible—do you experience your own mind as unified in that moment, or is it fragmented by distractions? What could you do today to bring your intellect to a single, indivisible focus?
?
The proof shows that the knower cannot be divisible or extended. Does your daily life reflect the belief that your soul is something beyond the material? When you pursue material pleasures, do you treat your soul as if it were just another extended thing? What one choice this week would honor the immaterial nature of your true self?
?
If knowledge of God is indivisible, then your relationship with Him cannot be compartmentalized into certain times or actions. In what area of your life have you tried to keep your faith separate, as if it were a part and not the whole? What would it mean to let that single knowledge unify your entire way of living starting today?

Razi's Second Proof for Void with Four Premises
Razi enumerates four premises to prove void exists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi argues for the existence of void using a hypothetical scenario with four premises that lead to a necessary conclusion.

Core Idea
Void can be proven by imagining two perfect surfaces being pulled apart.

This passage presents Razi's second proof for the existence of void (al-khalaa), a key concept in kalam cosmology. The argument is carefully structured as a series of premises that lead to a necessary conclusion. By following the logical steps, you will see how medieval Muslim theologians reasoned about physical space and the possibility of emptiness.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof statement
Begin by reading the initial statement of the proof and its hypothetical scenario. Notice the claim that void necessarily occurs when two planar surfaces are separated instantly.
This sets up the entire argument.
2
Examine the first premise
The first premise argues that perfectly planar surfaces can exist. Understand the reasoning about angles and small planar surfaces.
This premise is crucial for the possibility of the scenario.
3
Examine the second and third premises
The second premise asserts that two planar surfaces can be in complete contact. The third premise argues that one surface can be lifted entirely without breaking.
These premises establish the possibility of the action.
4
Analyze the fourth premise and conclusion
The fourth premise examines what fills the gap when the surfaces are separated. It considers two possibilities and shows that in both cases void must occur. Conclude how the proof is complete.
This leads to the necessary acceptance of void.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية في إثبات الخلاء : أنا إذا فرضنا سطحين مستويين انطبق أحدهما بتمامه على الآخر، ثم إذا فرضنا ارتفاع أحدهما عن الآخر دفعة، فعند هذا يلزم القطع بحصول الخلاء فيما بين ذينك الجسمين . وههنا مقدمات :
المقدمة الأولى : أنه يمكن فرض سطحين مستويين من كل الوجوه ويدل على إمكانه : أن عدم الاستواء في السطح. إما أن يكون بسبب اختلاف أجزائه في الارتفاع والانخفاض، أو بسبب حصول المسام فيه أما الأول فلا بد وأن يكون بسبب سطوح صغار يتصل بعضها ببعض، لا على الاستقامة، بل على الزاوية ولا بد الانتهاء إلى سطوح صغار مستوية، وإلا لذهبت الزوايا إلى غير النهاية وهو محال، وأما حصول المسام في أجزاء السطح فهذا لو حصل فلا بد أن يحصل بين كل منقذين سطح متصل، وإلا لزم كون السطح مركبا من نقط متباينة وذلك محال فوجب القطع بوجود سطوح مستوية.
وأما المقدمة الثانية : وهي يجوز وجود سطحين مستويين يتماسان بالكلية فهذا ظاهر. لأن تماس بعض جوانب السطوح ممكن فيكون تماسها أيضا بالكلية ممكنا ؛ لأن جميع الأجزاء المفروضة في السطح الواحد متساوية، وما صح على البعض جاز على الباقي.
وأما المقدمة الثالثة وهي أن ارتفاع أحد السطحين بكليته عن السطح الآخر ممكن فبرهانه أن أحد السطحين لما كان بكليته مماسا للسطح الآخر فإذا ارتفع أحد أجزائه عما تحته فالجزء المتصل يجب أيضا أن يرتفع عما تحته. وإلا لزم وقوع التفكك في السطح الأعلى وهو محال.
وأما المقدمة الرابعة - وهي أنه إذا حصلت هذه الأمور، لزم وقوع الخلاء : فيما بين هذين السطحين - وذلك لأنه لو حصل جسم فيما بينهما لكان ذلك الجسم إما أن ينتقل من الخارج إليه أو يحصل من مسام السطح الأعلى والأسفل. فإن انتقل من الخارج إلى ذلك الوسط فحينما يكون ذلك الجسم المنتقل حاصلا في الطرفين لا يكون حاصلا في الوسط ففي ذلك الوقت يكون الوسط خاليا وهو المطلوب. وأما القول بأنه يوجد من مسام الأعلى والأسفل. فهذا أيضا باطل لأنا فرضنا الكلام في سطحين لا شيء من المسام فيهما وأيضا فبتقدير حصول المسام إلا أنه لا بد وأن يحصل بين كل منفذين من تلك الأجسام سطح متصل فالشيء الذي ينزل من المسام يكون حال كونه متصلا بطرف تلك الفرجة، لا يكون حاصلا في وسط السطح المتوسط فيما بين الفرجتين، فيكون ذلك الوسط خاليا لا محالة فثبت أن القول بالخلاء لازم على كل التقديرات.
The second argument for the proof of void: That if we suppose two planar surfaces with one completely coinciding with the other, then if we suppose the lifting of one from the other instantaneously, then at that point it is necessarily concluded that void occurs between those two bodies. And here are premises:
The first premise: That it is possible to suppose two planar surfaces in every respect. Evidence of its possibility: That the lack of planarity in a surface is either because of difference in its parts in height and depth, or because of the occurrence of pores in it. As for the first, it cannot be but because of small surfaces that connect to each other, not on a straight line but at an angle, and it must end at small planar surfaces, otherwise the angles would go to infinity, which is impossible. As for the occurrence of pores in the parts of the surface, if this occurs, then there must occur between every two openings a connected surface, otherwise the surface would be composed of distinct points, which is impossible. So it is necessarily concluded that planar surfaces exist.
The second premise: That it is permissible for two planar surfaces to be in complete contact. This is evident because contact of some sides of surfaces is possible, so their complete contact is also possible, because all the supposed parts in one surface are equal, and what is valid for some is permissible for the rest.
The third premise: That the lifting of one of the two surfaces entirely from the other surface is possible. Its proof is that when one of the two surfaces is entirely in contact with the other surface, if one of its parts is raised from what is beneath it, then the connected part must also be raised from what is beneath it. Otherwise, disconnection would occur in the upper surface, which is impossible.
The fourth premise: That if these matters occur, then the occurrence of void between these two surfaces is necessary. That is because if a body were to occur between them, that body would either transfer from the outside to it or occur from the pores of the upper and lower surfaces. If it transfers from the outside to that middle, then at the moment when that transferring body is at the two ends, it is not in the middle, so at that time the middle would be empty, which is the desired conclusion. As for the claim that it arises from the pores of the upper and lower, this is also invalid because we have supposed the discussion regarding two surfaces that have no pores in them. Also, assuming the occurrence of pores, there must occur between every two openings of those bodies a connected surface, so the thing that descends from the pores, while it is connected to the edge of that gap, is not present in the middle of the intermediate surface between the two gaps, so that middle would be necessarily empty. Thus it is established that the claim of void is necessary in all estimations.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage is a proof (hujja) for the existence of void (al-khalaa). It is structured as a hypothetical reasoning with four premises, leading to a necessary conclusion. The argument begins by positing two perfectly planar surfaces in complete contact. If one lifts instantly, a gap must form. The first premise establishes that perfectly planar surfaces can exist, by arguing that any lack of planarity resolves to small planar surfaces or pores that require connected surfaces. The second premise asserts that such surfaces can be in full contact because partial contact is possible. The third premise argues that lifting one surface entirely is possible, as lifting one part forces the rest to lift to avoid disconnection. The fourth premise examines what fills the gap: either an external body moves in (leaving the middle empty at the instant of transfer) or a body emerges from pores (but the surfaces are assumed poreless, and even with pores, gaps remain). Thus void is necessary. The proof concludes powerfully that the claim of void is unavoidable on every estimation.

Key Terms
خلاء (khalaa)
سطح (sath)
مسام (masaam)
مقدمة (muqaddama)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof assumes that void actually exists from the start.
Correction: The proof does not assume actual void; it argues that if certain conditions (planar surfaces, instant lift) are met, void would necessarily be posited as a logical consequence.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof for void, what must be true about the surfaces at the start?
A) They must be porous.
B) They must be curved.
C) They must be planar and in complete contact.
D) They must be separated by a medium.

Key Takeaway
Razi's second proof for void is a rigorous logical argument that demonstrates the necessity of void through a thought experiment involving two planar surfaces.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine two perfect surfaces separating instantly, does the inevitable void that Razi describes challenge you to trust that your own logical reasoning can lead to certain truths about the unseen? Are you willing to follow such arguments to their conclusions, even when they lead to concepts like void that are beyond your direct experience?
?
Razi painstakingly builds each premise on logical necessity. In your own life, do you base your beliefs on such careful reasoning, or do you often accept things without rigorous proof? What would it take for you to apply this level of scrutiny to your own convictions?
?
The proof forces you to admit that void must exist under the given conditions. Does this intellectual surrender to a truth that is not directly observable strengthen your reliance on reason as a gift from Allah? How can you use this rational faculty to draw closer to certainty about Allah's oneness?

First Argument of the Deniers of Void
Razi presents the first argument from deniers of void: void would be a measurable entity, impossible for non-being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument used by deniers of void, which shows that if void existed it would be a measurable magnitude, contradicting its alleged non-existence.

Core Idea
Void cannot be non-existence because non-existence cannot be measured or compared.

This lesson unpacks a classic reductio ad absurdum argument from those who deny the existence of void. The argument begins by observing that different voids appear comparable in size, which would be impossible if void were pure non-being. Through careful reasoning, the deniers show that the very concept of void leads to a contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the structure of the argument become clear.
Notice how Razi builds from examples to a logical conclusion.
2
Identify the premise
The argument starts with the claim that void is susceptible to estimation, equality, and inequality. Look for the evidence given with examples.
3
Trace the reductio
Follow how the measurability of void leads to the conclusion that void would be a magnitude, not non-existence. Understand why non-existence cannot have quantitative relations.
This is the key move in the argument.
4
See the self-negation
The argument ends with a striking result: assuming void leads to its own negation. Ask yourself how a plenum (malaa') follows from the assumption of void.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما نفاة الخلاء فقد احتجوا بوجوه : الحجة الأولى : أن كل خلاء فإنه قابل للتقدير والمساواة والمفاوتة. بدليل أن الخلاء الحاصل بين طرفي الطاس أقل من الخلاء الحاصل بين جداري البلد، والخلاء الحاصل بين جداري البلد أقل من الخلاء الحاصل بين السماء والأرض فثبت أن كل خلاء فهو قابل للمساواة والمفاوتة وكل ما كان كذلك، امتنع أن يكون عدما محضًا لأنه لا يمكن أن يقال : هذا العدم نصف ذلك العدم أو ثلثه، وأن ذلك العدم أضعاف هذا العدم. ولا يمكن أن يقال : العدم عشرة أذرع أو ألف ذراع فالخلاء يمكن وصفه بهذه الأوصاف فثبت أن الخلاء لو حصل لكان موجودا قابلا للتقدير، فلا يكون الخلاء ،عدما بل مقدارا والمقدار إما أن يكون عبارة عن نفس الجسم، أو عن صفة قائمة بالجسم فثبت أنه لو فرض الخلاء حاصلا لما كان خلاء، بل كان ملاء فإذا فرض الخلاء يفضي ثبوته إلى نفيه فكان القول به باطلا.
As for the deniers of void, they have argued with several points: The first argument: That every void is susceptible to estimation, equality, and inequality. The evidence is that the void that occurs between the two sides of a basin is less than the void that occurs between the two walls of a city, and the void that occurs between the two walls of a city is less than the void that occurs between the sky and the earth. Thus it is established that every void is susceptible to equality and inequality. And everything that is so, it is impossible for it to be pure non-existence, because it cannot be said: this non-existence is half of that non-existence or a third of it, and that this non-existence is multiples of this non-existence. Nor can it be said: non-existence is ten cubits or a thousand cubits. So void can be described by these descriptions. Thus it is established that if void were to occur, it would be an existent thing susceptible to estimation, and thus void would not be non-existence but rather a magnitude. And magnitude is either an expression for the body itself or for an attribute subsisting in the body. Thus it is established that if void were assumed to occur, it would not be void, but rather a plenum. So if void is assumed, its existence leads to its negation, hence the claim of it is false.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage is the first argument (hujja) from those who deny void (nufaat al-khalaa). It is a reductio ad absurdum. The argument begins by observing that different voids are comparable: the void between a basin's sides is smaller than that between city walls, which is smaller than between sky and earth. Hence void admits equality and inequality (masawat and mufawata). Anything with these properties cannot be pure non-existence ('adam mahd) because non-existence cannot have fractional relations or measurements—you cannot say one non-existence is half another, or that it measures ten cubits. Therefore, if void existed, it would be a real, measurable thing (mawjud qabil li al-taqdir). But then it is not non-existence but a magnitude (miqdar). Magnitude is either the body itself or an attribute in a body. So void would be a plenum (malaa'), not void. Thus the assumption of void leads to its own negation, proving the claim false.

Key Terms
خلاء (khalaa)
عدم ('adam)
تقدير (taqdeer)
مقدار (miqdar)
ملاء (malaa')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument relies on actual physical comparisons rather than conceptual necessity.
Correction: The argument is conceptual: it shows that if void were real, it would have to have measurable properties, which non-existence cannot have, regardless of actual physical examples.

Checkpoint
Why do deniers of void argue that void cannot be non-existence?
A) Because non-existence is not a place.
B) Because non-existence cannot have comparative measurements like 'half' or 'more'.
C) Because void is imaginary.
D) Because the Quran denies void.

Key Takeaway
Deniers of void argue that void cannot be non-existence because void is measurable, and non-existence is not, leading to the conclusion that void would be a plenum, thus self-contradictory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument shows that something cannot be 'nothing' if it has measurable attributes. In your own life, do you ever treat abstract concepts (like 'emptiness' in your schedule or 'void' in your heart) as if they have tangible qualities? How does this lesson challenge you to be more precise about what exists and what does not?
?
The deniers of void argue from the impossibility of non-existence having magnitude. Are there areas where you have assumed something was 'nothing' (negligible, unimportant) but later discovered it had real effects? How can this argument train you to check your assumptions about what truly is non-existent?
?
If void were possible, it would actually be a plenum. This is a logical inversion. When have you found that something you thought was empty or meaningless turned out to be full of meaning or reality? How does that realization change how you approach concepts you currently dismiss?

The Self-Refuting Nature of Vacuum
First proof against vacuum from measurability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first theological argument against the existence of vacuum, which shows that the very concept of vacuum leads to a logical contradiction.

Core Idea
If vacuum were real, it would be measurable—but nothingness cannot be measured.

This argument from the deniers of vacuum uses a simple but powerful logical move: it shows that the very idea of vacuum, if taken seriously, entails measurability and comparability, which are properties of existing things, not of pure non-being. Thus, the concept of vacuum refutes itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Get a feel for the argument's flow.
The Arabic uses technical terms like 'خلاء' (vacuum) and 'تقدير' (measurement).
2
Identify the key claim
Find where the argument states that every vacuum is receptive of measurement, equality, and difference. Note the examples given.
3
Understand why measurability contradicts non-being
The argument reasons that non-being cannot be described as half or double, or measured in cubits. Since vacuum can be compared, it cannot be pure non-being.
This is the core logical step.
4
See the conclusion
The argument concludes that if vacuum existed, it would be a quantity (body or attribute), thus not a void. Hence the concept self-destructs.
Notice the reductio ad absurdum structure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما نفاة الخلاء فقد احتجوا بوجوه : الحجة الأولى : أن كل خلاء فإنه قابل للتقدير والمساواة والمفاوتة. بدليل أن الخلاء الحاصل بين طرفي الطاس أقل من الخلاء الحاصل بين جداري البلد، والخلاء الحاصل بين جداري البلد أقل من الخلاء الحاصل بين السماء والأرض فثبت أن كل خلاء فهو قابل للمساواة والمفاوتة وكل ما كان كذلك، امتنع أن يكون عدما محضًا لأنه لا يمكن أن يقال : هذا العدم نصف ذلك العدم أو ثلثه، وأن ذلك العدم أضعاف هذا العدم. ولا يمكن أن يقال : العدم عشرة أذرع أو ألف ذراع فالخلاء يمكن وصفه بهذه الأوصاف فثبت أن الخلاء لو حصل لكان موجودا قابلا للتقدير، فلا يكون الخلاء عدما بل مقدارا والمقدار إما أن يكون عبارة عن نفس الجسم، أو عن صفة قائمة بالجسم فثبت أنه لو فرض الخلاء حاصلا لما كان خلاء، بل كان ملاء فإذا فرض الخلاء يفضي ثبوته إلى نفيه فكان القول به باطلا.
As for the deniers of vacuum, they have argued with proofs: The first proof: That every vacuum is receptive of measurement, equality, and difference. Evidence is that the vacuum between the sides of a cup is less than the vacuum between the walls of a city, and the vacuum between the walls of a city is less than the vacuum between heaven and earth. So it is established that every vacuum is receptive of equality and difference, and everything that is thus is impossible to be pure non-being, because it cannot be said: This non-being is half of that non-being or a third of it, and that non-being is multiples of this non-being. Nor can it be said: Non-being is ten cubits or a thousand cubits. So vacuum can be described by these attributes, thus it is established that if vacuum occurred it would be an existent receptive of measurement, so vacuum would not be non-being but rather a quantity. And quantity is either an expression for the body itself or for an attribute subsisting in the body. So it is established that if vacuum were assumed to exist it would not be vacuum but rather a plenum. So if vacuum is assumed, its affirmation leads to its negation, thus the statement of it is invalid.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This is the first argument of those who deny vacuum. It argues that vacuum, if it existed, would be measurable and comparable (e.g., the vacuum in a cup is less than in a city). But non-being cannot be described as half or double of another non-being, nor can it be measured in cubits. Therefore, vacuum cannot be pure non-being; it must be a quantity (mikdar). A quantity is either a body or an attribute of a body. Since vacuum is supposed to be non-being, this leads to a contradiction: if it exists, it is a plenum (malaa), not a void. Hence the assumption of vacuum self-destructs.

Key Terms
خلاء
تقدير
مساواة
مفاوتة
مقدار
ملاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that measurability applies to space itself, but the argument uses this to show vacuum would be a body or accident, not nothing.
Correction: The argument shows that if vacuum is measurable, it must be an existing quantity, not a void. Thus the concept of vacuum is self-contradictory.

Checkpoint
According to the first argument, why can vacuum not be pure non-being?
A) Because non-being has no attributes.
B) Because vacuum can be described as half or double, which is impossible for non-being.
C) Because vacuum is a body.
D) Because vacuum is an accident.

Key Takeaway
The first argument against vacuum shows that the concept of vacuum is self-refuting because it implies properties that only belong to existing things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What assumptions do you hold about 'nothingness' that might actually treat it as a 'something'? Are you willing to expose those assumptions to logical scrutiny, even if it feels unsettling?
?
This argument shows that a self-contradictory belief destroys itself. Is there a belief or habit in your life that, when examined honestly, leads to its own negation? What will you do about it starting today?
?
If pure non-being cannot be measured, what does that tell you about the nature of existence and the necessity of a Creator who gives measure to all things? How should this change your awareness of His presence?

The Third Proof Against Vacuum and Its Refutation
Third proof: homogeneous vacuum makes rest or motion impossible; free will objection refuted.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof used by deniers of vacuum—that a homogeneous void makes rest and motion impossible—and its refutation, which shows that even divine free will cannot arbitrarily choose without a preponderating factor.

Core Idea
A homogeneous vacuum makes both rest and motion impossible—free will cannot break the symmetry.

Imam Razi presents a third argument against the existence of vacuum, based on the principle of tarjeeh bila murajjih (preference without a preponderator). This argument not only criticizes the concept of vacuum but also addresses a potential objection involving divine free will. By studying this passage, you will see how the same logical principle applies even to God's action.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation once to get the overall structure: the opponents' proof, the objection, and the refutation.
Notice that the argument is compact and uses logical reasoning.
2
Identify the core principle
Find where the argument says that in a homogeneous vacuum, rest and motion are impossible because they involve preferring one equal location over another without a reason.
This is the principle of tarjeeh bila murajjih.
3
Examine the objection and refutation
Read the objection that a free agent could choose arbitrarily. Then see how the refutation shows that even the free agent's power and will are equally related to all locations, so the problem remains.
The refutation strengthens the original argument by applying it to divine action.
4
Reflect on the universality of the principle
Consider why the same principle applies to both natural and voluntary actions. This shows the consistency of the author's reasoning.
This is a key point in kalam: the principle of sufficient reason is universal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة لهم : لو فرضنا الخلاء، لكان ذلك الخلاء متشابه الأجزاء، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك، امتنع أن يبقى الجسم فيه ساكنا ؛ لأن بقاءه في حيز معين مع مشابها لسائر الأحياز، ترجيح لأحد المتساويين على الآخر من غير مرجح. ويمتنع أيضا أن يبقى الجسم فيه متحركا؛ لأن الانتقال من أحد الحيزين إلى الحيز الآخر، تخصيص للمنتقل عنه بالهرب وللمنتقل إليه بالطلب. وذلك أيضا ترجيح لأحد المتساويين على الآخر من غير مرجح وهو محال. لايقال: لم لا يجوز أن يقال: إن الفاعل المختار يخصص الجسم ببعض تلك الأحياز على التعيين لمجرد القصد والاختيار ؛ لأنا نقول : إذا كانت الأحياز متساوية من جميع الاعتبارات والوجوه، كانت نسبة القدرة والإرادة إلى كلها على السوية. فلو اقتضت القدرة والإرادة تخصيص ذلك الجوهر ببعض تلك الأحياز دون البعض من غير مرجح أصلا، كان ذلك ترجيحا لأحد المتساويين على الآخر من غير مرجح، وهو محال .
The third proof for them: If we assumed vacuum, that vacuum would be homogeneous in parts. If that is so, it is impossible for a body to remain at rest in it, because its remaining in a particular location, while it is similar to all other locations, is a preference of one of two equals over the other without a preponderator. And it is also impossible for a body to remain moving in it, because the transition from one location to another is a specification of the location departed from as fleeing and the location arrived at as seeking, and that is also a preference of one of two equals over the other without a preponderator, which is impossible. It cannot be said: Why is it not permissible to say that the free agent specifies the body to some of those locations determinately by mere intention and choice? Because we say: If the locations are equal in all regards and aspects, then the relation of power and will to all of them is equal. So if power and will necessitate specifying that substance to some of those locations over others without any preponderator whatsoever, that would be a preference of one of two equals over the other without a preponderator, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This is the third argument against vacuum, claiming that a homogeneous void makes both rest and motion impossible. If a body is placed in a void, all locations are identical, so staying at one point would require arbitrarily choosing one equal over another (tarjeeh bila murajjih). Similarly, moving from one location to another would require a reason to leave one and seek another, also arbitrary. An objection is raised: could not a free agent (e.g., God) simply choose a location by will? The refutation argues that power and will are equally related to all locations; without any preponderating factor, choosing one is still arbitrary. The principle that preference without a preponderator is impossible applies even to divine action.

Key Terms
خلاء
متشابه الأجزاء
ترجيح بلا مرجح
فاعل مختار
قدرة
إرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that divine free will can arbitrarily choose a location without any reason, breaking the symmetry of the homogeneous void.
Correction: The refutation shows that even divine power and will are equally related to all equal options; choosing one without a preponderator would still be a preference without a preponderator, which is impossible. Therefore, divine action also requires a sufficient reason.

Checkpoint
How does the refutation answer the objection that a free agent could arbitrarily place the body?
A) It denies that a free agent exists.
B) It says power and will are equally related to all locations, so no preference is possible without a preponderator.
C) It claims the body itself chooses.
D) It says the vacuum is not homogeneous.

Key Takeaway
The principle that preference without a preponderator is impossible applies universally, including to divine action, making a homogeneous vacuum logically impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever assume that God's will is arbitrary, without reason? How does this argument challenge that assumption? Will you now trust that God's actions are based on wisdom even if you don't perceive it?
?
This argument shows that even God cannot do the logically impossible. Do you sometimes ask of God what is logically impossible? How should that change your dua or expectations?
?
The principle of sufficient reason applies to all choices. Examine your own life: are there decisions you make without a reason, expecting them to be justified by mere choice? How can you align your actions with this rational order?

Reframing Vacuum Measurement
Razi denies measurability of vacuum itself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi denies that vacuum itself can be measured or compared, and how he transfers measurement to potential bodies.

Core Idea
Measurement applies to potential bodies, not the void itself.

In answering the first argument of those who deny vacuum, Razi clarifies a crucial conceptual distinction. The objection assumes that if vacuum exists, it must be a quantity that can be measured. Razi rejects this premise by distinguishing between the vacuum itself and the bodies that could fill it. This short but precise response sharpens our understanding of what measurement truly entails.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: an initial denial, then a positive alternative, then an example.
The passage begins with a direct rejection: 'We do not concede that vacuum itself accepts measurement.'
2
Identify the key distinction
Razi distinguishes between 'vacuum itself' (nafs al-khala') and 'the thing that can occur in vacuum' (al-shay' alladhi yumkin husuluhu fi al-khala'). This is the core move.
Measurement, equality, and difference are attributed only to the latter.
3
Examine the example
The example contrasts the vacuum between the sides of a cup and a city. Razi says we do not say one vacuum is smaller; rather, the bodies that could fit in each space are being compared.
This clarifies the practical implication of the distinction.
4
Reflect on the argument's force
Consider how this answer undermines the first argument. The deniers claimed vacuum must be a quantity; Razi shows that what is measured is always the capacity for bodies, not the void itself.
This preserves the concept of vacuum without ascribing quantitative properties to it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الأول: لا نسلم أن نفس الخلاء يقبل التقدير والمساواة والمفاوتة، بل الشيء الذي يمكن حصوله في الخلاء، تثبت له هذه الأحكام مثلا، ولا نقول الخلاء الذي بين طرفي الطاس أقل من الخلاء الذي بين طرفي البلد، بل نقول الأجسام التي يمكن حصولها بين طرفي الطاس أقل من الأجسام التي يمكن حصولها بين طرفي البلد.
And the answer to the first: We do not concede that vacuum itself accepts measurement, equality, and difference. Rather, the thing that can occur in vacuum, these judgments are established for it. So we do not say that the vacuum between the sides of the cup is less than the vacuum between the sides of the city, but we say that the bodies that can occur between the sides of the cup are less than the bodies that can occur between the sides of the city.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This is the answer to the first argument of the deniers of vacuum. The objection denies that vacuum itself is measurable or comparable. Instead, the measurements (like smaller or larger) refer to the bodies that could potentially fill that space. So when one says 'the vacuum is smaller,' it is actually the capacity for bodies that is being compared. Thus the claim that vacuum must be a quantity is avoided.

Key Terms
خلاء
تقدير
جسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the key point in the answer to the first argument?
A) Vacuum is measurable because it is a body.
B) Measurement applies to potential bodies, not the void itself.
C) Vacuum is not real.
D) The deniers' examples are false.

Key Takeaway
Vacuum itself is not measurable; rather, it is the bodies that could occupy it that are measured.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Razi shifts measurement from the void to potential bodies. In your spiritual life, are you measuring the wrong things? Are you focusing on the 'void'—what is absent—instead of the 'potential bodies'—what can fill it with faith and action? Commit to one concrete change this week.
?
When you face uncertainty (like an empty future), do you mistakenly think the emptiness itself is the problem? How can you instead focus on the possibilities that could fill that space, as Razi teaches? Write down one specific fear about the future and reframe it as a space for potential good.

The Ash'ari Proof That God Can Recreate the Nonexistent
Deductive proof for God's power to recreate the nonexistent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari deductive argument that after annihilation, a thing remains possible of existence, and since God is powerful over all possibles, He can recreate it exactly.

Core Idea
Non-existence does not destroy possibility; God's power covers all possibles.

The Ash'ari school affirms that God can recreate the nonexistent identically. In this passage, Imam al-Razi presents a rational proof for this doctrine. He argues that even after a thing becomes nonexistent, its possibility of existence remains, and God's power encompasses all possible things. This lesson walks through the logical steps of this proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
The argument hinges on two key premises.
2
Identify the two main premises
The proof has two premises: (1) The nonexistent thing remains possible of existence. (2) God is powerful over all possible things. Locate these in the text.
3
Understand the justification for premise (1)
The author justifies why possibility persists using a dichotomy: the possibility is either a necessary concomitant of the thing's reality or an accidental attribute. Follow the reasoning that leads to the conclusion that possibility is permanent.
This is the core of the argument.
4
Connect to the established premise about God's power
Recall that the premise 'God is powerful over all possibles' has already been proven earlier. Combine it with premise (1) to reach the conclusion.
5
Grasp the conclusion
The conclusion is that God is powerful to recreate the nonexistent exactly. Ensure you see how it follows from the two premises.
This affirms the Ash'ari position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذي يدل على صحة هذا القول : أن الشيء إذا صار معدوماً، فإنه بعد العدم بقي جائز الوجود، والله تعالى قادر على جميع الجائزات فوجب القطع بكونه تعالى قادرًا على إعادته بعينه، بعد العدم
وإنما قلنا : إنه بعد عدمه بقي جائز الوجود؛ لأنه قبل عدمه جائز الوجود وهذا الجواز إما أن يكون من لوازم حقيقته، وإما أن يكون من عوارض حقيقته. فإن كان من لوازم حقيقته، وجب أن لا يزول . وإن كان من عوارض حقيقته كانت تلك الحقيقة بحيث يجوز عليها ذلك الجواز فينتقل إلى جواز الجواز، ولا يتسلسل، بل بالآخرة إلى جواز، هو من لوازم الحقيقة. وهذا نقيض حصول هذا الجواز حالتي الوجود والعدم فثبت بهذا أن الجواز حاصل أبدا . وأما أنه تعالى قادر على كل الجائزات فقد تقدم إثباته. ويلزم من مجموع الأمرين كونه تعالى قادرا على إعادة المعدوم.
And what indicates the correctness of this saying is that when a thing becomes nonexistent, it remains possible of existence after nonexistence. And God Almighty is powerful over all possible things, so it is necessary to be certain that He is powerful to recreate it exactly after nonexistence.
We only say that after its nonexistence it remains possible of existence because before its nonexistence it was possible of existence. This possibility is either from the necessary concomitants of its reality or from the accidents of its reality. If it is from the necessary concomitants of its reality, then it must not cease. If it is from the accidents of its reality, then that reality is such that this possibility can befall it, leading to a possibility of possibility, and there is no infinite regress, but ultimately it ends at a possibility that is a necessary concomitant of the reality. This is contrary to the occurrence of this possibility in both states of existence and nonexistence. Thus it is established that possibility obtains always. As for the fact that He is powerful over all possibles, its proof has preceded. And from the combination of both points it follows that He is powerful to recreate the nonexistent.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'ari proof for God's ability to recreate the nonexistent. The argument proceeds in stages: First, after a thing becomes nonexistent, it remains possible of existence. Second, God is powerful over all possible things. Therefore, God is powerful to recreate it. The author then defends the premise that it remains possible after nonexistence. The possibility (جواز) that existed before nonexistence must either be a necessary concomitant of the thing's reality (لازم حقيقته) or an accidental attribute (عارض). If necessary, it never ceases. If accidental, it requires a deeper possibility until reaching one that is necessary. In either case, possibility persists permanently. Since God's power over all possibles is already established, the conclusion follows.

Key Terms
جائز الوجود
لوازم الحقيقة
عوارض الحقيقة
إعادة المعدوم
قادر على كل الجائزات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that possibility of existence requires an existing substrate or that annihilation makes a thing impossible forever.
Correction: The author demonstrates that possibility is a property of the reality itself, independent of existence, and it persists after annihilation.

Checkpoint
What is the key premise about the nonexistent thing in the Ash'ari proof?
A) It becomes impossible.
B) It remains possible of existence.
C) It retains its essence.
D) It is not subject to any judgment.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari proof rests on the permanence of possibility after annihilation and God's universal power over all possibles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author argues that possibility remains even after a thing becomes nonexistent. What does this imply about your own deeds and their 'possibility' after you die? Will God be able to bring them back for judgment? Are you living as if your acts are permanently real or permanently possible before God?
?
If possibility is a necessary concomitant of your reality, what does that say about your capacity for change? Is there some deep aspect of your character that you assume will never change—and is that assumption based on this lesson or on your own despair?
?
This proof rests on God's power over all possibles. Is there a 'possible' change in your life that you have stopped praying for because you think it's too far gone or too late? How does this lesson challenge that belief?

Refuting the Objection that Possibility Requires an Existing Essence
Imam Razi presents an objection and refutes it with four proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash'ari theologians defend the possibility of judging nonexistent entities, using reason and logical argument.

Core Idea
Judgments about possibility do not require existing subjects; reason judges nonexistents.

The objection claims that possibility attaches to a quiddity, which must continue in nonexistence for possibility to remain. The Ash'ari response shows that this is not necessary. Instead, even after annihilation, we can judge things as possible or impossible, as proven by reason and consistent reasoning. This passage examines four proofs that dissolve the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the claim that possibility remains only if the quiddity survives; this is the crux of the opponent's argument.
The objection uses the language of 'necessary concomitant' (لازم).
2
Examine the initial reply
Note that the Ash'ari response does not fully rebut but points out its reliance on 'the nonexistent is a thing', which Ash'aris reject.
This sets up the deeper answer.
3
Follow the four proofs
The author provides four independent proofs that judging nonexistents is valid. For each, identify how it shows that attention does not require existence.
The fourth proof is incomplete in the text.
4
Reflect on the third proof's self-referential point
The third proof is a powerful logical argument: claiming something cannot be judged is itself a judgment.
This is a classic dialectical move.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : قولكم هذا الجواز لازم لماهيته فيبقى الجواز ببقاء الماهية). قلنا : هذا الكلام متين إلا أنه مبني على أن الماهية باقية حال العدم وهذا لا يتم إلا مع القول بأن المعدوم شيء، وأنتم لا تقولون به.
والجواب : أن بطلان الماهية حال العدم لا يمنع من الحكم عليها بالجواز والامتناع ويدل عليه وجوه :
أحدها: أن صريح العقل يحكم بأن هذا المحدث كان جائز الحدوث قبل أن حدث، وهذا الذي حكم العقل عليه بالجواز ليس هو الماهية ؛ لأن الماهية لو كانت واجبة التقرر والتحقق حالتي الوجود والعدم تكون ممتنعة التغير والتبدل من حيث إنها ماهية. ويمتنع أن يكون جواز التغير والتبدل نعتا وصفة لها بل لا بد أن يكون هذا الجواز نعتا للوجود فقط أو لموصوفية الماهية بالوجود ثم إن الوجود قبل تجدده البتة ما كان حاصلا فعلمنا أن عدم حصوله وعدم تحققه في نفسه، لا يمنع من الحكم عليه بالجواز.
وثانيها : إن الخصم يحكم على هذا الذي عدم وفني بالكلية بأنه يمتنع عوده والمحكوم عليه بامتناع العود ليس إلا ذلك الشخص الذي فني فعلمنا : أن فناءه لا يمنع صحة الحكم عليه بأنه جائز أو ممتنع.
وثالثها: أن الذي فني فهو بعد فنائه إما أن يجوز الحكم عليه بشيء من الأحكام، أو لا يجوز . فإن جاز الحكم عليه فقد سقط هذا السؤال، وإن امتنع الحكم عليه كان ذلك متناقضا، لأن تخصيصه بأنه يمتنع الحكم عليه حكم عليه بهذا الامتناع. فثبت أن القول بأنه يمتنع الحكم عليه يوجب كونه محكوما عليه. وما أدى ثبوته إلى نفيه كان باطلا، فبطل القول بأن ما فني وعدم امتنع الحكم عليه.
ورابعها : أنا نحكم على شريك الباري بأنه ممتنع وعلى الجمع بين الضدين بأنه ممتنع، فالمحكوم عليه بهذا الامتناع هو شريك الله تعالى، والجمع بين الضدين ثم الماهيات ليس لها تحقق أصلا البتة باتفاق العقلاء، فعلمنا أن الحكم على الشيء لا
If it is said: Your saying that this possibility is a necessary concomitant of its quiddity, so the possibility remains by the survival of the quiddity. We say: This speech is solid, except that it is based on the quiddity remaining in the state of nonexistence, and this is not complete except with the saying that the nonexistent is a thing, and you do not say that.
And the answer: That the annihilation of the quiddity in the state of nonexistence does not prevent judging it with possibility and impossibility, and proofs indicate this:
One of them: That pure reason judges that this originated thing was possible of occurrence before it occurred, and that which reason judged with possibility is not the quiddity; because if the quiddity were necessarily established and realized in both states of existence and nonexistence, it would be impossible of change and alteration insofar as it is a quiddity. And it is impossible for the possibility of change and alteration to be an attribute and description of it. Rather, this possibility must be an attribute of existence only or of the quiddity's being described by existence. Then existence before its origination was not at all present, so we know that its non-occurrence and non-realization in itself does not prevent judging it with possibility.
The second: That the opponent judges that this which has perished and become entirely annihilated is impossible of return, and that which is judged with impossibility of return is nothing but that person who has perished. So we know that its annihilation does not prevent the correctness of judging it with possibility or impossibility.
The third: That which has perished, after its perishing, either it is permissible to judge it with some judgments, or not. If it is permissible to judge it, then this objection collapses. If it is impossible to judge it, then that is contradictory, because specifying it as impossible to judge is a judgment upon it with this impossibility. Thus it is established that the saying that it is impossible to judge it necessitates it being judged. And what leads its affirmation to its negation is false, so the saying that what has perished and become nonexistent is impossible to judge is false.
The fourth: That we judge the partner of God (idol) as impossible and the combination of two opposites as impossible, and that which is judged with this impossibility is the partner of God and the combination of two opposites. Then quiddities have no realization at all whatsoever by agreement of the rational people. So we know that judging a thing does not...
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and the Ash'ari response. The objection argues that possibility requires an existing quiddity (ماهية), so the earlier proof fails because the Ash'aris deny the quiddity remains. The initial reply (قلنا) points out that the objection assumes the quiddity survives, which the Ash'aris reject. Then the author provides a fuller answer (والجواب) with four proofs that the annihilation of the quiddity does not prevent judgment. First proof: reason judges a thing possible before it exists, and that judgment is not about the quiddity (which would be unchangeable) but about existence or its attribution. Second proof: the opponent himself judges that the perished thing cannot return, which is a judgment on that thing. Third proof: if the perished thing cannot be judged, then the claim 'it cannot be judged' is itself a judgment, creating a contradiction. Fourth proof: we judge impossible things (like a partner of God) as impossible, yet those quiddities have no real existence. Thus judging does not require an existing quiddity.

Key Terms
ماهية
جواز
امتناع
حكم على الشيء
المعدوم شيء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Judgments about possibility or impossibility only apply to things that currently exist.
Correction: Reason can judge nonexistent things as possible or impossible, as demonstrated by the four proofs.

Checkpoint
According to the third proof, what happens if someone claims the perished thing cannot be judged at all?
A) It is a valid claim.
B) It is contradictory because the claim itself is a judgment.
C) It applies only to certain cases.
D) It means the thing exists in some form.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari defense shows that rational judgments do not depend on the existence of the judged entity; even the nonexistent can be rationally assessed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you judge something as impossible—like associating partners with Allah—do you rely on the fact that those false gods don't exist physically, or on a deeper rational conviction that they cannot be divine? How does this passage challenge you to base your faith on pure reason rather than on what you can see? What change must you make in your heart to affirm tawhid even when all visible evidence seems to point elsewhere?
?
If you were to lose every possession, loved one, or worldly certainty, would your judgment that Allah alone is worthy of worship remain unchanged? This passage shows that judgment can stand even when the subject is annihilated. How solid is your conviction that Allah exists, even when you feel His presence faintly? Ask yourself honestly: Do you need constant signs and blessings to believe, or is your faith rooted in rational necessity?
?
The passage proves that reason judges possibilities before they occur. Reflect on a past sin you committed. Before you did it, you knew it was possible but also forbidden. Did you judge it as possible but not necessary? How did that judgment affect your choice? Now, with this lesson, how can you train yourself to judge future sins as not only possible but also evil and avoidable? What concrete step will you take today to strengthen your rational aversion to sin?

The Impossibility of Judging Non-Existence
Al-Razi defends the impossibility of judging non-existence against an objection and gives a fifth argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Al-Razi argues that a judgment cannot have non-existence as its subject, and how he refutes objections and offers a fifth argument based on the logical opposition between existence and non-existence.

Core Idea
A judgment requires a real subject; pure non-existence has no reality to be judged.

Al-Razi tackles a fundamental puzzle: we often speak of 'impossible things' or 'non-existence,' but how can a judgment be about something that does not exist? In this passage, he defends the principle that every judgment requires a subject that has some reality, addressing a key objection and then presenting a fifth argument that uses the concept of non-existence itself to show its impossibility as a subject.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the argument flow. Note that the passage has two parts: a response to an objection and a fifth argument.
Pay attention to the terms 'المحكوم عليه' (subject of judgment) and 'الامتناع' (impossibility).
2
Understand the objection
Identify the objection Al-Razi anticipates: that the subject of the impossibility judgment is the mental form (الصورة المستحضرة) of the thing. Why might someone think this solves the problem?
The objector assumes the mental form can serve as an existent subject.
3
Analyze Al-Razi's response
Al-Razi argues that the mental form is itself existent in the mind, so the judgment is not about an impossible external existence but about the mental form—which is not what we intend. The intended subject (external existence) has no reality, so no judgment is possible.
Notice the distinction between mental existence and external existence.
4
Follow the fifth argument
Al-Razi presents a new argument: we judge that existence and non-existence are opposites. The subject of this judgment is the concept of non-existence (مسمى العدم). But since non-existence is the opposite of reality, it cannot have reality at all, so it cannot be a genuine subject.
This argument uses the very opposition of existence and non-existence to show that non-existence cannot be a subject.
5
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this matters for theology: if judgments about non-existence are impossible, then we cannot speak of 'things' that do not exist as subjects of divine power or knowledge in the same way. This reinforces the principle that all judgments require an existing subject.
This principle is foundational in Islamic theology for discussions about divine attributes and creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يستدعي كون المحكوم عليه ثبوتًا. لا يقال : المحكوم عليه بالامتناع هو الصورة المستحضرة في الذهن؛ لأنا نقول : تلك الصورة لما كانت حاضرة في الذهن موجودة هناك لم يكن الحكم عليها يكونها ممتنعة الوجود ، بل المحكوم عليه بالامتناع هو وجوده في الخارج، لكن وجوده في الخارج غير ثابت أصلاً، فإن المحكوم عليه بهذا الحكم لا تحقق له البتة. وخامسها : أنا نحكم بأن الوجود والعدم لا يجتمعان وهذا الحكم على مسمى العدم بأنه ينافي الوجود ويعانده فالمحكوم عليه بهذه المعاندة والمنافاة هو مسمى العدم، ومسمى العدم يمتنع أن يكون له تحقق؛ لأنه نقيض التحقق والتقرر، وأحد هذين النقيضين لا يكون عين الثاني.
[The judgment] requires the subject of judgment to be [actually] existent. Let it not be said: 'That whose impossibility is judged is the form present in the mind'; for we say: that form, since it is present in the mind and existent there, the judgment upon it is not that it is impossible of existence; rather, that whose impossibility is judged is its existence in the external [world], but its existence in the external [world] is not established at all, so the subject of this judgment has no reality whatsoever. And the fifth [argument]: we judge that existence and non-existence do not combine, and this judgment upon the designation of non-existence is that it contradicts existence and opposes it; so the subject of this contradiction and opposition is the designation of non-existence, and the designation of non-existence cannot have reality because it is the opposite of reality and establishment, and one of two opposites is not identical to the other.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the principle that judgment requires an actual subject. Al-Razi begins by reiterating that a judgment (specifically one of impossibility) requires the subject to be existent. He then addresses an objection: the object of the impossibility judgment might be a mental form (of the thing), not the external thing. His answer is that the mental form is itself existent in the mind, so the impossibility is not about that form but about the external existence of that thing; however, that external existence has no reality at all, so there is no actual subject for the judgment. He then presents a fifth argument: we judge that existence and non-existence are mutually exclusive; here the subject judged is 'non-existence' (al-ʿadam) as a concept. But non-existence as a concept cannot have any reality because it is the opposite of reality itself. Since the two opposites (existence and non-existence) cannot be identical, non-existence cannot be a real subject. Thus, the judgment is actually about a conceptual label, not an external reality.

Key Terms
المحكوم عليه
الامتناع
الصورة المستحضرة
العدم
الوجود
النقيضان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that we can judge non-existence because we have a concept of it in our minds, making the judgment possible.
Correction: Al-Razi clarifies that the concept in the mind is existent (as a mental entity), so it is not pure non-existence. The judgment about an external non-existent thing would require that non-existent thing to be a subject, which it cannot be because it has no reality.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi's fifth argument, why cannot non-existence (al-ʿadam) be the subject of a judgment?
A) Because non-existence has no reality and is the opposite of reality.
B) Because non-existence can only be known through the mind.
C) Because judgment requires a subject that is both mental and external.
D) Because non-existence is a relative concept.

Key Takeaway
A judgment cannot have pure non-existence as its subject because non-existence has no reality; even the concept of non-existence in the mind is existent, so the subject must always have some mode of existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you judge something as impossible, what is the actual subject of that judgment in your mind? Are you judging the external thing or your mental concept? How does this affect your certainty in your judgments?
?
Al-Razi argues that non-existence cannot be a subject of judgment. Have you ever caught yourself thinking about 'nothing' as if it were a thing? What does this reveal about the way your mind operates?
?
If non-existence has no reality, then what does it mean to fear non-existence (like death or annihilation)? Is that fear directed at nothing? How should this reshape your view of your own existence?

First Proof Against Re-Creation and Its Refutation
Al-Razi presents the first argument against re-creation and then refutes it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection to the possibility of recreating the non-existent and how al-Razi refutes it by denying the required premise.

Core Idea
Possibility does not require the subject to be actual at the time of judgment.

This lesson presents a classic kalam objection: if something no longer exists, how can it be re-created as the same thing? The objection claims that for re-creation to be possible, the thing must retain its identity after non-existence, which is impossible. Al-Razi's response cuts to the core by showing that the possibility judgment does not depend on the subject's actual existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first sentence of the Arabic. It states that judging a thing's possibility of being re-created depends on the thing being exactly itself (huwa huwa). After non-existence, it is pure negation, so judgment is invalid.
Notice the terms: هو هو, متعين, متخصص.
2
Identify al-Razi's response
Read the second sentence. Al-Razi answers by referring to earlier proofs that possibility does not require the subject to be actualized.
The answer is brief and references established arguments.
3
Reflect on the dialectical structure
This is a typical objection-and-answer (شبهة وجواب) structure. Al-Razi does not repeat his earlier proofs but cites them.
This shows the cumulative nature of kalam arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الأولى : أن الحكم على الشيء بأنه يجوز إعادته متوقف على كون ذلك الشيء هو هو، أعني على كونه متعينًا في نفسه متخصصا في ذاته، والشيء بعد عدمه نفي محض، وليس له تخصيص ولا تشخص، فكان الحكم عليه بجواز الإعادة باطلا. والجواب عن الأول : أنا قد دللنا على أن الحكم على الشيء بالجواز لا يستدعي كون المحكوم عليه متحققا متعينا في نفسه بالدلائل القاطعة.
The first proof: the judgment upon a thing that its re-creation is possible depends on that thing being exactly itself, meaning being determined in itself and specified in its essence; and a thing after its non-existence is pure negation, and it has no specification or individuation; so the judgment upon it that re-creation is possible is invalid. And the answer to the first: we have proven with decisive proofs that the judgment upon a thing concerning possibility does not require the subject of judgment to be actualized and determined in itself.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This lesson pairs an objection (proof) from those who deny the possibility of re-creating the non-existent (iʿādat al-maʿdūm) with al-Razi's response. The objection argues that for something to be re-created, it must remain the same individual (ʿayn al-shayʾ). But after non-existence, the thing is pure nothing, without any property or individuation, so it cannot be the same. Al-Razi's answer is that he has already established (in earlier arguments not repeated here) that judgment of possibility does not require the subject to be actually existent or determined in reality; thus the objection's premise is false. The answer is brief, referring back to prior proofs.

Key Terms
إعادة المعدوم
التخصيص
التشخص
الجواز
المحكوم عليه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Re-creating the non-existent is impossible because the thing must be exactly the same individual, which non-existence cannot provide.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that the condition of identity does not apply; possibility is judged without requiring actual existence of the subject.

Checkpoint
What is the core premise of the first proof against re-creation?
A) Re-creation is impossible because God cannot repeat an act.
B) Judgment of possibility requires the subject to be specifically determined in itself.
C) Non-existence is a relative state that cannot be judged.
D) The original thing ceases to exist entirely.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi refutes the first objection against re-creation by denying that possibility requires the subject to be actualized.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever doubted that Allah can bring you back after death because you think your life is just a random sequence with no lasting identity? How does al-Razi's refutation of that objection affect your certainty about your own resurrection?
?
If Allah can re-create you with the same identity even after complete annihilation, what does that mean for how you live today? Are you living as if your deeds will be resurrected with you?
?
When you consider the objection that nothing can return after non-existence, do you find any part of your heart that leans toward that doubt? How can you use al-Razi's answer to strengthen your faith in Allah's power to re-create?
