The Second Proof and Its Refutation: Time and Origination
Al-Razi presents and refutes the second argument about time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's second objection to re-creation and his refutation that origination is not conditioned by time.

Core Idea
Origination is not conditioned by time; time itself is created, avoiding infinite regress.

Al-Razi now presents a second objection that claims re-creation would require re-creating the original time, leading to a contradiction. However, he refutes it by explaining that the concept of origination does not depend on time; rather, time is a created thing. This lesson examines both the objection and the refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the structure: first the objection, then the answer.
2
Identify the objector's assumption
The objector assumes that re-creation would require the re-creation of the original time. Why would that be a problem? Because it would make the thing both a beginning and a re-creation in the same time.
This is a paradox the objector uses to argue against re-creation.
3
Understand Al-Razi's refutation
Al-Razi argues that origination (ḥudūth) is not conditional upon time. If it were, time itself would need to be originated, leading to an infinite regress. Instead, distinguish between the beginning and the re-created.
The beginning is not preceded by any origination; the re-created is preceded by an origination.
4
Reflect on the solution
Consider how Al-Razi's distinction removes the contradiction. The re-created thing does not need the same time; it simply has a prior origination. Time is irrelevant to the definition.
This shows that the objection is based on a misunderstanding of time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : أنه لو كانت إعادة المعدوم جائزة لكانت إعادة الوقت الذي أحدثه الله تعالى فيه أولا جائزة، وبتقدير أن يعيد الله تعالى الوقت الأول، ويعيده فيه كانت هذه الإعادة حدوثًا في وقته الأول، فيلزم أن يقال: إنه من حيث هو معاد ليس بمعاد، بل مبدأ، وذلك جمع بين النقيضين، وهو محال. والجواب عن الثاني : أن حدوث الشيء غير مشروط بالوقت، وإلا لكان ذلك الوقت حادثاً، فيلزم افتقاره إلى وقت آخر، ويلزم التسلسل، بل حدوث المبدأ هو الذي لا يكون مسبوقا بالحدوث البتة، وحدوث المعاد هو أن يكون مسبوقا بحدوث آخر، وعلى التقديرين يزول السؤال.
The second proof: if re-creation of the non-existent were possible, then the re-creation of the time in which God first brought it into existence would also be possible; and assuming that God re-creates the first time and re-creates it in that time, this re-creation would be a coming-into-existence in its first time; so it would necessarily be said that it, insofar as it is re-created, is not re-created but rather a beginning; and that is a combination of opposites, which is impossible. And the answer to the second: the coming-into-existence of a thing is not conditioned by time; otherwise, that time would itself be originated, leading to its need for another time, and an infinite regress would ensue. Rather, the coming-into-existence of the beginning (al-mabda') is that which is not preceded by any coming-into-existence at all, and the coming-into-existence of the re-created (al-muʿād) is that it is preceded by another coming-into-existence; in both cases, the objection disappears.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This lesson contains the second objection and its refutation. The objector argues that re-creation would require the re-creation of the original time, leading to a paradox: the thing would be both original and re-created in that same time, combining opposites. Al-Razi's answer is that origination (ḥudūth) is not conditional upon time; time itself is created. If origination required time, then time would also need to be originated, causing infinite regress. Instead, the 'beginning' (al-mabda') is that which is not preceded by any other origination, while the 're-created' (al-muʿād) is preceded by a prior origination. Thus the distinction avoids the contradiction; the objection is nullified.

Key Terms
إعادة المعدوم
الوقت
الحدوث
المبدأ
المعاد
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that time is an absolute container for events, so re-creation would require re-creating the original time.
Correction: Al-Razi clarifies that time itself is created and does not condition origination; the re-created thing is defined by being preceded by an origination, not by its temporal location.

Checkpoint
How does al-Razi refute the claim that re-creation would require re-creating the original time?
A) He argues that time is eternal and cannot be re-created.
B) He states that origination is not conditioned by time, otherwise infinite regress would follow.
C) He says that re-creation does not involve time at all.
D) He denies the possibility of re-creating time.

Key Takeaway
Time is created and does not condition origination; thus, the objection that re-creation would require re-creating time fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If time is not an absolute container but created by God moment by moment, how does that affect the way you view the 'time' you have wasted or lost? Are you holding onto a concept of time as if it were independent of God, and what would it mean to release that false security?
?
Al-Razi distinguishes between the beginning (al-mabda') and the re-created (al-muʿād). When you consider your own existence, do you live as if you are your own originator, or do you acknowledge that your very coming-into-existence is entirely from God and not tied to time? How does this reshape your sense of accountability for every moment?
?
The objection assumes that re-creation would require re-creating the past time, but al-Razi shows that time is not a condition. In your own life, are you trying to re-create or relive past moments, believing they are fixed containers? What would change if you truly accepted that every moment is created anew by God and that your past is not a container you can step back into?

Al-Razi's Third Proof Against Re-Creation and Its Refutation
Al-Razi states the third proof and then refutes it using individuality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's third objection to re-creation and his refutation based on the distinction between quiddity and individuality.

Core Idea
Two identical atoms are not one and the same; each has its own unique individuality.

Al-Razi now presents a subtle argument against the possibility of re-creating a non-existent thing. The argument relies on the idea that two newly created atoms are indistinguishable in essence, leading to a dilemma. But Al-Razi's own answer resolves the dilemma by appealing to the concept of individuation. Let us follow the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Notice the flow from the objection to the refutation.
The passage is dense but follows a logical order.
2
Understand the objection: the dilemma of identical atoms
Identify the scenario: an original atom perishes, then two new atoms are created. The objector claims that since both new atoms are identical in nature, there is no way to determine which one is the original, leading to absurdity if both are considered the original, or impossibility if neither is.
This is the core of the third proof.
3
See the initial counter-answer and its circularity
The objector anticipates a response that one atom is 'the very same' as the original. Al-Razi dismisses this as circular reasoning: it explains the thing by itself.
Al-Razi points out the fallacy of explaining identity by identity.
4
Grasp Al-Razi's refutation: individuality distinguishes
Al-Razi asserts that individuals of a species, though similar in essence (māhiyya), are not similar in individuality (shakhṣiyya). Each has its own determination (taʿayyun) and individuation (tashakhkhuṣ). Therefore, the two new atoms are not perfectly identical; one could be the original re-created, the other a likeness.
This refutation breaks the dilemma by denying the initial premise of complete identicalness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : إذا فرضنا أن جوهرًا قد عدم ثم فرضنا أن الله تعالى أعاده وفرضن أنه تعالى أحدث جوهرًا آخر فنسبة هذين الجوهرين إلى ذلك الذي عدم أولاً نسبة واحدة، فلم يكن كون أحد هذين الجوهرين عين ذلك الذي عدم، والآخر مثله أولى من العكس، فيلزم إما القول بأن كل واحد منهما هو عين ذلك الشيء الذي عدم، فيلزم أن يكون الشيء الواحد شيئين، وهو محال. وأما القول بأنه ليس كل واحد منهما عين ذلك الذي عدم، بل كان كل واحد منهما مغايرا له ومثلا له فذلك هو الحق، وهو يمنع من جواز إعادة المعدوم. ولا يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن أحدهما أولى بأن يكون عين الذي عدم وذلك لأن هذا عين ما كان والآخر مثله فكان أحدهما أولى بأن يكون عين ما كان؟ لأنا نقول : إن هذين الجوهرين اللذين حدثا مثلان من جميع الوجوه، فكانت نسبة كل واحد منهما إلى الذي عدم كنسبة الآخر إليه، وإذا كانا كذلك لم يكن كون أحدهما هو عين ذلك الذي عدم والآخر مثله أولى من العكس، فقولكم : إنما كان ذلك الذي عدم؛ لأن هذا هو عين ذلك هذا عين الشيء، تعليل للشيء بنفسه، ويكون المعنى أنه إنما كان هذا عين ما انقضى ؛ لأن هذا عين ما انقضى، ومعلوم أن هذا باطل. والجواب عن الثالث : أن أفراد النوع الواحد وإن كانت متماثلة في الماهية إلا أنه لا شك أنها غير متماثلة في الشخصية، بل كل واحد من تلك الأفراد مختص بتعينه وتشخصه، وإذا كان كذلك فلا نسلم أن نسبة هذين الجوهرين الحادثين إلى ذلك الذي عدم نسبة واحدة بل القول بأن هذه النسبة واحدة لا بد أن القول يصح إلا بأن إعادة المعدوم باطلة؛ لأن بتقدير أن يصح هذا القول كان أحد هذين الحادثين هو ذلك الذي عدم والحادث الآخر ليس عينه، بل مثله، فإذن القول بأن نسبة هذين الحادثين إلى ذلك الذي عدم نسبة واحدة مبني على أن إعادة المعدوم ممتنعة، ولو بينا هذا الامتناع بهذه المقدمة لزم الدور، وأنه باطل.
The third proof: if we suppose that an atom (jawhar) has perished, then we suppose that God, exalted, re-creates it, and we suppose that He creates another atom, then the relation of these two atoms to that which perished first is one and the same relation. So it is no more fitting that one of these two atoms be the very essence of that which perished and the other be its likeness than the opposite. Hence it follows either to say that each of the two is the very same thing that perished, which would entail that one thing is two things, which is impossible; or to say that neither of the two is the very thing that perished, but each is different from it and similar to it—that is the truth, and it prevents the possibility of re-creation of the non-existent. And let it not be said: 'Why is it not permissible to say that one of them is more fitting to be the essence of that which perished, because this one is the very thing that was, and the other is its likeness, so one is more fitting to be the essence of what was?' For we say: these two atoms that come into existence are similar in all respects, so the relation of each to that which perished is like the relation of the other to it. And if they are so, then it is no more fitting that one of them be the essence of that which perished and the other be its likeness than the opposite. So your statement 'It is that which perished because this is the very essence of that thing' is an explanation of the thing by itself, and the meaning is: it is exactly what passed away because it is exactly what passed away, and this is obviously invalid. And the answer to the third: the individuals of a single species, although they are similar in quiddity (māhiyya), yet there is no doubt that they are not similar in individuality (shakhṣiyya); rather, each one of those individuals is distinguished by its determination (taʿayyun) and its individuation (tashakhkhuṣ). And if so, we do not concede that the relation of these two newly occurred atoms to that which perished is one and the same; rather, the statement that this relation is one and the same is only valid if the re-creation of the non-existent is impossible; because assuming this statement is valid, one of these two newly occurred atoms would be that which perished, and the other would not be its essence but its likeness. Therefore, the statement that the relation of these two newly occurred things to that which perished is one and the same is based on the impossibility of re-creation; and if we were to prove this impossibility by this premise, there would be circular reasoning (al-dawr), and that is invalid.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This lesson contains the third and most intricate objection followed by al-Razi's refutation. The objection imagines an original atom that perishes, then God creates two new atoms. Since both new atoms are identical in nature, there is no way to determine which one is the original and which is a copy. Thus either both are the original (absurd), or neither is (implying re-creation is impossible). The objector anticipates a response: perhaps one is metaphysically tied to the original by being 'the very same'. Al-Razi dismisses this as circular. In his answer, al-Razi invokes a key kalam doctrine: individuals of a species, though similar in essence (māhiyya), are distinguished by their individual determinations (taʿayyun and tashakhkhuṣ). Therefore the two new atoms are not completely identical; each has its own haecceity. Thus the relation of each to the original is not the same; one could be the original re-created, the other a likeness. The objection's circularity is exposed: the claim that the relation is the same presupposes the impossibility of re-creation, which it attempts to prove.

Key Terms
جوهر
إعادة المعدوم
الماهية
الشخصية
التعين
التشخص
الدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two things that are identical in all properties are the same entity.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that individuals of a species, though identical in quiddity (essence), are distinct in their individuality (shakhṣiyya), so they are not the same.

Checkpoint
What is al-Razi's key point in refuting the third proof against re-creation?
A) Atoms cannot be identical because they have different positions.
B) Individuals of the same species are not identical in their individuality (shakhṣiyya).
C) God creates each atom from nothing, so there is no original to compare.
D) The objection commits the fallacy of composition.

Key Takeaway
The refutation shows that re-creation is possible because individuals have unique determinations that persist through non-existence, allowing a given atom to be re-created as the very same individual.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe that God will resurrect you on the Day of Judgment as the very same person you are now. But do you truly live today as if your unique individuality — your shakhṣiyya — will be brought back, accountable for every detail of your life? Or do you act as if death erases your personal identity, making your choices insignificant? Reflect on one specific habit you have that you would be ashamed to face if it were re-created exactly as you left it.
?
The objector argued that two identical atoms cannot be distinguished, so re-creation is impossible. But Al-Razi reminded us that every individual has a unique determination (taʿayyun) from God. In your daily interactions, do you treat others as unique individuals with their own God-given identities, or do you lump them into categories, forgetting that each soul is distinct and unrepeatable? How will you change one interaction tomorrow to honor someone's individual dignity?
?
Al-Razi used logical reasoning to defend a core theological truth. Do you use your intellect to strengthen your faith, or do you rely solely on imitation and emotion? When you encounter doubts about resurrection or God's power, do you dismiss them or engage with them using the tools of rational argument? Commit to one step this week to apply reason to a belief you hold, so that your conviction is built on understanding, not blind acceptance.

Second Objection: Eternity vs. Everlastingness of Souls
Razi presents a second objection and response on soul eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection against the eternity of souls and how Razi defends the traditional view using a mathematical argument about infinity.

Core Idea
An actually infinite number of souls is impossible because every existing number is finite.

This passage presents a dialectical exchange where the opponent concedes that souls are eternal but questions why they cannot be everlasting. Razi responds by first exposing the hidden assumption of infinite past cycles, then addresses the possibility of an actually infinite number of souls with a subtle mathematical premise. The argument hinges on the nature of numbers and infinity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the back-and-forth structure: objection, response, concession, new objection, final response.
Pay attention to key terms like a-zaliyya, abadiyya, ghayr mutanahiya.
2
Analyze the first response
The first response argues that everlastingness would make currently existing souls infinite. This relies on the assumption that past cycles are infinite, which Razi says was refuted earlier.
Reflect on why that assumption is problematic.
3
Examine the second objection and response
The opponent concedes but asks why infinite souls cannot exist now. Razi responds with a principle: every existing number can increase or decrease, so it must be finite.
Consider why an actually infinite number cannot be increased or decreased.
4
Reflect on the subtle premise
Razi notes that this premise involves a subtle discussion from his philosophical works. Think about what makes it subtle.
This connects to broader debates on infinity in philosophy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمنا أنها أزلية، فلم قلتم بأنها لا تكون أبدية؟ قوله : لو كانت أبدية لكانت النفوس الموجودة الآن غير متناهية. قلنا : هذا بناء على أن الأدوار الماضية غير متناهية وقد بينا إبطال ذلك في مسألة الحدوث. سلمنا، لكن لم لا يجوز أن يقال: حصل الآن نفوس غير متناهية ؟ قوله : كل عدد موجود فهو قابل للزيادة والنقصان وكل ما كان كذلك فهو متناه. قلنا : هذه المقدمة فيها بحث دقيق ذكرناه في الكتب الفلسفية.
We grant that they are eternal, so why do you say they are not everlasting? He said: If they were everlasting, the souls existing now would be infinite. We said: This is based on past cycles being infinite, and we have shown the refutation of that in the topic of origination. We grant that, but why is it not allowed to say: infinite souls exist now? He said: Every existing number is susceptible to increase and decrease, and everything that is so is finite. We said: This premise contains a subtle discussion which we have mentioned in the philosophical books.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage is an objection and response exchange. It begins with the second objection against the argument that souls are eternal but not everlasting. The objector argues: we accept that souls are eternal (azaliyya), but why do you claim they are not everlasting (abadiyya)? The response is that if they were everlasting, the souls existing now would be infinite in number. But this assumes that past cycles of time are infinite, which has already been refuted in the section on origination (huduth). The objector then concedes that point but asks: why cannot infinite souls exist now? The response cites a mathematical principle: every existing number is capable of increase and decrease, and anything capable of increase and decrease is finite. Therefore, an actually infinite number of souls is impossible. Razi notes that this premise involves a subtle investigation detailed in his philosophical works.

Correction: The passage shows that even without infinite past cycles, an actually infinite number of souls now is impossible because any existing number is finite by virtue of being susceptible to increase and decrease.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The opponent assumed that infinite souls could exist; Razi corrected that. Is there an area in your own beliefs where you have assumed something infinite (e.g., endless time to repent, unlimited chances) that needs correction? What will you do today to act on the finite time you have?
?
If every existing number is finite, then your life has a finite number of days. Does this truth make you feel anxious or empowered? How does it affect the way you will spend the next 24 hours? Commit to one concrete action.

Analogy from Individuals to Group in Tawatur
Razi presents an analogical argument that permissibility of lying on individuals implies permissibility on the group.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the analogical argument used to deny that tawatur yields certainty, and why it does not prove impossibility of tawatur.

Core Idea
An analogy from individuals to group does not remove the possibility of lying from collective reports.

Imam Razi here presents one of the arguments of those who reject tawatur as a source of certainty. They employ analogical reasoning to show that if lying is possible for each individual reporter, it remains possible for the entire group. This passage examines that inference and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: first a general principle, then an example, then the application to reporters.
The passage uses two analogies: one from events and one from the Zanj people.
2
Identify the analogical structure
The argument relies on the idea that what holds for each individual also holds for the whole group. Find the two analogies and see how they lead to the conclusion about reporters.
3
Understand the purpose of the argument
This argument is used by those who claim that tawatur cannot produce certainty. Reflect on why the possibility of lying for each reporter would extend to the group.
The conclusion is that the collective report still carries the possibility of lying, so it does not yield certainty.
4
Recognize the limitation
Note that this argument does not prove that tawatur is impossible, only that it does not produce certainty. This is a common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أن المتكلمين يقولون لما كان كل واحد من الحوادث له أول وجب الكل أن يكون له أول وكل العقلاء يقولون : إذا كان كل واحد من الزنج موصوفا بالسواد، وجب في الكل أن يكونوا موصوفين بالسواد فكذا ههنا ؛ لما جاز الكذب على كل واحد من المخبرين وجب أن يكون هذا الجواز باقيا في حق الكل.
The second [point]: The mutakallimun say: since each one of the events has a beginning, it is necessary that the whole has a beginning; and all rational people say: if each one of the Zanj is described with blackness, it is necessary that all of them are described with blackness. So likewise here: since lying is possible for each one of the reporters, it is necessary that this possibility remains in respect of the whole.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage presents the second point of the first maqam, an argument used by those who reject tawatur entirely. It uses analogical reasoning (qiyas): just as in the case of contingent events each has a beginning and therefore the totality must have a beginning, and just as in the case of blackness each individual Zanj is black and therefore the whole group is black, so in the case of reports: since lying is possible for each individual reporter, that possibility must extend to the entire group. Therefore, tawatur does not produce certainty because the possibility of lying applies collectively.

Key Terms
جواز الكذب
المخبرين
الكل
قياس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument proves that tawatur is impossible.
Correction: The argument only shows that tawatur does not yield certainty; it does not prove the impossibility of tawatur.

Checkpoint
According to the analogy in this passage, what is the relationship between permissibility of lying on individuals and the group?
A) It becomes impossible in the group.
B) It remains possible in the group.
C) It is unknown in the group.
D) It becomes certain that they did not lie.

Key Takeaway
Analogical reasoning from individuals to group does not eliminate the possibility of collective lying, so tawatur alone does not produce certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a piece of information you firmly believe because many people have told you. After reading this lesson, can you honestly say you have ever considered the possibility that all those people could be mistaken or lying? If not, what does that mean for your certainty? Will you examine your sources more critically today?
?
This argument shows that even a multitude can, in principle, be wrong. When it comes to your faith – which is built on reports from early Muslims – have you ever felt uneasy that this same possibility might apply to the transmission of religious knowledge? How do you reconcile that possibility with your conviction? What step can you take today to strengthen or re-evaluate the basis of your belief?

The Third Sophism: Burden of Proof in Tawatur
Razi's third sophism demanding a specific number and proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's third sophism and why the proponent bears the burden of proof for a specific number that prevents lying in tawatur.

Core Idea
The proponent must pinpoint the exact number that prevents lying—and prove it.

In the third sophism, Razi challenges the proponent of tawatur to move beyond general claims about large numbers. Since lying is possible for each individual separately, the proponent must identify a specific threshold where collective lying becomes impossible. This lesson explores Razi's argument that the burden of proof lies squarely on the one making the claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the sophism's structure. Notice how Razi builds the argument with two premises.
This is a demand for evidence, not an outright rejection.
2
Identify the two premises
The sophism builds on: (1) lying is possible for each individual alone, and (2) the mere assembly of 100 or 200 does not prevent lying.
3
Understand the burden shift
Razi argues that the proponent must specify a concrete number and prove its preventing effect. The objector only needs to demand proof, not provide it.
4
Relate to epistemological debates
Consider how this demands precision in probabilistic arguments for tawatur. The sophism does not deny tawatur but questions the sufficiency of vague large numbers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثالثة : أنه لما ثبت أن الكذب جائز على كل واحد منهم حال الانفراد، وثبت أن الاجتماع في المائة والمائتين غير واقع لذلك الخبر - وأنتم المستدلون ـ فعليكم أن تذكروا واحدًا معينا، وتذكروا أن انتهاء الاجتماع إلى ذلك الحد المعين مانع من الكذب لكنكم أنتم مطالبون بإقامة البرهان على ذلك، وأما نحن فتتكفينا المطالبة؛ لأنا نحن السائلون، وأنتم المستدلون.
The third sophism: Since it is established that lying is possible for each one of them individually, and it is established that the coming together in the hundred and two hundred is not real for that report - and you are the proponents - it is upon you to mention a specific [number], and to mention that the reaching of the gathering to that specific limit is a preventer of lying. But you are required to provide the proof of that. As for us, we are satisfied with the demand, because we are the questioners and you are the proponents.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This is the third sophism. It states that since lying is possible for each individual separately, and the fact that the group size reaches 100 or 200 does not by itself prevent lying (as the proponent claims), the proponent must specify a particular number and demonstrate that this number is a preventing factor. The objector is satisfied with simply demanding proof, because they are the questioner and the proponent is the one who must provide evidence.

Key Terms
الانفراد
الاجتماع
المانع
البرهان
السائل
المستدل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Razi's third sophism denies the possibility of tawatur.
Correction: Razi only challenges the proponent to specify and prove a preventing number; he does not affirm impossibility.

Checkpoint
What does this sophism demand from the proponent of tawatur?
A) Accept that tawatur is false.
B) Specify a number that prevents lying and prove it.
C) Admit that all reporters are truthful.
D) Provide a mathematical formula.

Key Takeaway
Razi's third sophism places the burden on the proponent to specify and prove a precise numerical threshold that prevents lying in tawatur.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you demanded others to provide airtight proof for a belief you doubted, while refusing to examine the evidence yourself? What would it take for you to accept tawatur as a valid epistemic source?
?
If you truly believed that truth requires a demonstrated threshold, would you be willing to apply that same standard to your own most deeply held convictions? What would that expose?
?
The objector in this sophism is content with merely demanding proof. In your own spiritual journey, have you ever used the excuse 'the proof isn't strong enough' as a shield to avoid submitting to the truth? How would your life change if you stopped demanding and started seeking?

The First Sophism: Unknown Number of Intermediate Generations
Razi's first sophism: unknown number of intermediate generations prevents certainty from tawatur.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi argues that uncertainty about the number of intermediate generations undermines the certainty of tawatur reports.

Core Idea
If the number of intermediate generations is unknown, their attributes cannot be known, breaking the chain of certainty.

Razi presents a sophisticated argument against the certainty of tawatur-based knowledge of prophethood. He starts with a general epistemological principle: knowing an attribute requires knowing the thing itself. Then he applies it to the generations transmitting reports about the Prophet. This lesson unpacks that logical chain.

Guided Path
1
Understand the key premise
Read the first sentence: 'Knowledge of an attribute of a thing is conditioned upon knowledge of that thing.' Why is this necessary? Reflect on its implications for any knowledge claim.
This premise is described as certain (yaqiniyya).
2
Apply to intermediate generations
Razi applies the premise to the generations between us and the eyewitnesses. To know they have the attributes of tawatur, we must know the very thing (their essence, i.e., their number and existence).
The ‘thing’ here refers to the entire set of intermediate generations.
3
Draw the conclusion
Since we do not know the number of intermediate generations, their essences are unknown, and thus their attributes are unknown. Therefore, we cannot claim certainty from this tawatur report.
This is the core of the sophism – it targets the possibility of certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الأولى : أن العلم بصفة الشيء مشروط بالعلم بذلك الشيء، وهذه المقدمة يقينية. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : ما لم نعلم أن جميع الطبقات التي بيننا وبين الطبقة الذين شاهدوا محمدا كانوا موصوفين بالصفات المعتبرة في كون التواتر مفيدًا للعلم، لم يمكنا القطع بصحة هذا التواتر، لكن العلم بكون هذه الطبقات المتوسطة موصوفين بصفات التواتر علم بصفة من صفاتهم، وقد ثبت ان العلم بصفة الشيء مشروط بالعلم بذاته، فإذن لا يمكننا أن نعلم أن هذه الطبقات موصوفة بهذه الصفات، إلا إذا علمنا عدد تلك الطبقات ووجودهم، لكننا لا نعلم البتة من الطبقات المتوسطة أنها كم هي ؟ وإذا كان كذلك كانت ذواتها مجهولة لنا، وإذا كانت الذوات مجهولة كانت الصفات مجهولة، فلم يكن العلم بكونهم موصوفين بالصفات المعتبرة في التواتر حاصلًا، فوجب أن لا يحصل بهذا الخبر علم، والله أعلم.
The first sophism: Knowledge of an attribute of a thing is conditioned upon knowledge of that thing, and this premise is certain. If you understand this, we say: As long as we do not know that all the generations between us and the generation who witnessed Muhammad were described with the attributes considered in making tawatur productive of knowledge, we cannot be certain of the validity of this tawatur. But knowledge that these intermediate generations are described with the attributes of tawatur is knowledge of an attribute of theirs, and it has been established that knowledge of an attribute of a thing is conditioned upon knowledge of its essence. Therefore, we cannot know that these generations are described with these attributes unless we know the number of those generations and their existence. Yet we do not know at all the number of the intermediate generations - how many they are? If that is so, their essences are unknown to us, and if the essences are unknown, the attributes are unknown. So knowledge that they are described with the attributes considered in tawatur is not obtained. Hence, no knowledge is obtained through this report. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This is the first sophism of the second maqam, targeting the second type of tawatur used to establish prophethood. The argument: knowledge of a thing's attribute is conditioned upon knowledge of the thing itself. To know that the intermediate generations between us and the eyewitnesses of Prophet Muhammad possess the attributes that make tawatur productive of knowledge, we must know their existence and number. But we do not know the number of those intermediate generations. Hence, their essences (dhawat) are unknown to us, and consequently their attributes are unknown. Consequently, we cannot have knowledge through this tawatur report.

Key Terms
العلم بصفة الشيء
العلم بذات الشيء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument denies all historical knowledge.
Correction: It only targets certainty through tawatur, not all historical knowledge. Razi is pointing out a specific logical flaw in claiming certainty from uninterrupted transmission.

Checkpoint
According to this sophism, why can we not be certain of the tawatur report about Prophet Muhammad?
A) Because the reporters might be unknown.
B) Because we don't know the number of intermediate generations.
C) Because the report is not from reliable sources.
D) Because the report is about miracles.

Key Takeaway
The certainty of tawatur reports depends on knowing the number and attributes of intermediate generations, which Razi argues is unknowable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi argues that we cannot be certain of the tawatur report because we don't know the number of intermediate generations. When you recite the Quran or repeat a hadith, have you ever paused to ask yourself: 'How do I know this chain is unbroken?' What would you do if you found a gap in your knowledge?
?
If a logical doubt like this entered your heart, would you be able to resolve it, or would you simply ignore it? How does your reliance on the transmission of Islam affect your daily worship and trust in Allah? Are you willing to investigate until certainty is restored?
?
The principle that knowledge of an attribute requires knowledge of the essence seems obvious, yet do you apply this to your own pursuit of Islamic knowledge? Do you seek to know the 'essence' (the sources, the chains, the proofs) or are you content with surface-level attributes? What concrete step will you take today to verify one piece of religious knowledge you currently hold?

Objections Against Tawatur: The Skeptic's Challenge
Razi enumerates objections against tawatur with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key objections raised against the reliability of tawatur, including the problem of rumor-mongering and historical gaps.

Core Idea
Skeptics argue that tawatur is unreliable because widespread rumors can be false and great events remain unknown.

Imam Razi begins by presenting the arguments of those who dispute the epistemic value of tawatur. They point to observed cases of rumor-mongering and historical lacunae to undermine the certainty often attributed to widely transmitted reports. This lesson introduces these objections as a prelude to their refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic passage covering the objections. Note how the objectors use contemporary examples of false rumors and historical gaps to argue against tawatur.
2
Identify the three examples
The objectors give three examples: rumor-mongering in our time, the lack of tawatur in details of prayer, and the loss of histories of ancient nations. Find these in the text.
3
Understand the underlying principle
The objectors claim that great events' news must reach us, but they provide counterexamples where such news did not reach us. Reflect on how this challenges the reliability of tawatur.
This principle is later refuted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونحن نشاهد أن مثل هذا الإرجاف كثيرًا ما يحصل في زماننا، فإذا جاز ذلك في هذا الزمان منه ونحن نشاهده فكذا في سائر الأزمنة. وكذبًا. لا يقال : كل ما كان كذلك فإنه لا بد أن ينكشف بالآخرة أنه كان إرجافا لأنا نقول : لم قلتم إنه لو كان الأمر واقعا على هذا الوجه لوجب أن يصل خبره إلينا؟ أقصى ما في الباب أن يقال: إنه واقعة عظيمة والوقائع العظيمة يجب أن تصل إلينا أخبارها. إلا أنا نقول : هذه القاعدة منقوضة بصور منها : أن رسول الله داوم على أداء الصلوات نيفا وعشرين سنة، ثم إنه لم ينقل إلينا شيء من أحوال الصلاة، إلا برواية الآحاد ومع التعارض، ولهذا السبب اختلف المجتهدون في كيفية أداء الصلاة اختلافا شديدا لا يرجى زواله. ومنها : أن أحوال عاد و ثمود وسائر الأمم وسائر أرباب الدول والملل كانت أمورًا عظيمة، ثم إنها خفيت وما وصلت إلينا أقصى ما في الباب أن يقال : طالت المدة بيننا وبينهم ، فخفيت إلا أن هذا إنما ينفع إذا ذكرتم في ضبط المدة الطويلة، والمدة القريبة ضابطًا معلوما، حتى إن ما كان قريبا نحكم عليه بأنه لا بد أن يكون معلوما لنا ، وما كان بعيدا لم يجب فيه ذلك، إلا أن ذكر هذا الضابط كالمتعذر والممتنع. فهذه جملة الوجوه التي تمسك بها الطاعنون في التواتر. والجواب : اعلم أن العلماء اختلفوا في أن العلم الحاصل عقيب خبر التواتر علم ضروري أم نظري؟ والاختيار أنه ضروري، وذلك لأن الضروري هو الذي إذا شكك فيه صاحبه لا يتشكك، ونحن نجرب أنفسنا عند سماع هذه الشبهات التي ذكرتموها فنجدها جازمة بأن محمدا لو كان موجودًا، وكان يدعي أنه رسول الله إلى الخلق، ولا نجد هذه الشبهات التي ذكرتموها طاعنة في ذلك الجزم وقادحة فيه، فثبت أن العلم الحاصل عقيب خبر التواتر علم ضروري فكانت هذه الشبهات طاعنة في الضروريات، فلم تكن مستحقة للجواب ؛ لأن ذلك الجواب لا محالة يكون نظريًا، فيتوقف الضروري عليه فيقع الدور، وهو محال، فعلمنا أن أمثال هذه الشبهات لا تستحق الجواب
And we observe that such rumor-mongering often occurs in our time, so if that is possible in this time from him and we observe it, then likewise in all other times. And falsehood. It is not said: everything that is like that must eventually be revealed to have been rumor-mongering, for we say: why did you claim that if the matter was actually in this way, its news must necessarily reach us? The most that can be said is that it is a great event, and great events' news must reach us. But we say: this principle is refuted by examples, among them: that the Messenger of God continued performing prayers for over twenty years, yet nothing of the conditions of prayer was transmitted to us except through ahad (single) narrations and with contradiction, and for this reason the mujtahids differed severely in the manner of performing prayer, in a way that its removal is not hoped. And among them: that the conditions of 'Ad and Thamud and other nations and all the rulers of states and religions were great matters, yet they were concealed and did not reach us; the most that can be said is: the period between us and them became long, so they were concealed, but this only benefits if you mention a known criterion for distinguishing the long period from the short period, so that what is near we judge that it must be known to us, and what is distant it is not necessary for it to be known. But mentioning such a criterion is nearly impossible and unattainable. So these are the sum of the arguments held by the critics of tawatur. And the answer: Know that the scholars differed concerning the knowledge obtained from the report of tawatur: is it necessary (daruri) or theoretical (nazari)? The chosen view is that it is necessary, because the necessary is that which, when its possessor doubts it, he does not remain in doubt. And we test ourselves when hearing these doubts you mentioned and find ourselves certain that Muhammad existed and claimed to be the Messenger of God to creation, and we do not find these doubts you mentioned as attacking that certainty and damaging it. So it is established that the knowledge obtained from the report of tawatur is necessary knowledge. So these doubts are attacks on necessary matters, so they are not worthy of an answer; because that answer would necessarily be theoretical, so the necessary would depend on it, resulting in circularity (dawr), which is impossible. So we know that such doubts are not worthy of an answer.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This is an objection-and-answer passage. The objectors raise several arguments against the reliability of tawatur (mutual corroboration), claiming that rumors can be widespread yet false, and that many great events in history are unknown to us. They cite examples like the details of the Prophet's prayers being transmitted only through ahad reports, and the histories of ancient nations being lost. The answer refutes these by affirming that the knowledge from tawatur is necessary (daruri). Necessary knowledge is so certain that doubting it is impossible; one finds oneself certain despite hearing objections. Since the doubts attack necessary knowledge, responding to them with theoretical arguments would create a circular dependency (necessary relying on theoretical), which is impossible. Therefore, such doubts do not deserve a response.

Key Terms
تواتر (tawatur)
إرجاف (rumor-mongering)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That tawatur is always indubitable and no counterexamples exist.
Correction: The objections show that there are apparent counterexamples where widespread reports are false or great events are unknown, but the refutation will address these.

Checkpoint
Which example do the objectors give to challenge the principle that great events' news must reach us?
A) The details of the Prophet's prayer
B) The story of Adam
C) The miracles of Moses
D) The conquest of Mecca

Key Takeaway
Skeptics challenge tawatur by pointing to rumor-mongering and historical gaps, arguing that even great events can be unknown or misreported.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a widely repeated report about Islam, do you accept it without verifying? How does the existence of rumor-mongering change your responsibility to seek knowledge from reliable sources?
?
The skeptics argue that even great events can be unknown. In your own life, are there important truths about Islam that you have neglected to learn because you assumed they would be well-known? What are you doing to ensure you are not relying on assumptions rather than verified knowledge?
?
Al-Razi presents the objection that the details of the Prophet's prayer were not transmitted via tawatur, leading to disagreement among scholars. Do you feel confident that you have learned the prayer correctly from trustworthy sources, or have you relied on hearsay? How can you take immediate steps to confirm your knowledge?

Refuting the Objections: The Necessity of Tawatur Knowledge
Razi refutes objections by asserting tawatur yields necessary knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes objections against tawatur by affirming that the knowledge it produces is necessary (daruri) and thus immune to theoretical doubt.

Core Idea
Tawatur yields necessary knowledge so certain that doubting it leads to circularity.

After presenting the objections, Imam Razi offers a decisive refutation. He declares that the knowledge obtained from tawatur is necessary, meaning it cannot be genuinely doubted. Any attempt to refute it using theoretical arguments would cause a logical circularity because the necessary would depend on the theoretical. Thus, such objections do not deserve a response.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'والجواب'. Notice the distinction between necessary (daruri) and theoretical (nazari) knowledge.
2
Understand the concept of necessary knowledge
Razi defines necessary knowledge as that which cannot be doubted when one introspects. Reflect on how tawatur produces such certainty.
3
Recognize the circularity argument
Razi argues that if the objections attack necessary knowledge, answering them would require theoretical reasoning, making the necessary depend on the theoretical, which is impossible. This is the key refutation.
4
Apply the principle
Consider how this defense protects tawatur from skeptical challenges. Why is it not a subjective claim?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونحن نشاهد أن مثل هذا الإرجاف كثيرًا ما يحصل في زماننا، فإذا جاز ذلك في هذا الزمان منه ونحن نشاهده فكذا في سائر الأزمنة. وكذبًا. لا يقال : كل ما كان كذلك فإنه لا بد أن ينكشف بالآخرة أنه كان إرجافا لأنا نقول : لم قلتم إنه لو كان الأمر واقعا على هذا الوجه لوجب أن يصل خبره إلينا؟ أقصى ما في الباب أن يقال: إنه واقعة عظيمة والوقائع العظيمة يجب أن تصل إلينا أخبارها. إلا أنا نقول : هذه القاعدة منقوضة بصور منها : أن رسول الله داوم على أداء الصلوات نيفا وعشرين سنة، ثم إنه لم ينقل إلينا شيء من أحوال الصلاة، إلا برواية الآحاد ومع التعارض، ولهذا السبب اختلف المجتهدون في كيفية أداء الصلاة اختلافا شديدا لا يرجى زواله. ومنها : أن أحوال عاد و ثمود وسائر الأمم وسائر أرباب الدول والملل كانت أمورًا عظيمة، ثم إنها خفيت وما وصلت إلينا أقصى ما في الباب أن يقال : طالت المدة بيننا وبينهم ، فخفيت إلا أن هذا إنما ينفع إذا ذكرتم في ضبط المدة الطويلة، والمدة القريبة ضابطًا معلوما، حتى إن ما كان قريبا نحكم عليه بأنه لا بد أن يكون معلوما لنا ، وما كان بعيدا لم يجب فيه ذلك، إلا أن ذكر هذا الضابط كالمتعذر والممتنع. فهذه جملة الوجوه التي تمسك بها الطاعنون في التواتر. والجواب : اعلم أن العلماء اختلفوا في أن العلم الحاصل عقيب خبر التواتر علم ضروري أم نظري؟ والاختيار أنه ضروري، وذلك لأن الضروري هو الذي إذا شكك فيه صاحبه لا يتشكك، ونحن نجرب أنفسنا عند سماع هذه الشبهات التي ذكرتموها فنجدها جازمة بأن محمدا لو كان موجودًا، وكان يدعي أنه رسول الله إلى الخلق، ولا نجد هذه الشبهات التي ذكرتموها طاعنة في ذلك الجزم وقادحة فيه، فثبت أن العلم الحاصل عقيب خبر التواتر علم ضروري فكانت هذه الشبهات طاعنة في الضروريات، فلم تكن مستحقة للجواب ؛ لأن ذلك الجواب لا محالة يكون نظريًا، فيتوقف الضروري عليه فيقع الدور، وهو محال، فعلمنا أن أمثال هذه الشبهات لا تستحق الجواب
And we observe that such rumor-mongering often occurs in our time, so if that is possible in this time from him and we observe it, then likewise in all other times. And falsehood. It is not said: everything that is like that must eventually be revealed to have been rumor-mongering, for we say: why did you claim that if the matter was actually in this way, its news must necessarily reach us? The most that can be said is that it is a great event, and great events' news must reach us. But we say: this principle is refuted by examples, among them: that the Messenger of God continued performing prayers for over twenty years, yet nothing of the conditions of prayer was transmitted to us except through ahad (single) narrations and with contradiction, and for this reason the mujtahids differed severely in the manner of performing prayer, in a way that its removal is not hoped. And among them: that the conditions of 'Ad and Thamud and other nations and all the rulers of states and religions were great matters, yet they were concealed and did not reach us; the most that can be said is: the period between us and them became long, so they were concealed, but this only benefits if you mention a known criterion for distinguishing the long period from the short period, so that what is near we judge that it must be known to us, and what is distant it is not necessary for it to be known. But mentioning such a criterion is nearly impossible and unattainable. So these are the sum of the arguments held by the critics of tawatur. And the answer: Know that the scholars differed concerning the knowledge obtained from the report of tawatur: is it necessary (daruri) or theoretical (nazari)? The chosen view is that it is necessary, because the necessary is that which, when its possessor doubts it, he does not remain in doubt. And we test ourselves when hearing these doubts you mentioned and find ourselves certain that Muhammad existed and claimed to be the Messenger of God to creation, and we do not find these doubts you mentioned as attacking that certainty and damaging it. So it is established that the knowledge obtained from the report of tawatur is necessary knowledge. So these doubts are attacks on necessary matters, so they are not worthy of an answer; because that answer would necessarily be theoretical, so the necessary would depend on it, resulting in circularity (dawr), which is impossible. So we know that such doubts are not worthy of an answer.

Commentary
This is an objection-and-answer passage. The objectors raise several arguments against the reliability of tawatur (mutual corroboration), claiming that rumors can be widespread yet false, and that many great events in history are unknown to us. They cite examples like the details of the Prophet's prayers being transmitted only through ahad reports, and the histories of ancient nations being lost. The answer refutes these by affirming that the knowledge from tawatur is necessary (daruri). Necessary knowledge is so certain that doubting it is impossible; one finds oneself certain despite hearing objections. Since the doubts attack necessary knowledge, responding to them with theoretical arguments would create a circular dependency (necessary relying on theoretical), which is impossible. Therefore, such doubts do not deserve a response.

Key Terms
علم ضروري (daruri knowledge)
علم نظري (nazari knowledge)
دور (circularity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That tawatur knowledge is subjective or can be refuted by rational arguments.
Correction: Razi argues that tawatur knowledge is objectively necessary and cannot be genuinely doubted; thus rational objections are irrelevant.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, why is the knowledge from tawatur considered necessary?
A) Because it is based on many witnesses
B) Because one cannot genuinely doubt it when introspecting
C) Because it is taught by scholars
D) Because it is mentioned in the Quran

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi refutes objections against tawatur by showing that its knowledge is necessary, and responding to doubts would lead to circularity, so such doubts are unworthy of reply.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter objections to your belief in Muhammad's prophethood, do you feel a deep, unshakable certainty (daruri) as Razi describes, or do you find yourself unsettled? What does your reaction reveal about the strength of your conviction, and what will you do to strengthen it from today?
?
Razi argues that necessary knowledge is enough and that responding to every doubt would lead to circularity. Is there an area of your faith where you are overly reliant on intellectual arguments for things that should be immediately certain? Are you willing to trust that necessary knowledge is sufficient, or are you holding onto doubts that need to be surrendered?
?
The test of necessary knowledge is whether it remains firm when doubted. Right now, can you honestly say that your belief in the core historical facts of Islam (e.g., the prophethood of Muhammad) is such that no intellectual objection can shake it? If not, what is the root cause, and what concrete steps will you take to cultivate that certainty?

The First Aspect: God's Actions Are Not for Purposes
First aspect: God's actions cannot be purposive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first part of the third objection: that the Ash'arī principle that God's actions are not based on purposes undermines the argument that miracles confirm prophethood.

Core Idea
If God's actions have no purposes, miracles cannot be for confirmation.

Imam al-Razi now presents the third objection against the miracle argument. He grants that miracles are from God but challenges the claim that they serve the purpose of confirming the prophet. The first aspect draws on the Ash'arī doctrine that God's actions are not motivated by any aims or purposes, which would then make it impossible to argue that miracles were created specifically for confirmation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the first part of the objection carefully. Notice that it begins with 'We grant' (سلمنا) and then raises the key challenge.
The word 'سلمنا' indicates a concession for the sake of argument.
2
Understand the Ash'arī principle
Identify the claim that the opponent has established decisive proofs (الدلائل القاهرة) that God's actions cannot be based on purposes. This is a core Ash'arī position.
Reflect on why this principle is important in Islamic theology.
3
Apply the principle to miracles
See how the objector concludes: if God's actions cannot have purposes, then miracles cannot be said to be created for the purpose of confirmation. This directly attacks the miracle argument.
This step shows the logical implication of the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاعتراض الثالث : سلمنا ان فاعلها هو الله تعالى ؛ فلم قلتم إنه تعالى فعلها لغرض التصديق ؟ وتقريره من وجهين : الأول : أنكم أقمتم الدلائل القاهرة على أنه يمتنع أن تكون أفعال الله تعالى وأحكامه معللة بشيء من الأغراض والمقاصد، وإذا كان كذلك امتنع القول بأنه تعالى إنما خلق هذه المعجزات لأجل غرض التصديق. الوجه الثاني : هو أنا نقول : الفعل إما أن يكون موقوفا على الداعي وإما أن لا يكون، فإن كان موقوفا على الداعي كان الجبر لازما ـ على ما قررناه في مسألة خلق الأفعال - وإذا لزم الجبر كان الباري تعالى هو الخالق للكفر والمعاصي؛ لأنه هو الذي فعل القدرة والداعية اللتين مجموعهما يوجب هذه القبائح وإذا كان الأمر كذلك لم يكن الإضلال ممتنعا في حق الله تعالى، وإذا كان كذلك فلم لا يجوز أن يقال: إنه تعالى أظهر هذه المعجزات على يد هذا الكاذب لأجل الإضلال؟ وأما إذا قلنا إن الفعل غير موقوف على الداعي فحينئذ لا يبعد أن يقال : أنه تعالى خلق هذه المعجزة لا لغرض البتة، وحينئذ لا يمكن الاستدلال بحق المعجزة على التصديق.
The third objection: We grant that its agent is God Almighty; but why do you say that He acted for the purpose of confirmation? Its explanation is from two aspects: First: You have established decisive proofs that it is impossible for God's actions and judgments to be based on any purposes and aims. If this is so, then it is impossible to say that God created these miracles for the purpose of confirmation. The second aspect: we say: an action either depends on a motive (al-dāʿī) or does not. If it depends on the motive, then compulsion (al-jabr) is necessary—as we have established in the issue of the creation of acts—and if compulsion is necessary, then the Creator is the creator of unbelief and sins because it is He who created the ability and the motive, the combination of which necessitates these vile acts. If this is so, then misguidance is not impossible in God's right. And if this is so, then why is it not permissible to say that God manifested these miracles at the hand of this liar in order to misguide? But if we say that the action does not depend on the motive, then it is not far-fetched to say that God created this miracle without any purpose at all, and then it is not possible to infer by the right of the miracle to confirmation.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection to the argument that miracles prove prophethood. It grants that miracles are from God but challenges the claim that they are performed for the purpose of confirming the prophet. The objection is explained in two aspects. First: The Ashʿarī school holds that God's actions are not based on any purposes or aims; therefore, one cannot say that God created miracles with the purpose of confirmation. Second: The objection explores the implications of determinism. If actions depend on a motive, then God is the creator of all actions, including disbelief, and could also create miracles to mislead. If actions do not depend on a motive, then miracles can occur without any purpose, and thus cannot serve as evidence for truthfulness.

Key Terms
الاعتراض الثالث
سلمنا
التصديق
الدلائل القاهرة
الأغراض والمقاصد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think this objection affirms that God acts without purpose as a settled truth, but it is presented as a hypothetical based on the opponent's own doctrine.
Correction: The objection is using the opponent's own premises to show inconsistency; it does not necessarily endorse the principle itself.

Checkpoint
What is the first aspect of the third objection based on?
A) The necessity of miracles for confirmation.
B) The principle that God's actions cannot have purposes.
C) The existence of decisive proofs for prophethood.
D) The role of motive in human actions.

Key Takeaway
The first aspect argues: if God's actions have no purposes, then miracles cannot be for confirmation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial, do you find yourself demanding to know the purpose behind it, or can you submit to God's will even when no purpose is apparent? How does this lesson's assertion that God's actions are not based on purposes challenge your need for explanations?
?
The objector uses the idea that God acts without purpose to deny that miracles confirm prophets. Have you ever used a theological concept to avoid a spiritual responsibility? What would it mean to accept that God's actions—including sending prophets—are not for any purpose you can define, yet still respond with full faith?
?
If you truly believed that nothing in your life happens for a purpose you can grasp, would your relationship with God shift from seeking understanding to pure submission? Are you willing to trust Him without knowing the 'why'?

The Second Aspect: The Dilemma of Motive
Second aspect: the dilemma of motive and its implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second aspect of the third objection: the dilemma concerning whether actions depend on a motive, and how each alternative undermines the miracle argument.

Core Idea
Whether actions depend on a motive or not, miracles cannot prove truthfulness.

The second aspect of the objection presents a powerful dilemma. It examines whether an action depends on a motive (al-dāʿī) or not. If it does, determinism follows, making God the creator of all actions, including disbelief, so He could create miracles to misguide. If not, miracles can occur without any purpose, and thus cannot serve as evidence. This dilemma cuts both ways and is designed to show the inconsistency in the opponent's reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dilemma
Read the passage and notice the two alternatives: action depends on motive, or it does not. This is the central division.
The word 'إما... وإما' signals a dichotomy.
2
Explore the first horn: motive leads to determinism
If action depends on motive, then God creates both ability and motive, making Him responsible for all acts, including disbelief. Then He could create miracles to mislead.
This horn relies on the deterministic view (al-jabr) discussed earlier in the context of creation of acts.
3
Explore the second horn: no motive means no purpose
If action does not depend on motive, then God can create miracles without any purpose, so they cannot be used as evidence for confirmation.
This horn shows that even if free from determinism, the miracle argument fails.
4
Synthesize the overall impact
Realize that both horns lead to the same conclusion: miracles cannot reliably confirm prophethood. This is a powerful objection.
The dilemma is a classic tool in kalām to force an opponent into a difficult position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاعتراض الثالث : سلمنا ان فاعلها هو الله تعالى ؛ فلم قلتم إنه تعالى فعلها لغرض التصديق ؟ وتقريره من وجهين : الأول : أنكم أقمتم الدلائل القاهرة على أنه يمتنع أن تكون أفعال الله تعالى وأحكامه معللة بشيء من الأغراض والمقاصد، وإذا كان كذلك امتنع القول بأنه تعالى إنما خلق هذه المعجزات لأجل غرض التصديق. الوجه الثاني : هو أنا نقول : الفعل إما أن يكون موقوفا على الداعي وإما أن لا يكون، فإن كان موقوفا على الداعي كان الجبر لازما ـ على ما قررناه في مسألة خلق الأفعال - وإذا لزم الجبر كان الباري تعالى هو الخالق للكفر والمعاصي؛ لأنه هو الذي فعل القدرة والداعية اللتين مجموعهما يوجب هذه القبائح وإذا كان الأمر كذلك لم يكن الإضلال ممتنعا في حق الله تعالى، وإذا كان كذلك فلم لا يجوز أن يقال: إنه تعالى أظهر هذه المعجزات على يد هذا الكاذب لأجل الإضلال؟ وأما إذا قلنا إن الفعل غير موقوف على الداعي فحينئذ لا يبعد أن يقال : أنه تعالى خلق هذه المعجزة لا لغرض البتة، وحينئذ لا يمكن الاستدلال بحق المعجزة على التصديق.
The third objection: We grant that its agent is God Almighty; but why do you say that He acted for the purpose of confirmation? Its explanation is from two aspects: First: You have established decisive proofs that it is impossible for God's actions and judgments to be based on any purposes and aims. If this is so, then it is impossible to say that God created these miracles for the purpose of confirmation. The second aspect: we say: an action either depends on a motive (al-dāʿī) or does not. If it depends on the motive, then compulsion (al-jabr) is necessary—as we have established in the issue of the creation of acts—and if compulsion is necessary, then the Creator is the creator of unbelief and sins because it is He who created the ability and the motive, the combination of which necessitates these vile acts. If this is so, then misguidance is not impossible in God's right. And if this is so, then why is it not permissible to say that God manifested these miracles at the hand of this liar in order to misguide? But if we say that the action does not depend on the motive, then it is not far-fetched to say that God created this miracle without any purpose at all, and then it is not possible to infer by the right of the miracle to confirmation.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection to the argument that miracles prove prophethood. It grants that miracles are from God but challenges the claim that they are performed for the purpose of confirming the prophet. The objection is explained in two aspects. First: The Ashʿarī school holds that God's actions are not based on any purposes or aims; therefore, one cannot say that God created miracles with the purpose of confirmation. Second: The objection explores the implications of determinism. If actions depend on a motive, then God is the creator of all actions, including disbelief, and could also create miracles to mislead. If actions do not depend on a motive, then miracles can occur without any purpose, and thus cannot serve as evidence for truthfulness.

Key Terms
الوجه الثاني
الداعي
الجبر
خلق الأفعال
الإضلال
الكفر
المعاصي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the objection affirms that God actually misguides people through miracles, but it is a hypothetical argument to show inconsistency.
Correction: The objection is not claiming that God does misguide; it is showing that the opponent's premises allow that possibility, which undermines the reliability of miracles.

Checkpoint
According to the second aspect, if an action depends on a motive, what does it imply about God's role?
A) God is not involved in human actions.
B) God creates both ability and motive, making Him responsible for all actions.
C) Miracles are always for confirmation.
D) Human free will is absolute.

Key Takeaway
The second aspect uses a dilemma to show that whether or not actions depend on a motive, miracles cannot be reliable evidence for prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If miracles could theoretically be used to misguide, on what basis do you trust that the miracles of the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) were from Allah? Examine your heart: is your faith built on rational proofs that could be doubted, or on a deeper certainty that Allah grants?
?
The dilemma suggests that without a motive, God's actions could be arbitrary. Does this thought disturb your trust in Allah's wisdom and purpose? How do you reconcile the possibility of arbitrary divine action with your belief in a wise and just Creator? What does your answer reveal about your reliance on human logic versus submission to divine will?

The Proof that Harder Acts Yield Greater Reward (Hadith and Qiyas)
Imam Razi presents a hadith and a qiyas argument for greater reward for harder acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the hadith and qiyas arguments that establish why harder acts of worship yield greater reward.

Core Idea
Harder worship brings greater reward; otherwise the hardship would be pointless.

This lesson examines a concise but powerful proof from Imam Razi. He combines a prophetic hadith with a rational analogy (qiyas) to demonstrate that the difficulty of an act of obedience is proportional to its reward. The argument hinges on the principle that God would not command pointless hardship.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the two parts: the hadith citation and the qiyas argument.
Notice that the qiyas argument is structured as a conditional.
2
Identify the hadith and its role
The passage begins with a hadith from Aisha: 'Your reward is according to your exertion.' This serves as a textual basis.
The hadith is a direct statement from the Prophet.
3
Understand the qiyas argument step by step
The qiyas argues: If easy and hard acts had equal reward, the extra hardship would be pointless harm, which God prohibits. Since He does not prohibit hard acts, the extra hardship must be compensated by greater reward.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Reflect on the underlying principle
The argument assumes that God does not command useless hardship. This principle is derived from divine wisdom and justice.
Think about how this principle applies to other aspects of worship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثالثة والثلاثون لعائشة : «أجرك على قدر نصبك». وأما القياس : فهو أن الطاعات السهلة والطاعات الشاقة لو اشتركا في قدر الثواب لكان تحمل ذلك القدر من المشقة الزائدة الخالية عن الفائدة ضررًا، وتحمل الضرر الخالي عن الفائدة محظور قطعا فكان يجب أن تحرم عليه تلك الطاعة الشاقة، ولما لم يكن كذلك علمنا أن الأشق أكثر ثوابًا.
The thirty-third issue. From Aisha: 'Your reward is according to your exertion.' And as for the analogy (qiyas): it is that if easy acts of obedience and difficult acts of obedience were equal in the amount of reward, then bearing that amount of additional hardship devoid of benefit would be harmful, and bearing harm devoid of benefit is absolutely prohibited, so it would be necessary that that difficult act of obedience be forbidden for him. And since it is not so, we know that the more difficult is greater in reward.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the principle that harder acts of obedience yield greater reward. It begins with a hadith from Aisha: 'Your reward is according to your exertion.' Then it provides a rational (qiyas) argument: If easy and difficult acts of obedience were equal in reward, then the additional hardship would be pointless harm, which is prohibited. Since difficult acts are not forbidden, we know they must have greater reward. The argument uses the principle that God does not command pointless hardship; therefore the hardship must be compensated by greater reward.

Key Terms
أجر
نصب
القياس
الطاعات السهلة
الطاعات الشاقة
الثواب
المشقة
الضرر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that the qiyas argument is about the necessity of reward, i.e., that God must reward.
Correction: The argument is not about compulsion on God, but about the wisdom and consistency of divine legislation: God would not command pointless hardship without compensation.

Checkpoint
According to the qiyas argument, why must harder acts of obedience have greater reward?
A) Because the hadith says so.
B) Because if they had equal reward, the hardship would be pointless and thus prohibited.
C) Because God loves hardship.
D) Because prophets said so.

Key Takeaway
The harder an act of obedience, the greater its reward, because God does not impose pointless hardship without just compensation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you choose the easier path in worship, do you truly believe that the harder path would bring no greater reward, or are you avoiding the truth of this hadith? What would change if you acted on the certainty that every hardship in obedience is matched by a greater reward from Allah?
?
Think of a specific act of worship you find difficult. What is the extra effort costing you? Now ask yourself: if you truly believed that the reward is 'on your exertion,' would you still hold back, or would you rush to earn that greater reward? What is stopping you from experiencing that reward today?
?
The qiyas argument shows that Allah does not command pointless hardship. Reflect on a time you resented a difficult obligation. How might that resentment have blinded you to the immense reward Allah was offering through that very hardship? Will you change your perspective starting from your next obligatory act?
