The Difficulty of Angelic Worship
Razi argues that angels' worship is harder than humans' and therefore their reward is greater.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that angels' worship is more difficult than humans' and why this implies their superiority.

Core Idea
The hardest worship proves the greatest reward.

The debate over whether angels or humans are superior involves multiple proofs. This lesson examines the third proof, which focuses on the difficulty of worship. Razi argues that despite having fewer afflictions and more blessings, angels persist in worship with extraordinary constancy, making their worship harder and thus their reward greater. We will explore the two main reasons he gives and consider the counter-argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the overall argument. Note that Razi uses a hadith and Quranic references as evidence.
Pay attention to the two 'wujuh' (reasons) he enumerates.
2
Identify the two reasons
Find where Razi says 'الوجه الأول' and 'الوجه الثاني'. What is the core idea of each reason? The first reason deals with safety from afflictions, the second with monotony of worship.
Each reason is a separate logical step.
3
Analyze the first reason
Consider why safety from afflictions and a pleasant environment make worship more difficult. Razi uses the ship verse to show human ingratitude, contrasting with angelic constancy.
He also mentions Adam to highlight human weakness.
4
Analyze the second reason
Understand how constant performance of a single act without boredom demonstrates difficulty. Razi compares it to the diversity in human worship and books.
The angels' continuous glorification is evidence.
5
Examine the counter-objection
The passage ends with an objection that human worship is actually harder. Note that Razi does not fully refute it here, but it sets up further discussion.
This shows the argument is not undisputed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة: لمن قال بتفضيل الملك على البشر : عبادات الملائكة أشق فيكون الملك أفضل إنما قلنا إنها أشق لوجوه : الأول : أنهم آمنون من الآفات التي يكون البشر خائفًا منها، مثل الغرق والحرق والقتل والمرض والحاجة والشقاوة والكفر والمعصية وأيضا فالسماوات التي هي مساكنهم وأماكنهم كالجنان والبساتين الطيبة بالنسبة إلى الأرض. وكل من كان تنعمه أكثر وخوفه أقل كان تمرده أشد ولهذا قال تعالى: ﴿فَإِذَا رَكِبُوا فِي الْفُلْكِ دَعوا اللهَ مُخْلِصِينَ لَهُ الدِّينَ فَلَمَّا نَجَّاهُمْ إِلَى الْبَرِّ إِذَا هُمْ يُشْرِكُونَ﴾ [العنكبوت: ٦٥]. ثم إن الملائكة مع كثرة أسباب التنعم والتمرد منذ خلقوا بقوا مشتغلين بالعبادة، خاشعين وجلين مشفقين لا يلتفتون إلى نعيم الجنان واللذات، بل بقوا مقبلين على الطاعات الشاقة، موصوفين بالفزع الشديد وكأنه لا يقدر أحد من بني آدم أن يبقى كذلك يوما واحدا، فضلا عن تلك الأعصار المتطاولة، ويؤكده قصة آدم فإنه أطلق له في جميع مواضع الجنة بقوله تعالى: ﴿وَكُلَا مِنْهَا رَغَدًا حَيْثُ شِئْتُمَا﴾ [البقرة : ٣٥] ومنعه من شجرة واحدة فلم يملك نفسه حتى وقع في الشر، وهذا يدل على أن طاعتهم أشق من طاعة البشر. الوجه الثاني : في بيان أن طاعتهم أشق : أن انتقال المكلفين من نوع عبادة إلى نوع آخر كالانتقال من بستان إلى بستان أما الإقامة على نوع واحد فإنها تورث الملالة، ولهذا السبب جعلت التصانيف مقسومة بالأبواب والفصول، وجعل كتاب الله مقسوما بالسور والأخماس والأعشار ، ثم إن الملائكة كل واحد منهم مواظب على عمل واحد لا يعدل عنه إلى غيره كما قال تعالى: يُسَبِّحُونَ اللَّيْلَ وَالنَّهَارَ لَا يَفْتُرُونَ [الأنبياء: ٢٠] وقال حكاية عنهم : وَإِنَّا لَنَحْنُ الصَّافُونَ وَإِنَّا لَنَحْنُ المُسَبِّحُونَ [الصافات: ١٦٥ - ١٦٦]. فثبت بما ذكرنا أن عبادتهم أشق، وإذا ثبت هذا وجب أن يكونوا أكثر ثوابًا لقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم : أفضل الأعمال أحمزها والاعتراض عليه معارض بما ذكرنا من أن عبادات البشر أشق فتكون أفضل.
The third proof: For those who say that angels are superior to humans: The worship of angels is more difficult, so the angel is better. We say it is more difficult for several reasons: First: They are safe from the afflictions that humans fear, such as drowning, burning, being killed, illness, need, misery, disbelief, and sin. Also, the heavens, which are their dwellings and places, are like gardens and pleasant orchards compared to the earth. And whoever has more enjoyment and less fear, his rebellion is more severe. That is why God said: 'When they board a ship, they call upon God, making pure for Him their religion, but when He delivers them to land, behold, they associate partners' [Al-Ankabut 29:65]. Then the angels, despite having many reasons for enjoyment and rebellion, since they were created, remained occupied with worship, humble, fearful, apprehensive, not turning to the bliss of gardens and pleasures, rather they remained devoted to difficult acts of obedience, described with intense terror; it is as if no human could remain like that for a single day, let alone those extended ages. This is corroborated by the story of Adam, for he was allowed in all places of paradise by God's statement: 'And eat from it freely wherever you wish' [Al-Baqarah 2:35], but He prohibited him from one tree, yet he could not control himself until he fell into sin. This shows that their obedience is more difficult than human obedience. The second point: In explaining that their obedience is more difficult: The transition of responsible beings from one type of worship to another is like moving from one garden to another, whereas remaining on one type causes boredom. For this reason, books are divided into chapters and sections, and the Book of God is divided into surahs, fifths, and tenths. Then the angels, each one of them is constant upon one single action, not turning away from it to another, as God said: 'They glorify night and day, never slackening' [Al-Anbiya 21:20], and He narrated from them: 'Indeed we are those who stand in rows, and indeed we are those who glorify' [As-Saffat 37:165-166]. So it is established by what we have mentioned that their worship is more difficult. And if this is established, then they must receive more reward, because of the Prophet's saying (peace be upon him): 'The best deeds are the most difficult.' And the objection to it is countered by what we have mentioned that the worship of humans is more difficult, so they are better.
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof that angels are superior to humans: their worship is harder, so they deserve more reward. The proof has two reasons. First, angels are safe from worldly afflictions (drowning, burning, etc.) and their environment is like gardens, so they have less fear and more enjoyment, which would normally lead to more rebellion, yet they persist in difficult worship. This is contrasted with humans who become ungrateful when safe (the ship verse). The story of Adam (who fell into sin despite minimal restriction) shows the difficulty of human obedience compared to angels. Second, monotony of worship: humans get bored of one type of worship, so God diversified religious obligations into chapters and verses, but angels each perform one specific act of worship continuously without tiring. This constancy under monotony proves greater difficulty. The conclusion: since their worship is harder, they get more reward based on the hadith that the best deeds are the most difficult. However, the argument is then countered by the objection that human worship is actually harder because humans face temptations and weakness, so humans may be better. The text ends with this objection, leaving it unresolved here.

Key Terms
آمنون من الآفات
التنعم
التمرد
الملالة
المواظبة
أفضل الأعمال أحمزها

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Because angels live in ease and are free from afflictions, their worship is easier than that of humans.
Correction: The argument turns this on its head: their ease and safety make their persistent worship more remarkable and thus more difficult. Humans, despite hardship, often falter; angels never do.

Checkpoint
According to the first reason, why is angelic worship more difficult than human worship?
A) Angels have more afflictions to overcome.
B) Angels remain devoted despite being safe from afflictions.
C) Humans worship more continuously than angels.
D) Angels rebel more than humans.

Key Takeaway
The difficulty of worship is not measured by external hardships alone, but by the constancy and devotion despite favorable conditions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you had angels' immunity from all suffering and infinite pleasure, would you still worship God with the same intensity? What does your answer reveal about the condition of your heart?
?
You change your acts of worship constantly to avoid boredom—reciting different surahs, varying prayers, switching between dhikr. But an angel has performed the same single act for millennia without complaint. When was the last time you persisted in a difficult act of worship until it became easy, rather than running from it?
?
Adam was allowed every blessing except one tree, and he failed. You have been given countless blessings and only a few prohibitions. Where in your life are you still grasping at that 'one tree'? What would it take for you to trust God's command over your own desire?

The First Proof: Emphasis Shows Probability
Razi's first argument: emphasis shows universality is probable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's first argument that if general terms were definitive, adding emphasis would be redundant, so their universality is only probable.

Core Idea
If general terms were certain, emphasis would be impossible — but it's allowed.

Razi begins with a logical argument: if the word 'all' already meant 'every single one definitively', then saying 'all, all of them' would be meaningless repetition. Since Arabic speakers naturally add emphasis to general terms, the original meaning must be probable, not certain.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic for the first proof and its translation. Note the logical structure.
2
Understand the logical principle
The key point is 'تحصيل الحاصل' (obtaining the obtained) — if something is already obtained, re-obtaining it is impossible.
3
Connect to the conclusion
Since emphasis is acceptable, the universality cannot be definitive; it must be probable.
This shows that general terms are not قطعي but ظني.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحدها: أن هذه الصيغ لو أفادت الاستغراق قطعًا لامتنع إدخال لفظ التأكيد عليها؛ لأن تحصيل الحاصل محال، ولما أجمعوا على أنه يحسن إدخال الألفاظ المؤكدة عليها علمنا إفادتها الاستغراق ظنية لا قطعية.
وثانيها: أن العلم بأن هذه الألفاظ موضوعة للاستغراق إما أن يحصل بخبر التواتر أو بخبر الآحاد، والأول باطل وإلا لما رفع الخلاف فيه، فيبقى الثاني، لكن خبر الآحاد لا يفيد إلا الظن.
وثالثها: أنا نرى أن الناس كثيرًا ما يعبرون عن الأكثر بلفظ الكل والجمع على سبيل المبالغة، قال تعالى: ﴿ وَأُوتِيَتْ مِن كُلِّ شَىءٍ ﴾ [النمل: ٢٣] ومعلوم أنها ما أوتيت من العرش والكرسي والجنة والنار، فعلمنا أن العادة العامة جارية بإطلاق لفظ الكل والجمع على الأكثر على وجه المبالغة، وإذا كان هذا أمرًا معتادًا علمنا أن دلالة هذه الألفاظ على الاستغراق دلالة ظنية محتملة لا قطعية.
إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إنه لا يجوز التمسك بهذه الألفاظ في هذه المسألة؛ لأن هذه المسألة قطعية والتمسك فيها بالدليل الظني باطل قطعا.
One of them: If these forms indicated universality definitively, then it would be impossible to insert a word of emphasis upon them, because obtaining the obtained is impossible. Since they agreed that it is good to insert emphatic words upon them, we know that their indication of universality is probable, not definitive. Second: The knowledge that these words are placed for universality is either obtained by mutawatir report or by ahad report. The first is false, otherwise disagreement about it would not have arisen, so the second remains. But ahad report only yields probability. Third: We see that people often express the majority by the word 'all' and 'plural' by way of exaggeration. God said: 'And she was given of everything' (27:23), and it is known that she was not given the Throne, the Kursi, Paradise, and Hell. So we know that the general custom is to use the word 'all' and 'plural' for the majority by way of exaggeration. Since this is customary, we know that the indication of these words for universality is probable and possible, not definitive. If this is established, we say: It is not permissible to rely on these words in this matter, because this matter is definitive and relying on probable evidence in it is definitively invalid.
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage presents three proofs that general terms (صيغ العموم) do not yield certainty but only probability. The author first states that if these terms were definitively universal, adding emphasis (تأكيد) would be impossible because that would be obtaining the already obtained. Since emphasis is accepted, the universality is only probable. Second, the knowledge of these terms being for universality comes either from tawatur (mass transmission) or ahad (single report); tawatur is false because disagreement persists, so it must be ahad, which only gives probability. Third, people commonly use 'all' for 'most' hyperbolically, as in the Quranic verse about Bilqis; thus the indication is probable. The conclusion: since this question is definitive (قطعية), relying on merely probable evidence is invalid.

Key Terms
صيغ العموم
الاستغراق
التأكيد
تحصيل الحاصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: General terms always give certain knowledge about every individual.
Correction: Razi shows that if they were certain, emphasis would be impossible, but emphasis is common, so they are only probable.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, why does the permissibility of adding emphasis to general terms prove they are not definitive?
A) Because emphasis changes the meaning.
B) Because if they were definitive, emphasis would be redundant (تحصيل الحاصل).
C) Because emphasis is only used for doubtful matters.
D) Because general terms cannot be emphasized.

Key Takeaway
The first proof shows that the ability to emphasize general terms implies their universality is probable, not certain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own understanding of religious texts, have you ever assumed a general statement is absolute when it might only be probable? How does Razi's argument challenge you to re-examine such assumptions?
?
What 'emphasis' in your life—repeated reminders or rituals—might be a sign that you are trying to convince yourself of something that is not certain? Will you honestly evaluate whether those emphases reveal weakness in your conviction?
?
Razi uses logic to question certainty. Can you identify a belief you hold that you treat as definitive, yet the evidence for it is only probable? How will you adjust the strength of your commitment to match the level of evidence?

The Second Proof: Transmission Type Limits Certainty
Razi's second argument: transmission type limits certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's second argument that our knowledge of general terms comes from ahad reports, which only yield probability.

Core Idea
Knowledge of general terms comes from ahad reports, which are only probable.

Razi's second argument shifts to the epistemology of language. He argues that our knowledge that words are placed for universality must come either from mass-transmitted reports (tawatur) or single reports (ahad). Since tawatur would end disagreement (but it hasn't), the knowledge must come from ahad, which only gives probability.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic for the second proof and its translation. Notice the logical bifurcation.
2
Understand the two sources
Razi presents two possibilities: mutawatir (mass) or ahad (single). He rejects the first because disagreement persists.
This shows that the knowledge is not certain.
3
Apply the conclusion
Since ahad reports give only probability, the universality indicated is also probable.
Thus general terms are not قطعي.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحدها: أن هذه الصيغ لو أفادت الاستغراق قطعًا لامتنع إدخال لفظ التأكيد عليها؛ لأن تحصيل الحاصل محال، ولما أجمعوا على أنه يحسن إدخال الألفاظ المؤكدة عليها علمنا إفادتها الاستغراق ظنية لا قطعية.
وثانيها: أن العلم بأن هذه الألفاظ موضوعة للاستغراق إما أن يحصل بخبر التواتر أو بخبر الآحاد، والأول باطل وإلا لما رفع الخلاف فيه، فيبقى الثاني، لكن خبر الآحاد لا يفيد إلا الظن.
وثالثها: أنا نرى أن الناس كثيرًا ما يعبرون عن الأكثر بلفظ الكل والجمع على سبيل المبالغة، قال تعالى: ﴿ وَأُوتِيَتْ مِن كُلِّ شَىءٍ ﴾ [النمل: ٢٣] ومعلوم أنها ما أوتيت من العرش والكرسي والجنة والنار، فعلمنا أن العادة العامة جارية بإطلاق لفظ الكل والجمع على الأكثر على وجه المبالغة، وإذا كان هذا أمرًا معتادًا علمنا أن دلالة هذه الألفاظ على الاستغراق دلالة ظنية محتملة لا قطعية.
إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إنه لا يجوز التمسك بهذه الألفاظ في هذه المسألة؛ لأن هذه المسألة قطعية والتمسك فيها بالدليل الظني باطل قطعا.
One of them: If these forms indicated universality definitively, then it would be impossible to insert a word of emphasis upon them, because obtaining the obtained is impossible. Since they agreed that it is good to insert emphatic words upon them, we know that their indication of universality is probable, not definitive. Second: The knowledge that these words are placed for universality is either obtained by mutawatir report or by ahad report. The first is false, otherwise disagreement about it would not have arisen, so the second remains. But ahad report only yields probability. Third: We see that people often express the majority by the word 'all' and 'plural' by way of exaggeration. God said: 'And she was given of everything' (27:23), and it is known that she was not given the Throne, the Kursi, Paradise, and Hell. So we know that the general custom is to use the word 'all' and 'plural' for the majority by way of exaggeration. Since this is customary, we know that the indication of these words for universality is probable and possible, not definitive. If this is established, we say: It is not permissible to rely on these words in this matter, because this matter is definitive and relying on probable evidence in it is definitively invalid.
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage presents three proofs that general terms (صيغ العموم) do not yield certainty but only probability. The author first states that if these terms were definitively universal, adding emphasis (تأكيد) would be impossible because that would be obtaining the already obtained. Since emphasis is accepted, the universality is only probable. Second, the knowledge of these terms being for universality comes either from tawatur (mass transmission) or ahad (single report); tawatur is false because disagreement persists, so it must be ahad, which only gives probability. Third, people commonly use 'all' for 'most' hyperbolically, as in the Quranic verse about Bilqis; thus the indication is probable. The conclusion: since this question is definitive (قطعية), relying on merely probable evidence is invalid.

Key Terms
خبر التواتر
خبر الآحاد
ظنية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Linguistic knowledge is always certain because language is divinely revealed.
Correction: Razi argues that knowledge of word meanings comes from reports, and not all reports are certain.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi reject the possibility that knowledge of general terms comes from mutawatir reports?
A) Because there is no mutawatir report about language.
B) Because if it were mutawatir, there would be no disagreement, but there is disagreement.
C) Because mutawatir reports are only about religious matters.
D) Because ahad reports are more common.

Key Takeaway
The second proof argues that knowledge of general terms comes from ahad reports, so it is only probable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a general command in the Quran like 'establish prayer,' do you assume your understanding of its scope is certain? How does Razi's argument that such knowledge is only probable challenge your confidence? What would it mean for you to hold that interpretation with humility?
?
Think of a theological issue where you disagree with another Muslim. Have you ever dismissed their view because you believed your position was based on certain knowledge? How might Razi's argument about disagreement and probability change the way you approach that disagreement?
?
Razi says that if knowledge of general terms were mutawatir, no disagreement would exist. Is there an area of your faith where you assume there is unanimous agreement, but in reality there is legitimate difference? Are you willing to investigate whether your certainty is actually only probability?

The Third Proof: Customary Usage Shows Probability
Razi's third argument: customary hyperbole and conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's third argument that people commonly use 'all' to mean 'most' hyperbolically, so general terms are probable, and the conclusion that definitive matters cannot rely on probable evidence.

Core Idea
People often use 'all' hyperbolically for 'most', so the meaning is probable.

Razi's third argument is empirical: we observe that people frequently use terms like 'all' and 'every' to exaggerate, not to mean literally every single one. He cites the Quranic verse about the Queen of Sheba to show this. Then he concludes that since the theological question is definitive, relying on probable general terms is invalid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic for the third proof and the conclusion. Note the Quranic evidence.
2
Understand the customary usage
Razi points out that people say 'she was given everything' when she wasn't literally given everything. This hyperbole is common.
3
Connect to the conclusion
Since this usage is common, the indication of general terms is probable, not certain. Therefore, they cannot be used to settle definitive theological questions.
This is the practical implication of all three proofs.

Source Text

The Ninth Proof: Analogy of the Servant
Imam Razi presents the ninth proof using a servant analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses a servant analogy to argue that God's promise of reward outweighs His threat of punishment for a believing sinner.

Core Idea
God's generosity demands that great faith outweighs minor sin.

Imam Razi presents a compelling analogy from human social norms to demonstrate that God, being the Most Generous, must give precedence to His promise of reward over His threat of punishment for a believer who commits minor sins. This lesson unpacks that analogy and its theological implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once carefully, then the English translation. Notice how Razi structures the argument with a premise and an analogy.
The passage uses a single, continuous argument.
2
Identify the sinner's deeds
Razi categorizes the sinner's deeds: faith as the best obedience, and sin as below the middle level of evil. Understand why this distinction matters for the argument.
Faith is the ultimate good; minor sin is not the worst evil.
3
Analyze the servant analogy
Consider the hypothetical master-servant relationship. Why would valuing a minor sin over great service be considered ignoble? Reflect on how this applies to God.
The analogy relies on common human understanding of generosity.
4
Draw the theological conclusion
Razi concludes that since God is the Most Generous, He must prefer the great deed of faith over the minor sin, making the promise preponderant. Ensure you see the logical flow.
This proof supports the precedence of wa'd over wa'eed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التاسع : أن هذا العاصي أتى بأحسن الطاعات - وهو الإيمان - ولم يأت بأقبح القبائح . وهو الكفر - فأتى في طبقة الخيرات بما هو الغاية القصوى، وأتى في طبقة الشر لا بما هو الغاية، بل بما هو دون الوسط، والرجل إذا كان له عبد ثم أتى عبده بأعظم أنواع الخدمة والطاعة ثم أتى في باب المعاصي والذنوب بمعصية ليست في غاية القبح، بل دون الوسط، فلو أن المولى رجح تلك المعصية الخفيفة على تلك الطاعة العظيمة لعد ذلك السيد والمولى لئيما مؤذيًا، بعيدًا عن الكرم، ومعلوم أن هذا غير لائق بكرم أكرم الأكرمين وأرحم الراحمين فعلمنا ان الرجحان حاصل لجانب الوعد.
The ninth: That this sinner has performed the best of acts of obedience — and it is faith — and has not performed the ugliest of ugly deeds — and it is disbelief — so he has come in the category of good deeds with what is the ultimate extreme, and he has come in the category of evil not with what is the extreme, but rather with what is below the middle. And a man, if he has a servant and then his servant performs the greatest types of service and obedience, then comes in the door of sins and offenses with a sin that is not at the extreme of ugliness, but rather below the middle, and if the master were to prefer that minor sin over that great obedience, that master and lord would be counted as vile, harmful, far from generosity. And it is known that this is not befitting the generosity of the Most Generous of the generous and the Most Merciful of the merciful. So we know that preponderance is on the side of the promise.
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage presents the ninth proof for the precedence of God's promise (wa'd) over His threat (wa'eed) in the case of a grave sinner (fasiq) who dies without repentance. The argument is an analogy (qiyas) from human social norms. The sinner has performed the greatest good deed—faith (iman)—and has not committed the worst evil—disbelief (kufr). He has achieved the ultimate in good deeds, while his sin is only a minor one, below the middle level of evil. If a human master had a servant who performed the greatest services but then committed a minor offense, and the master were to value that minor offense more than the great service, the master would be considered ignoble and ungenerous. Since God is the Most Generous and Most Merciful, He must give greater weight to the deed of faith than to the minor sin. Therefore, the promise of reward is preponderant over the threat of punishment for such a person.

Key Terms
العاصي
الإيمان
الكفر
الوسط
الطاعات
المعاصي
الوعد
الوعيد
الرجحان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that this proof means the sinner will never face any punishment at all.
Correction: The passage argues for preponderance or outweighing, not absolute nullification. The promise of reward is stronger, but the threat may still be realized in a lesser form or after purgation.

Checkpoint
What does Imam Razi compare the fasiq's situation to in the ninth proof?
A) A king and a rebel
B) A master and a servant who performs great service but commits a minor sin
C) A father and a disobedient son
D) A teacher and a lazy student

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi's ninth proof uses a rational analogy from human generosity to show that God's promise of reward must outweigh His threat of punishment for a believer who commits minor sins.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were to stand before God today, would you honestly consider your faith to outweigh your minor sins? What specific evidence from your life supports that, or what does its absence reveal about your heart?
?
When you commit a sin you consider 'minor', do you truly trust that God's generosity is greater than that offense, or do you secretly fear that your sin will tip the scale against you? How does that trust (or lack of it) affect your daily relationship with Him?
?
Imagine you are the servant in the analogy: you have served your Master with great devotion but then slipped into a small fault. Do you believe the Master will be generous? Now ask yourself: are you living as though you believe that, or are you paralyzed by guilt? What would change in your life if you fully embraced that God's promise outweighs your failure?

The Second Mu'tazili Proof: Specificity of Threat Verses
The Mu'tazila argue that specific threat verses take precedence over general promise verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's argument that specific threat verses override general promise verses in determining God's threat.

Core Idea
Specific divine threats outweigh general promises in scripture.

The Mu'tazila employed established principles of Islamic legal theory to argue for the preponderance of threat over promise. Here, they present a textual argument: because verses threatening punishment are specific in their application, they should be given precedence over the general promises of reward. This reasoning draws on the well-known rule that the specific text (al-khass) takes priority over the general (al-'amm).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the Mu'tazila apply a principle from usul al-fiqh to theological argument.
2
Understand the hermeneutical principle
Recall that in Islamic legal reasoning, a specific text (khass) is given precedence over a general text ('amm) when they conflict. Ask why this principle is used here.
This principle is not limited to fiqh; it applies to any textual interpretation.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this argument strengthens the Mu'tazili position that God's threat of punishment is definitive, not contingent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني : في ترجيح جانب الوعيد على جانب الوعد : أن آيات الوعيد خاصة، و آيات الوعد عامة والخاص مقدم على العام. الوجه الثالث : أن الناس جبلوا على الفساد والشر فكانت الحاجة إلى الزاجر أشد فكان جانب الوعيد راجحا.
The second aspect: regarding giving preponderance to the side of threat over the side of promise: that the verses of threat are specific, and the verses of promise are general, and the specific is given precedence over the general. The third aspect: that people are naturally disposed to corruption and evil, so the need for a deterrent is more severe, so the side of threat is preponderant.
Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This passage presents two additional Mu'tazilite proofs for the preponderance of threat over promise. The second proof is a textual argument: verses of threat are specific (they refer to particular sins), whereas verses of promise are general (they promise paradise to believers in general). In the science of usul, when a specific text conflicts with a general text, the specific is given precedence. Therefore, the threat verses outweigh the promise verses. The third proof is a practical argument: human beings are naturally inclined to corruption and evil. Therefore, the need for a deterrent (i.e., the threat of punishment) is stronger than the need for encouragement. Hence, the threat side is preponderant. Both arguments are concise and rely on widely accepted principles.

Key Terms
خاص
عام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila arbitrarily prefer harsh interpretations.
Correction: Their argument is based on a standard principle of textual interpretation (specific over general), not arbitrariness.

Checkpoint
What principle do the Mu'tazila use to argue that threat verses outweigh promise verses?
A) The general takes precedence over the specific.
B) The specific takes precedence over the general.
C) The more recent revelation abrogates the earlier.
D) Threat verses are more numerous.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila use the principle of specific precedence to argue that God's threat verses dominate over promise verses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quran, do you honestly give more weight to the verses of promise or to the verses of threat? Which ones shape your daily actions more? The Mu'tazila argue that specific threats should have more weight. How would your life change if you truly took every specific threat in the Quran as applying to you today? Commit to memorizing one verse of threat this week and reflecting on its meaning for your soul.
?
The argument rests on the principle that specific texts take precedence over general ones. In your own obedience to Allah, do you tend to focus on the general command (e.g., 'do good') while neglecting specific prohibitions (e.g., 'do not commit this sin')? Which specific command of Allah are you currently ignoring, and what will you do about it today?
?
If you were to stand before Allah tomorrow and He asked you why you paid more attention to His promises than His threats, what would you say? Does your life demonstrate that you believe His threats are real and imminent? This lesson challenges you to examine whether your faith is based on complacency or on a balanced fear and hope.

The Third Mu'tazili Proof: Human Nature and the Need for Deterrence
The Mu'tazila argue that human disposition to evil requires stronger threat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's argument that human inclination toward evil necessitates a stronger deterrent, making God's threat preponderant.

Core Idea
Human corruption makes threat a necessary deterrent.

The Mu'tazila also appeal to human nature in their third argument. Observing that people are naturally inclined to corruption and evil, they conclude that a stronger deterrent (zajir) is required. Since threat of punishment serves as that deterrent, it must be preponderant over promise. This argument is psychological and practical, not textual.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the reasoning: human nature → need for deterrent → threat outweighs promise.
2
Consider the underlying assumption
The Mu'tazila assume that threat is more effective than promise in deterring evil. Reflect on whether this assumption is valid.
This argument is based on observation of human behavior.
3
Evaluate the argument's strength
This argument is based on human psychology. Compare it to the textual argument in the second proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني : في ترجيح جانب الوعيد على جانب الوعد : أن آيات الوعيد خاصة، و آيات الوعد عامة والخاص مقدم على العام. الوجه الثالث : أن الناس جبلوا على الفساد والشر فكانت الحاجة إلى الزاجر أشد فكان جانب الوعيد راجحا.
The second aspect: regarding giving preponderance to the side of threat over the side of promise: that the verses of threat are specific, and the verses of promise are general, and the specific is given precedence over the general. The third aspect: that people are naturally disposed to corruption and evil, so the need for a deterrent is more severe, so the side of threat is preponderant.
Source pages p. 393

This passage presents two additional Mu'tazilite proofs for the preponderance of threat over promise. The second proof is a textual argument: verses of threat are specific (they refer to particular sins), whereas verses of promise are general (they promise paradise to believers in general). In the science of usul, when a specific text conflicts with a general text, the specific is given precedence. Therefore, the threat verses outweigh the promise verses. The third proof is a practical argument: human beings are naturally inclined to corruption and evil. Therefore, the need for a deterrent (i.e., the threat of punishment) is stronger than the need for encouragement. Hence, the threat side is preponderant. Both arguments are concise and rely on widely accepted principles.

Key Terms
زاجر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila think God is harsh and vengeful.
Correction: They argue that threat is necessary due to human nature, not that God is inherently harsh.

Checkpoint

A) Textual analysis of specific and general verses.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argue that because humans are prone to evil, God's threat must be stronger than His promise to effectively deter sin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
If God's threat is preponderant due to human nature, how does that affect your understanding of His mercy? Do you feel that focusing on threat contradicts His mercy, or does it actually guide you to righteousness? What change in your attitude toward sin does this lesson demand?

First Ash'ari Response to Mu'tazili Proof
The Ash'ari response argues that praise in this world equally supports promise, and hadd does not prove eternal punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari response uses symmetrical reasoning from worldly praise and challenges the evidential value of hadd penalties.

Core Idea
Worldly praise and punishment cancel each other; hadd does not prove eternal hell.

Imam al-Razi now presents the Ash'ari counter-argument to the Mu'tazilite proof from worldly punishment. The response uses two moves: a symmetry argument and a critique of the significance of hadd. Understanding this subtle rebuttal is key to grasping later kalam debates on eternal punishment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the use of Qur'anic verse 6:54 as evidence.
2
Identify the symmetry argument
The Ash'ari argues that just as the Mu'tazila use worldly punishment to support threat, the Ash'aris can use worldly praise to support promise. Find the exact reasoning in the passage.
3
Analyze the hadd objection and response
The Mu'tazila might ask: if no curse, why hadd? The Ash'ari responds that hadd is deterrent, not necessarily eternal punishment. Note the example of the repentant person.
4
Reflect on the overall impact
Consider why this response weakens the Mu'tazilite proof without disproving it entirely. How does it set up further discussion?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أجاب أصحابنا عن الترجيح الأول من وجوه : الأول: أنه لما وجدت آيات دالة على أنهم يلعنون ويذمون ويعذبون في الدنيا بسبب معاصيهم كذلك أيضا حصلت آيات دالة على أنهم يعظمون ويمدحون الدنيا بسبب إيمانهم قال الله تعالى : ﴿وَإِذَا جَاءَكَ الَّذِينَ يُؤْمِنُونَ بِنَايَتِنَا فَقُلْ سَلامُ عَلَيْكُم كتب رَبُّكُمْ عَلى نَفْسِهِ الرَّحْمَةُ ﴾ [الأنعام: ٥٤] فهم يرجحون الآيات الدالة على حصول الوعيد في الآخرة بالآيات الدالة على حصول اللعن والدم في الدنيا، ونحن أيضًا نرجح الآيات الدالة على حصول وعد الإحسان والعفو في الآخرة بالآيات الدالة على حصول المدح والتعظيم في الدنيا، فلم كان قولهم أرجح من قولنا؟ بل كان الترجيح معا من الوجوه المذكورة. أقصى ما في الباب أن يقال : فإن كان لا يلعن ولا يذم في الدنيا فلم يقام في الدنيا عليه الحد؟ فتقول : هذا ضعيف؛ لأن الحد قد يقام على سبيل التنكيل، ألا ترى أن التائب قد يقام عليه الحد أنه ليس هناك لعن ولا نكال فعلمنا أنه لا يمكن الاستدلال مع بإقامة الحد على حصول استحقاق العذاب.
Our companions responded to the first preponderance from [several] aspects. The first: that when there exist verses indicating that they are cursed, reviled, and punished in this world due to their sins, likewise there have come verses indicating that they are magnified and praised in this world due to their faith. Allah the Exalted said: 'And when those who believe in Our verses come to you, say: Peace be upon you; your Lord has prescribed mercy for Himself' [al-An'am 6:54]. They give preponderance to the verses indicating the occurrence of threat in the Hereafter by means of the verses indicating the occurrence of curse and bloodshed in this world, and we also give preponderance to the verses indicating the occurrence of the promise of beneficence and pardon in the Hereafter by means of the verses indicating the occurrence of praise and magnification in this world. So why is their statement more preponderant than ours? Rather, the preponderance is equal from the aspects mentioned. The most that can be said is: 'If he is not cursed nor reviled in this world, why is the hadd penalty established against him in this world?' So we say: This is weak, because the hadd penalty may be established by way of deterrent punishment. Do you not see that the repentant person may have the hadd penalty established against him, while there is no curse nor punishment [there]? So we know that it is not possible to use the establishment of the hadd penalty as evidence for the entitlement to chastisement.
Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This passage is the first Ash'ari response to the first Mu'tazilite proof. It is a rebuttal that neutralizes the Mu'tazilite argument. The response proceeds in two steps. First, it points out that just as there are verses indicating that sinners are cursed and punished in this world, there are also verses indicating that believers are praised and magnified in this world (e.g., the verse from al-An'am). The Mu'tazila use worldly punishment to argue for threat in the Hereafter; the Ash'aris can equally use worldly praise to argue for promise in the Hereafter. Thus, the two proofs are symmetrical and cancel each other out. Second, the response addresses a potential objection: if the sinner is not cursed in this world, why is he subjected to the hadd? The answer is that the hadd may be a purely deterrent punishment, not necessarily indicating eternal chastisement. The example of the repentant person who still receives the hadd shows that the hadd does not entail eternal curse or punishment. Therefore, the Mu'tazilite inference from hadd to eternal punishment is invalid.

Key Terms
تعارض الأدلة
حد
تائب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari response denies that hadd is punishment at all.
Correction: The Ash'aris do not deny that hadd is punishment; they deny that it necessarily indicates eternal punishment in the Hereafter. The hadd can be a deterrent without implying eternal chastisement.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the first Ash'ari response to the Mu'tazilite argument?
A) Worldly praise of believers proves that promise is stronger than threat.
B) The hadd penalty is never applied to repentant sinners.
C) The Mu'tazilite argument is weaker because it ignores the verses of praise.
D) Both A and C, and the hadd does not prove eternal punishment.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari response shows that the Mu'tazilite proof from worldly punishment is not decisive because worldly praise equally supports promise, and hadd penalties are not proof of eternal chastisement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you judge your own spiritual state based on how people treat you in this world—whether they praise or blame you? How does this lesson challenge your reliance on worldly opinion as a measure of your standing with Allah?
?
The lesson shows that even legal penalties (hadd) do not necessarily indicate eternal punishment. When you witness someone being punished for a sin, do you privately assume they are less favored by Allah? How can you correct this assumption in light of this passage?
?
The Ash'ari response uses the example of a repentant person receiving hadd without curse. Have you ever been punished or faced consequences for a sin you repented from? Did you feel it meant Allah was still angry? How does this lesson change your understanding of such situations?

Second Ash'ari Response: Worldly Punishment Verses Conflict with Promise
The second Ash'ari response: worldly punishment verses conflict with promise verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'aris used the principle of textual conflict to neutralize the Mu'tazilite appeal to worldly punishment verses.

Core Idea
Verses of promise conflict with every verse implying threat, not just threat verses.

The Mu'tazila cited worldly punishment verses to support threat verses. The Ash'aris now point out a logical flaw: these verses themselves conflict with promise verses. If they conflict, they cannot tip the scales. This argument closes another Mu'tazilite loophole.

Guided Path
1
Understand the conflict principle
The Ash'aris claim that verses of promise conflict not only with explicit threat verses but also with any verse that implies threat in the Hereafter.
This expands the scope of conflict beyond the obvious.
2
Recognize the Mu'tazilite assumption
According to the Mu'tazila, verses about worldly punishment (curse, disgrace, etc.) entail threat in the Hereafter.
The Ash'aris accept this entailment for the sake of argument.
3
See the consequence
If those verses entail threat, then they conflict with promise verses. Conflict prevents them from giving preponderance to threat verses, so the Ash'ari preponderances remain unchallenged.
The argument is about weighing evidence, not denying the verses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني : أن آيات الوعد كما أنها معارضة لآيات الوعيد، فكذلك هي معارضة لكل آية دالة على معنى يستلزم حصول الوعيد في الآخرة، وعندكم أن الآيات الدالة على حصول اللعن والخزي والنكال والعذاب في الدنيا مستلزمة لحصول الوعيد في الآخرة، فيحصل التعارض بين هذه الآيات بين آيات الوعيد، وإذا حصل التعارض في هذا المقام لم تكن هذه الآيات مرجحة لآيات الوعد على آيات الوعيد، فيبقى ما ذكرناه من الترجيحات سليمًا عن المعارض.
The second aspect: that the verses of promise, just as they conflict with the verses of threat, likewise they conflict with every verse that indicates a meaning entailing the occurrence of threat in the Hereafter. And according to you, the verses indicating the occurrence of curse, disgrace, punishment, and chastisement in this world entail the occurrence of threat in the Hereafter. So conflict occurs between these verses and the verses of threat. And when conflict occurs in this context, these verses do not give preponderance to the verses of promise over the verses of threat. So what we mentioned of preponderances remains free from opposition.
Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This passage presents the second Ash'ari response to the first Mu'tazilite proof. It is a technical argument about textual conflict. The Ash'aris point out that the verses promising mercy conflict not only with the verses of threat but also with every verse that implies threat in the Hereafter—including the verses about worldly punishment that the Mu'tazila use as evidence. According to the Mu'tazila, the verses about curse, disgrace, and worldly chastisement entail threat in the Hereafter. Therefore, these verses themselves conflict with the verses of promise. But when two sets of verses conflict, neither can be used to give preponderance to the other. Thus, the Mu'tazilite proof fails, and the Ash'ari preponderances (presented earlier) stand without opposition from this argument.

Key Terms
معارضة
استلزام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari argument denies the authenticity or meaning of worldly punishment verses.
Correction: It does not deny them; it argues that because they conflict with promise verses, they cannot be used as evidence to support threat verses.

Checkpoint
According to the second Ash'ari response, why do the verses about worldly punishment not help the Mu'tazilite case?
A) Because those verses are not authentic.
B) Because they conflict with the verses of promise and thus cannot preponderate over them.
C) Because they are general, not specific.
D) Because they only apply to disbelievers.

Key Takeaway
Worldly punishment verses cannot support threat verses because they themselves conflict with promise verses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read verses about punishment or calamities in this world, do you automatically assume they indicate Allah's anger and eternal threat? How does this argument challenge that automatic assumption?
?
Have you ever found yourself relying on certain verses to support a harsh view of Allah while ignoring the overwhelming verses of mercy? What would it take for you to rebalance your understanding?
?
If even the worldly punishment verses cannot outweigh the promise verses, how should that transform the way you interpret trials and difficulties in your own life?

Definition of Muwazana and First Proof Against It
Razi defines muwazana and presents the first proof of its invalidity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's definition of muwazana and his first rational proof demonstrating its logical impossibility.

Core Idea
Muwazana fails logically: reward and punishment cannot cancel each other without contradiction.

Imam Razi introduces the concept of muwazana, the idea that prior reward and temporary punishment can balance each other. He contrasts it with the non-balancing view and then provides a decisive proof against it. This lesson walks through that proof step by step, showing why the balancing concept leads to necessary contradictions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get a feel for the argument. Notice the definitions and the two possible scenarios.
2
Understand the two positions
Identify what muwazana and non-muwazana mean. Note the attribution to Abu Hashim and Abu Ali.
3
Follow the proof chain
Razi presents a dilemma: either the prior prevents the temporary or not. Follow each branch and see why both lead to the failure of balancing.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The proof shows that muwazana is impossible. Why does this matter for Islamic theology?
This connects to broader discussions of divine justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأعني بالموازنة أن يقال : كان الثواب عشرة أجزاء، والعقاب الطارئ خمسة أجزاء، فسقط الخمسة بالخمسة، ويبقى له من الثواب خمسة خالية عن المعارض وأما عدم الموازنة فهو أن الطارئ يسقط السابق بقدره، ولا يسقط الطارئ البتة بل يبقى بحاله، والقول بالموازنة مذهب أبي هاشم والقول بعدم الموازنة مذهب أبي علي. فنقول : القول بالموازنة باطل، ويدل عليه وجوه : الأول: أن ثبوت السابق إما أن يمنع الطارئ من الدخول في الوجود، أو لا يمنعه، فإن منعه لم يدخل الطارئ في الوجود، و إذا لم يوجد الطارئ، بقي السابق كما كان، وهذا يمنع من الإحباط. وأما إن قلنا : إن السابق لم يمنع الطارئ من الدخول في الوجود، بل دخل في الوجود، فهذا الطارئ إما أن يؤثر في عدم السابق أو لا يؤثر فيه، فإن أثر فيه صار الطارئ غالبًا والسابق مغلوبًا، وعند صيررة السابق مغلوبا امتنع أن وامتنع أن يؤثر في إعدام الطارئ، فلم تكن الموزانة حاصلة، و إن قلنا بأن الطارئ بعد دخوله في الوجود لم يؤثر في إعدام السابق، فعلى هذا فقد وجد الطارئ مع السابق واجتمعا في الوجود، وعلى هذا التقدير لم يحصل بينهما منافاة أصلا، وإذا كان كذلك امتنع أن يكون أحدهما مؤثرًا في عدم الآخر، وفي زواله، فثبت بهذا البرهان القوي فساد القول بالموازنة.
And I mean by al-muwazana (the balancing) that it is said: the reward was ten parts, and the temporary punishment five parts, so the five fell by the five, and there remains for him from the reward five free from opposition. As for non-balancing, it is that the temporary removes the prior by its amount, but the temporary is not removed at all, rather it remains as it is. The saying of balancing is the doctrine of Abu Hashim, and the saying of non-balancing is the doctrine of Abu Ali. So we say: the saying of balancing is false, and proofs indicate it: the first: that the existence of the prior either prevents the temporary from coming into existence or does not prevent it. If it prevents, the temporary does not come into existence, and if the temporary does not exist, the prior remains as it was, and this prevents nullification. But if we say that the prior did not prevent the temporary from coming into existence, rather it came into existence, then this temporary either affects the non-existence of the prior or does not affect it. If it affects it, then the temporary becomes predominant and the prior overpowered. When the prior becomes overpowered, it is impossible for it to affect the annihilation of the temporary, so the balancing does not obtain. And if we say that the temporary after coming into existence did not affect the annihilation of the prior, then on this, the temporary has come into existence with the prior and they coexist. On this estimation, there is no opposition between them at all. And if it is so, it is impossible for one to affect the non-existence and removal of the other. So by this strong proof, the corruption of the saying of balancing is established.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of muwazana (balancing) and presents the first of three proofs against it. Razi first explains what muwazana means: that some of the prior reward cancels part of the temporary punishment, leaving a net reward. He contrasts this with the non-balancing view of Abu Ali, where the temporary only removes a corresponding amount of the prior but itself remains. He then presents the first proof: if the prior reward prevents the punishment from existing, then punishment never occurs and there is no nullification. If the prior does not prevent the punishment, then either the punishment annihilates the prior or not. If it annihilates, then the prior cannot attack the punishment, so balance fails. If it does not annihilate, they coexist without opposition, so neither affects the other. In both cases, the balancing concept fails.

Key Terms
الموازنة
الإحباط
السابق
الطارئ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Muwazana is a simple, balanced exchange where reward and punishment cancel each other half-half.
Correction: Razi's proof shows that the concept leads to logical contradictions: either the prior prevents the temporary entirely, or if they coexist, one dominates the other or neither affects the other; in no case is there a balanced cancellation.

Checkpoint
In Razi's first proof against muwazana, what happens if the prior reward does not prevent the punishment and the punishment does not annihilate the prior?
A) The prior is neutralized
B) They coexist without opposition
C) The punishment overcomes the prior
D) The prior destroys the punishment

Key Takeaway
Muwazana is rationally untenable because any scenario leads to either no interaction or unilateral defeat, not a balanced cancellation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What would it mean for your own deeds if muwazana were true? Would you feel more or less responsible for each sin, knowing it could simply cancel out a good deed? How does Razi's proof change your perspective on the gravity of your sins?
?
If reward and punishment cannot simply balance out, then what determines the final outcome for a person? How does this affect your reliance on your own good deeds versus relying on Allah's mercy? What will you change today in how you evaluate your actions?
?
The proof shows that either reward dominates or punishment dominates—they cannot coexist neutrally. In your own spiritual life, are you more aware of the 'dominant' factor? Is your hope in your good deeds or in Allah's forgiveness? How can this proof guard you against complacency or despair?

Second Proof: The Impossibility of Mutual Nullification
Razi refutes muwazana by showing simultaneous and successive nullification are impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's second proof against muwazana, which shows that mutual nullification between prior reward and temporary punishment is logically impossible.

Core Idea
Mutual nullification is absurd because it leads to contradiction.

In this passage, Imam Razi presents the second argument against the theory that reward and punishment can cancel each other out. He examines two possible scenarios for their mutual nullification and shows that both lead to logical absurdities. This proof strengthens the case that divine reward and punishment are not subject to simple balancing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the logical structure: the author first states the hypothesis and then divides it into two possibilities.
Focus on the two divisions: simultaneous and successive.
2
Identify the two divisions
The passage states that mutual nullification would have to be either simultaneous or successive. Locate these two scenarios in the text.
They are introduced with 'إما أن يكون معا، أو على التعاقب'.
3
Understand why both are rejected
For simultaneous nullification, Razi argues it would force the two to exist and not exist at the same time. For successive nullification, it would require an impossible reversal of dominance. Reflect on these contradictions.
The commentary will help clarify the reasoning.
4
Connect to the overall refutation
Since both possibilities are impossible, the original assumption of mutual nullification is false. This refutes the theory of muwazana.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني في بطلانه هو أنه لو كان كل واحد من السابق والطارئ، مؤثرًا في عدم الآخر لكان تأثير كل واحد منهما في الآخر إما أن يكون معا، أو على التعاقب، والقسمان باطلان، فبطل القول بكون كل واحد منهما مؤثرا في عدم الآخر. وإنما قلنا : إنه يمتنع حصول ذلك معاً ؛ لأن المؤثر في عدم كل واحد منهما وجود الآخر، فلو حصل العدمان معا لحصل الوجودان معا حال حصول العدمين ؛ لأن العلة واجبة الحصول عند حصول المعلول فيلزم كونهما موجودين حال كونهما معدومين، وهو محال . وإنما قلنا : إنه يمتنع حصول ذلك على التعاقب لا معا؛ لأنه يقتضي أن يعود المغلوب بعد صيرورته مغلوبًا غالبًا، وذلك محال.
The second proof of its falsity is that if each of the prior and the temporary were effective in the non-existence of the other, then the effect of each on the other would either be simultaneously or successively, and both divisions are false, so the saying that each is effective in the non-existence of the other is false. And we only say: it is impossible for that to occur simultaneously, because the effective factor in the non-existence of each is the existence of the other. So if the two non-existences occurred together, the two existences would occur together at the time of the occurrence of the two non-existences, because the cause is necessarily present at the time of the effect's occurrence, so it would necessitate that they be existent at the time they are non-existent, which is impossible. And we only say: it is impossible for that to occur successively (not simultaneously), because it would require that the overpowered, after becoming overpowered, becomes predominant, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage gives the second proof against muwazana. It assumes that if each of the prior reward and temporary punishment could nullify the other, the nullification would have to be either simultaneous or successive. Simultaneous nullification is impossible because it would require each to both exist and not exist at the same time. Successive nullification would require a reversal where the former becomes predominant again after being defeated, which is also impossible. Therefore, mutual nullification is absurd, invalidating the balancing theory.

Key Terms
التعاقب
العلة
المعلول
الوجود
العدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that reward and punishment can simply cancel each other out like positive and negative numbers.
Correction: Razi shows that mutual nullification leads to logical impossibility because it would require simultaneous existence and non-existence or an impossible reversal of dominance.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi reject simultaneous mutual nullification between prior and temporary?
A) It would require them to be both existent and non-existent at the same time
B) It would require them to be equal in strength
C) It would violate the principle of non-contradiction
D) Both A and C

Key Takeaway
Mutual nullification is logically impossible because it would require simultaneous existence and non-existence or an impossible reversal of dominance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held two beliefs that logically contradict each other, like the idea that both reward and punishment could cancel out? How did that affect your faith or actions, and what did you do to resolve it?
?
Razi shows that even the sequence of events cannot salvage a flawed premise. Is there a belief or habit in your life that you keep trying to justify by reordering or delaying—but which ultimately leads to an impossible contradiction?
?
The proof forces you to accept that some things are simply impossible, no matter how you arrange them. What is one area of your life where you need to stop negotiating with impossibility and submit to the truth, even if it requires a fundamental change?

Third Proof Against Muwazana and Conclusion
Razi gives the third proof against muwazana and concludes all three proofs invalidate it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's third proof against muwazana, which exposes a circular dependence, and how it concludes the refutation.

Core Idea
Circular dependence invalidates the muwazana theory.

Razi presents his final and most subtle objection to the theory of muwazana. Building on the previous two proofs, he now identifies a logical trap: if the removal of reward depends on the arrival of punishment, and the arrival of punishment depends on the removal of reward, the argument becomes circular. This third proof closes the case against muwazana.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the full argument. Notice the conditional structure.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Understand the condition
The proof begins by stating a condition: the arrival of one contrary requires the removal of the first contrary.
3
Identify the circularity
If the removal of the first contrary were caused by the arrival of the second, then each depends on the other—a circular dependency that is impossible.
This is the core logical flaw.
4
Connect to the conclusion
Razi states that all three proofs together prevent the validity of muwazana. Reflect on how this third proof complements the earlier ones.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث : وهو أن شرط طريان أحد الضدين زوال الضد الأول، فلو كان زوال الضد الأول معللا بطريان الضد الثاني لزم توقف كل واحد مهما على الآخر، وهو محال. فهذه الوجوه الثلاثة دالة على المنع من صحة الموازنة.
The third proof: that the condition of the arrival of one of the two contraries is the removal of the first contrary. So if the removal of the first contrary were caused by the arrival of the second contrary, it would necessitate that each of them be dependent on the other, which is impossible. So these three proofs indicate the prevention of the validity of muwazana.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This short passage presents the third proof against muwazana and then concludes the entire refutation. Razi argues: the arrival of a new contrary (the punishment) requires the prior removal of the old contrary (the reward). If that removal were itself caused by the arrival of the new contrary, then each depends on the other (circularity), which is impossible. After presenting all three proofs, Razi states that they collectively demonstrate the invalidity of muwazana.

Key Terms
الضدين
طريان
الموازنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The third proof is just a repetition of the earlier proofs.
Correction: The third proof targets a different logical issue: circular dependence, not infinite regress or contradiction. It shows that the muwazana theory forces necessary conditions to be mutually dependent, which is impossible.

Checkpoint
What logical problem does the third proof identify in the muwazana theory?
A) Infinite regress
B) Circular dependence
C) Contradiction
D) False analogy

Key Takeaway
The third proof invalidates muwazana by showing that the removal of one contrary cannot be caused by the arrival of the other without circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi shows that circular dependency in causation is impossible. Where in your own life have you used circular reasoning to justify a habit or belief, and what would it cost you to break that loop?
?
If the removal of a bad quality cannot depend on the arrival of a good one without circularity, what does that mean for how you approach sincere repentance? How can you avoid relying on a 'balance' and instead seek direct transformation?
?
The proof concludes that muwazana is invalid. Are there areas where you subconsciously expect God to 'balance' your deeds in a way that lets you off the hook? What would it mean to accept that His justice is not mechanically balanced?

Razi's Rational Proof for the Obligation of Imamate
Razi presents the first station of his proof: people must appoint a leader.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's two-part rational proof that appointing a leader is obligatory upon the people, based on the necessity of repelling harm.

Core Idea
Imamate is rationally obligatory because it repels inevitable harm, and repelling harm is a universal duty.

This passage is from Imam Razi's Al-Arbaeen fi Usul al-Din, where he sets out to prove that the Imamate (leadership) is obligatory upon the Muslim community. Razi approaches this not from revelation alone, but from rational necessity. He divides the discussion into two stations: first, proving that the people must appoint a leader, and second, showing that this obligation does not fall upon God. Here he elaborates the first station, combining empirical observation with a self-evident moral principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two stations
Read the opening lines to see Razi's two-part plan: (1) obligation on people to appoint a leader, (2) no obligation on God. Focus on the first station.
This structure shows Razi's comprehensive approach.
2
Grasp the core argument
The proof has two premises: (a) appointing an Imam repels harm that otherwise would occur, (b) repelling harm from oneself is obligatory as far as possible. Therefore, people must appoint an Imam.
This is a straightforward deductive argument.
3
Examine the empirical premise
Razi supports the first premise by observing that countries with a strong, guiding leader are more orderly than those without. This is known by inductive reasoning from customs.
He appeals to common experience, not scripture.
4
Understand the universal agreement on the second premise
All rational people agree that repelling harm is obligatory, whether through rational intuition (good and evil) or consensus of the prophets.
Razi ensures the premise is accepted by different schools of thought.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولنا فيه مقامان : أحدهما : بيان أنه يجب على الخلق نصب رئيس لأنفسهم. وثانيهما : بيان أنه لا يجب على الله نصب الإمام لهم. أما المقام الأول فالدليل عليه أن نصب الإمام يتضمن اندفاع ضرر لا يندفع إلا بنصبه ودفع الضرر عن النفس واجب بقدر الإمكان، وهذا يقتضي أنه يجب على العقلاء أن ينصبوا إماما لأنفسهم. بيان المقام الأول: وهو أنه يجب على الخلق نصب رئيس لأنفسهم، فهو أنا نرى أن البلد إذا حصل فيه رئيس قاهر مهيب سائس يأمرهم بالأفعال الجميلة وينهاهم عن القبائح كان حال البلد في البعد عن التشويش والفساد والقرب من الانتظام والصلاح أتم مما إذا لم يكن لهم مثل هذا الرئيس والعلم به ضروري بعد استقراء العادات، فثبت أن نصب الرئيس يقتضي اندفاع أنواع من المضار لا تدفع إلا بنصبه، وإذا كان كذلك كان هذا الرئيس دافعا للضرر عن النفس. وأما أن دفع الضرر عن النفس واجب بقدر الإمكان فهذا متفق عليه بين العقلاء، أما عند من يقول بالحسن والقبح العقليين فإنه يقول : وجوب هذا معلوم في بداهة العقول، وأما عند من ينكر ذلك فإنه يقول : وجوب هذا ثابت بإجماع الأنبياء
And we have regarding it two stations: one of them is to clarify that it is obligatory upon the people to appoint a leader for themselves. The second is to clarify that it is not obligatory upon God to appoint an Imam for them. As for the first station, the proof for it is that the appointment of an Imam entails the repelling of harm that is not repelled except by his appointment, and repelling harm from oneself is obligatory as much as possible, and this necessitates that it is obligatory upon rational people to appoint an Imam for themselves. Clarification of the first station: that it is obligatory upon the people to appoint a leader for themselves is that we see that a country, if it has a dominant, awe-inspiring, governing leader who commands them to good actions and forbids them from evil deeds, the condition of the country in being far from disturbance and corruption and close to order and rectitude is more complete than when they do not have such a leader, and knowledge of this is necessary after induction of customs. So it is established that the appointment of a leader entails the repelling of types of harms that are not repelled except by his appointment, and if that is the case, then this leader is a repeller of harm from oneself. As for the fact that repelling harm from oneself is obligatory as much as possible, this is agreed upon among rational people. As for those who affirm rational good and evil, they say: its obligation is known in the primordiality of intellects. As for those who deny that, they say: its obligation is established by the consensus of the prophets.
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage presents Razi's own position on the obligation of Imamate. He outlines two stations: first, proving that it is obligatory upon the people to appoint a leader; second, proving that it is not obligatory upon God to do so. Here he elaborates the first station. The proof is in two parts. First, the appointment of an Imam entails repelling harm that cannot be repelled otherwise. Second, repelling harm from oneself is obligatory as much as possible—this is universally accepted, either by rational intuition or by consensus of the prophets. Therefore, rational people must appoint an Imam. To support the first premise, Razi argues from empirical observation: when a country has a strong, authoritative leader who commands good and forbids evil, it is more orderly and less corrupt than when it lacks such a leader. This is known necessarily after considering common customs. Thus, the leader's appointment repels harms that would otherwise occur, and since repelling harm is obligatory, the appointment becomes obligatory. Razi notes that the obligation of repelling harm is agreed upon by all rational people, differing only in its basis: for those who believe in rational good and evil, it is self-evident; for others, it is proven by the consensus of prophets.

Key Terms
مقامان
دفع الضرر
قاهر مهيب سائس
حسن وقبح عقلي
بداهة العقول
إجماع الأنبياء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Razi's proof depends solely on the doctrine of rational good and evil (حسن وقبح عقلي), making it invalid for those who reject that doctrine.
Correction: Razi builds his proof on a universal agreement: repelling harm is obligatory. He notes that even those who deny rational ethics accept this through the consensus of the prophets, so the argument works for all rational people.

Checkpoint
What is the second premise of Razi's proof for the obligation of appointing an Imam?
A) That the Imam must be infallible
B) That repelling harm from oneself is obligatory as much as possible
C) That God commands it in revelation
D) That the country will be prosperous

Key Takeaway
Razi proves the obligation of Imamate by combining empirical observation of societal order with the rational principle that repelling harm is obligatory, ensuring the argument is accepted by all rational people regardless of their ethical framework.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You see corruption and disorder around you—in your family, community, or even in your own soul. Yet you hesitate to step up and take responsibility, telling yourself 'it's not my job.' Razi says that appointing a leader to repel harm is obligatory, and repelling harm is a duty for all rational people. What harm in your life are you leaving unrepelled because you refuse to act? What concrete step will you take this week to become a repeller of that harm?
?
The proof Razi offers begins with an empirical observation: countries with strong leaders are more orderly. Look at your own inner life—your habits, your schedule, your spiritual state. Do you have a 'leader' within yourself that commands good and forbids evil? Or are you ruled by impulse and neglect? What would it cost you to appoint your intellect as that commanding, awe-inspiring guide? And what would it cost you not to?
?
Razi grounds the duty to repel harm in a principle agreed upon by all rational people. Think of a specific harm you know you should address—maybe a quarrel among friends, an injustice at work, or a sin you tolerate. What is stopping you? Is it fear of losing comfort, reputation, or convenience? If you truly believe that repelling harm is a universal duty, how will you answer when you stand before God and He asks you why you left that harm unrepelled?
