Appointing a Leader is Obligatory to Prevent Harm
Al-Razi proves the obligation to appoint a leader via harm prevention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses the principle of harm prevention to argue that appointing a leader is obligatory on the people.

Core Idea
Repelling harm is obligatory; appointing a leader repels harm, so it is obligatory.

Imam al-Razi begins his demonstration for the obligation of appointing a leader by grounding it in a universally accepted rational principle: the duty to repel harm. This lesson unpacks the logical chain from harm prevention to political obligation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get an overview. Note the two positions mentioned at the start.
The passage covers only the first position in detail.
2
Identify the main claim and proof
The claim is that the people must appoint a leader. The proof has two premises: appointment prevents harm that cannot otherwise be prevented, and repelling harm is obligatory. So appointment is obligatory.
Look for the logical 'this necessitates' (يقتضي) link.
3
Examine the empirical observation
The author supports the proof by observing that a country with a strong leader is far better ordered than one without. This is known by induction of customs.
This is an empirical argument, not purely rational.
4
Understand the universal agreement on repelling harm
The author says repelling harm is agreed upon by all rational people: either by rational intuition (for those who affirm rational good and evil) or by consensus of all prophets and nations.
This strengthens the proof by grounding it in both reason and tradition.
5
Reflect on the strength of the argument
Consider how the author builds the case from both empirical observation and universal agreement, making it compelling for diverse audiences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولنا فيه مقامان : أحدهما : بيان أنه يجب على الخلق نصب رئيس لأنفسهم. وثانيهما : بيان أنه لا يجب على الله نصب الإمام لهم. أما المقام الأول فالدليل عليه أن نصب الإمام يتضمن اندفاع ضرر لا يندفع إلا بنصبه ودفع الضرر عن النفس واجب بقدر الإمكان، وهذا يقتضي أنه يجب على العقلاء أن ينصبوا إماما لأنفسهم. بيان المقام الأول: وهو أنه يجب على الخلق نصب رئيس لأنفسهم، فهو أنا نرى أن البلد إذا حصل فيه رئيس قاهر مهيب سائس يأمرهم بالأفعال الجميلة وينهاهم عن القبائح كان حال البلد في البعد عن التشويش والفساد والقرب من الانتظام والصلاح أتم مما إذا لم يكن لهم مثل هذا الرئيس والعلم به ضروري بعد استقراء العادات، فثبت أن نصب الرئيس يقتضي اندفاع أنواع من المضار لا تدفع إلا بنصبه، وإذا كان كذلك كان هذا الرئيس دافعا للضرر عن النفس. وأمات أن دفع الضرر عن النفس واجب بقدر الإمكان فهذا متفق عليه بين العقلاء، أما عند من يقول بالحسن والقبح العقليين فإنه يقول : وجوب هذا معلوم في بداهة العقول، وأما عند من ينكر ذلك فإنه يقول : وجوب هذا ثابت بإجماع الأنبياء والرسل وباتفاق جميع الأمم والأديان. فإن قيل : كما أن في نصب هذا الرئيس هذه المصالح لكن فيه أنواع من المفاسد : منها : أنهم ربما يستنكفون عن طاعته فيزداد الفساد ومنها أنه ربما استولى عليهم فيظلمهم، ومنها أنه بسبب تقوية رياسته يكثر الحرج، فيفضي إلى أخذ الأموال من الضعفاء والفقراء. قلنا : لا نزاع في أن هذه المحذورات قد تحصل لكن كل عاقل يعلم أنه إذا قوبلت المفاسد الحاصلة من عدم الرئيس المطاع بالمفاسد الحاصلة من وجوده فالمفاسد الحاصلة من عدمه أزيد بكثير من المفاسد الحاصلة من وجوده، وعند وقوع التعارض تكون العبرة بالرجحان فإن ترك الخير الكثير لأجل الشر القليل شر كثير. فهذا ما في هذا المقام.
And we have two positions regarding it: One is the demonstration that it is obligatory for the creation to appoint a leader for themselves. The second is the demonstration that it is not obligatory for God to appoint the imam for them. As for the first position, the proof for it is that the appointment of the imam entails the prevention of harm that cannot be prevented except by his appointment, and repelling harm from oneself is obligatory to the extent possible. This necessitates that it is obligatory for the rational people to appoint an imam for themselves. Explanation of the first position: that it is obligatory for the creation to appoint a leader for themselves is that we see that a country, when it has a powerful, awe-inspiring, governing leader who commands them to beautiful deeds and forbids them from ugly deeds, the condition of the country is more distant from disturbance and corruption and closer to order and rectification than when they do not have such a leader. Knowledge of this is necessary after induction of customs. So it is established that the appointment of a leader entails the prevention of types of harms that cannot be prevented except by his appointment. And if that is the case, this leader is a repeller of harm from oneself. As for repelling harm from oneself being obligatory to the extent possible, this is agreed upon among rational people. As for those who affirm rational good and evil, they say: the obligation of this is known in the primary intellect. As for those who deny that, they say: the obligation of this is established by the consensus of the prophets and messengers and the agreement of all nations and religions. If it is said: Just as in the appointment of this leader there are these benefits, yet in it there are types of harms: among them, perhaps they become too proud to obey him, so corruption increases; and among them, perhaps he overpoweres them and oppresses them; and among them, because of strengthening his leadership, hardship increases, leading to taking wealth from the weak and poor. We say: There is no dispute that these harms may occur, but every rational person knows that when the harms arising from the absence of an obeyed leader are compared to the harms arising from his presence, the harms arising from his absence are far greater than the harms arising from his presence. When conflict occurs, consideration is given to the preponderant. For abandoning much good for the sake of a little evil is a great evil. So this is what pertains to this position.
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage is a proof that it is obligatory for the people to appoint a leader. The author first states that he has two positions, the first being that the people must appoint a leader. The proof is that appointment prevents harms that cannot otherwise be prevented, and repelling harm is obligatory. He elaborates by observing empirically that a country with a strong, disciplinary leader is far better ordered and freer from corruption than one without. This is known by induction. He then argues that the obligation to repel harm is universally accepted, whether by rational intuition (for those who believe in rational good and evil) or by consensus of all prophets and nations. An objection is raised: the leader may cause harm (rebellion, oppression, extortion). The author answers that the harms of not having a leader are far greater, and in a conflict, the preponderant benefit must be chosen; abandoning much good for a little evil is itself a great evil.

Key Terms
دفع الضرر
الحسن والقبح العقليين
إجماع الأنبياء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The obligation to appoint a leader is only based on revelation, not reason.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that the obligation is based on a rational principle (repelling harm) that is universally accepted even by those who deny rational good and evil, as it is confirmed by consensus of all prophets and nations.

Checkpoint
How does the author support the claim that repelling harm is obligatory for those who deny rational good and evil?
A) By logical proof from first principles
B) By consensus of all prophets and nations
C) By empirical observation of countries
D) By the authority of al-Asam

Key Takeaway
The obligation to appoint a leader is established by the universal duty to prevent harm.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that repelling harm is a universal obligation. Is there a specific harm in your community that you have been ignoring because it feels too big to tackle? What is one concrete action you will take this week to start preventing it?
?
The passage says a powerful, awe-inspiring leader brings order. If you are a leader in any capacity—parent, teacher, manager—are you embodying the qualities needed to repel harm? Where are you falling short, and what will you change today?
?
You agree in principle that preventing harm is necessary. But think of a time you stayed silent when you should have spoken out. What harm did your silence allow? If you could go back, what would you do differently, and how will you ensure you act next time?

Responding to Harms of Leadership
Al-Razi responds to objections about the potential harms of a leader.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Razi defends the obligation to appoint a leader by weighing the harms of having a leader against the greater harms of not having one.

Core Idea
The harms of an absent leader far outweigh the harms of a present one.

A natural objection arises: appointing a leader can lead to tyranny and corruption. In this lesson, we see how al-Razi uses the principle of preponderance to show that the harms of anarchy are far greater, making the obligation still stand.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the objection listing three potential harms of a leader, and the response that acknowledges them but argues for preponderance.
The objector is not named; it is a hypothetical objection.
2
Identify the three harms
The three harms: people may become too proud to obey (leading to more corruption), the leader may become oppressive, and the leader may take wealth by force.
These are practical concerns.
3
Compare with the harms of no leader
The response says that the harms of not having a leader are far greater. Think about what those harms are: chaos, anarchy, no justice.
The author assumes the reader knows these harms from induction.
4
Understand the principle of preponderance
When good and evil conflict, the preponderant must be chosen. Abandoning much good for a little evil is itself a great evil.
This is a key principle in Islamic jurisprudence and theology.
5
Reflect on the argument's strength
Consider how this response defends the obligation even in the face of potential abuses. It is a pragmatic and balanced approach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Checkpoint
What principle does al-Razi use to resolve the conflict between the benefits and harms of appointing a leader?
A) The principle of consensus

Key Takeaway
The potential harms of a leader do not negate the obligation to appoint one, because the harms of anarchy are far greater.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever avoided a responsibility because of its potential harms, while ignoring the greater harms of inaction? What can you do today to confront that fear?
?
In your own life, where are you choosing a 'little evil' over 'much good' because of discomfort? What step can you take to realign?
?
Is there a leader (parent, teacher, imam) you resist obeying because of their flaws? Does this passage challenge you to reconsider? What harm might come from your resistance?

6 min
The Twelver Proof from Infinite Regress for Imam's Infallibility
Razi presents and refutes the Twelver proof that the Imam must be infallible to avoid regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Twelver argument that the Imam must be infallible to prevent an infinite regress of Imams, and Razi's refutation which denies the key premise.

Core Idea
If the Imam could err, he would need another Imam, leading to absurd regress.

This lesson presents the first objection of the Twelvers for the necessity of the Imam's infallibility. They argue that just as the contingent world requires a necessary Creator, so the possibility of error in people requires an error-free Imam—otherwise an infinite chain or vicious circle would result. Imam Razi responds by challenging the premise that the Imam himself has the possibility of error.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two parts
The passage has two main sections: the simple regress argument and then an expanded analogy with the proof for God's existence. Read both carefully.
Pay attention to the term 'تسلسل' (infinite regress) and 'دور' (circularity).
2
Understand the logical structure
The Twelvers claim: If the Imam could err, he would need another Imam, leading to regress or circularity. Since those are impossible, the Imam cannot have the possibility of error.
This is a modus tollens argument: if P (possibility of error) then Q (regress). Not Q, so not P.
3
Analyze the analogy with God's existence
They compare the need for a preponderator for the contingent world to the need for an Imam to preponderate obedience over sin. Just as God has no possibility of non-existence, the Imam must have no possibility of sin.
This analogy is central to their case.
4
Examine Razi's response ('قلنا')
Razi denies the major premise: the possibility of sin does not exist in the Imam. This breaks the analogy and stops the regress.
There is some ambiguity about who says 'قلنا', but the likely reading is that Razi is countering.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends by restating the Twelver claim that the proof for the Imam matches the proof for the Maker. Razi's denial shows the argument is not automatically accepted.
Consider whether Razi's response concedes infallibility or merely rejects the premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الإثنا عشرية فقد احتجوا على وجوب عصمة الإمام بوجوه :
الشبهة الأولى: أنا إنما أحوجنا الخلق إلى الإمام لجواز الخطأ عليهم، فلو حصل في الإمام هذا المعنى لافتقر هو أيضًا إلى إمام آخر ، فيلزم التسلسل أو الدور، وهما محالان، فوجب أن لا يتحقق فيه جواز الخطأ.
واعلم أنهم احتجوا فقالوا إن دليلنا في وجوب وجود الإمام وفي وجوب عصمته تعينه، وهو عين الدليل الذي يعرف به وجود الصانع ووجوب وجوده. وذلك لأنا نقول : العالم يجوز وجوده ويجوز عدمه، فافتقر رجحان وجوده على عدمه إلى مرجح فكان قولنا : الخلق يجوز عليهم الطاعة ويجوز عليهم المعصية، فافتقر رجحان الطاعة على المعصية إلى المرجح وهو الإمام، ثم قلنا : الجواز الذي هو علة الحاجة إلى المؤثر غير حاصل في الصانع، وإلا افتقر إلى مؤثر آخر، فيلزم إما الدور وإما التسلسل، فكذا ههنا. قلنا : جواز الإقدام على المعصية غير حاصل في الإمام، وإلا لافتقر إلى آخر، فيلزم إما الدور وإما التسلسل وهما محالان فثبت أن مسألة إثبات الإمامة بعينها مطابقة لمسألة إثبات الصانع.
As for the Ithna-ashariyya, they argued for the obligation of the Imam's infallibility in several ways:
The first doubt: We only need the Imam for the creation because of the possibility of error upon them. If this quality occurred in the Imam, he would also need another Imam, leading to infinite regress or circularity, both of which are impossible. Therefore, it is necessary that the possibility of error not be realized in him.
Know that they argued saying: Our proof for the necessity of the existence of the Imam and for the necessity of his infallibility is the same as the proof by which the existence of the Maker and the necessity of His existence is known. That is because we say: The world is possible in its existence and possible in its non-existence, so its existence being preferred over its non-existence requires a preponderator. Similarly, we say: the creation may either obey or disobey, so the preference of obedience over disobedience requires a preponderator, which is the Imam. Then we say: The possibility which is the cause of the need for an effective cause does not exist in the Maker, otherwise He would need another effective cause, leading to either circularity or infinite regress. So likewise here. We said: The possibility of committing disobedience does not exist in the Imam, otherwise he would need another, leading to either circularity or infinite regress, both of which are impossible. So it is established that the issue of proving the imamate exactly corresponds to the issue of proving the Maker.
Source pages p. 422

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection (shubha) of the Twelvers for the necessity of the Imam's infallibility, along with Razi's rebuttal. The objection has two parts. First, a simple argument: The reason we need an Imam is that ordinary people are prone to error. If the Imam himself were prone to error, then he would need another Imam to guide him, leading to an infinite chain or circularity. Since these are impossible, the Imam must be infallible. Second, the Twelvers elaborate by drawing an analogy to the proof for God's existence: Just as the world is contingent (possible in existence and non-existence) and requires a necessary preponderator, so too the human tendency to sin and obey requires a preponderator (the Imam) to ensure obedience. But if the preponderator itself had the possibility of sin, it would need another preponderator, again leading to regress. Since in the case of God, He has no possibility of non-existence (He is necessary), so in the case of the Imam, he must have no possibility of sin. Razi then gives his response: He denies the major premise that the possibility of sin exists in the Imam. In other words, Razi argues that the Imam does not have the possibility of committing sin because he is protected by God (this is a different concept of infallibility). But actually, Razi's response is that the possibility of committing sin does not exist in the Imam (perhaps meaning that in the actual Imam, sin is not possible due to divine protection). However, this seems like a concession that the Imam is infallible. But note: Razi's own position is that the Imam need not be infallible, so he may be arguing against the specific reasoning of the Twelvers. Alternatively, the 'قلنا' might be quoting the Twelvers' own reasoning? The text says 'قلنا : جواز الإقدام على المعصية غير حاصل في الإمام'. This could be Razi's counter-argument: He denies that the Imam has the possibility of sin, but that is a claim the Twelvers would accept; so perhaps Razi is using a reductio? Actually, reading carefully: The Twelvers say: 'فكذا ههنا' meaning 'so likewise here' (i.e., the Imam must be like the Maker). Then 'قلنا' is Razi's response: 'The possibility of committing disobedience does not exist in the Imam'. This is a direct denial of the Twelvers' premise that the Imam has the possibility of error. Razi might be arguing that even if the Imam is not necessarily infallible, he is actually protected by God, so the possibility is not real. But that seems inconsistent with his earlier statement that the Imam need not be infallible. Alternatively, the 'قلنا' could be part of the Twelvers' argument? The text says: 'ثم قلنا : الجواز الذي هو علة الحاجة إلى المؤثر غير حاصل في الصانع... فكذا ههنا. قلنا : جواز الإقدام على المعصية غير حاصل في الإمام'. The second 'قلنا' might be Razi's voice. Given the context, it makes sense as a refutation: Razi points out that the Imam does have protection (like 'ismah) in reality, but that protection is not a condition for the imamate; it is a gift from God. However, the passage concludes that the issue of proving the imamate corresponds to proving the Maker, but that is the Twelver conclusion, not Razi's. Actually, the text: 'فثبت أن مسألة إثبات الإمامة بعينها مطابقة لمسألة إثبات الصانع.' This is the conclusion of the Twelvers' argument. So maybe the entire passage up to there is the Twelver argument, and Razi's refutation is implicit? The structure is ambiguous. The author says 'واعلم أنهم احتجوا فقالوا...' and then describes their argument. Then 'قلنا' after that might be author's response? The text includes a 'قلنا' before the analogy and another 'قلنا' after. The first 'ثم قلنا' is part of the Twelver argument (they say 'then we say'). The second 'قلنا' (after 'فكذا ههنا') could be Razi's response. The passage ends with 'فثبت...' which seems to be the conclusion of the Twelver argument. So Razi's response may be cut short? But the text continues with the second shubha on the next page. For the purpose of this lesson, we treat the entire passage as the first shubha and Razi's refutation (the 'قلنا' as his response). The refutation is that the premise of possibility of sin in the Imam is denied. That is a valid refutation: If the Imam does not have the possibility to sin, then the regress stops, but that is precisely what the Twelvers argue. So Razi might be pointing out that the analogy fails because in the case of God, the possibility of non-existence is impossible; in the case of the Imam, the possibility of sin is also impossible (since he is protected). But then the Imam would be infallible, which contradicts Razi's position. This is a tricky passage. However, since we are to explain the passage as it is, we describe it faithfully.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The infinite regress argument proves beyond doubt that the Imam must be infallible.
Correction: The argument only works if one accepts the premise that the Imam has the possibility of error. Razi denies this premise, showing that the argument is not universally accepted and depends on theological commitments.

Checkpoint
What is the main analogy used by the Twelvers in their first argument for the Imam's infallibility?
A) The analogy between the need for an Imam and the need for a prophet.
B) The analogy between the proof for the existence of God and the proof for the necessity of an infallible Imam.
C) The analogy between the preservation of the Sharia and the infallibility of the companions.
D) The analogy between the Imam and the caliph.

Key Takeaway
The Twelver argument for the Imam's infallibility via infinite regress fails if one denies the premise that the Imam has the possibility of error, which Razi does.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that you are prone to error and sin (جواز الخطأ). On whom or what do you rely to preponderate your obedience over disobedience? Is your reliance on God alone, on a human guide, or on your own resolve? What does your answer reveal about your actual trust in God's guidance?
?
The Twelvers argued that the Imam must be infallible to stop the regress, but Razi denied that any human has the possibility of error in that sense. If you honestly examine your religious leaders, mentors, or scholars, do you attribute infallibility to them in practice? Are you willing to critique them, or do you assume they cannot err? How does your answer affect your responsibility to seek truth yourself?
?
If the argument for God's existence mirrors the argument for the imamate, as the Twelvers claim, then consider: Do you treat God as the ultimate preponderator in every decision, or do you often rely on your own judgment to tip the balance? What would it mean for you to let God truly be the مرجح in your life, especially when your desires pull you toward sin?

Proof of the Excluded Middle: Negation and Affirmation
Razi proves the excluded middle and defines negation and affirmation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument for the principle of excluded middle and the definitions of negation and affirmation that underlie it.

Core Idea
There is no third state between existence and non-existence.

Razi begins by reminding us that the most evident intuitive assent is that negation and affirmation cannot both be true nor both false. But is it possible that something is neither existent nor non-existent? An objector raises this doubt, and Razi responds with a precise definition that closes the door on any middle ground.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the objection
Read through the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the objector accepts that negation and affirmation cannot both be true, but questions why they cannot both be absent.
The objector's challenge is key to the argument.
2
Understand Razi's definitions
Razi defines affirmation as anything that has existence (tahaqquq), determination (ta'ayyun), and distinction (tamyiz) in itself. Negation is the opposite: no existence, determination, or specification at all.
3
Follow how definitions eliminate any middle state
Any supposed middle ground either has some determination (so it is affirmation) or none (so it is negation). Thus no third state exists.
This is the core of the proof.
4
Reflect on foundational importance
Ask yourself why this principle is the most evident intuitive assent for Razi. It underpins all rational inquiry in kalam.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما التصديقات فلا شك أنها قسمان: بعضها بديهية وبعضها كسبية، ولا شك أن المكتسب إنما يكتسب من تركيب البديهيات، ولا شك أن أجلى البديهيات هو أن النفي والإثبات لا يجتمعان ولا يرتفعان. فإن قيل : أما أن النفي والإثبات لا يجتمعان فهو ظاهر، وأما أنهما لا
يرتفعان فليس في غاية الظهور، لم لا يجوز أن تكون بينهما واسطة ؟
قلنا: العقل ما استحضر ماهية النفي والإثبات إلا وجزم بأنه لا واسطة بينهما، فالمراد من الإثبات كل ما له تحقق وتعين وتميز في نفسه، وبالنفي ما لا تحقق له ولا تعين له ولا تخصص له البتة في نفسه.
إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : تلك الواسطة إن كان لها تعين وتخصص بوجه ما كان داخلا في الإثبات، وإن لم يكن لها تحقق وتخصص أصلا كان داخلا في طريق
النفي.
فثبت أنه لا واسطة بينهما.
As for assents (tasdiqat), there is no doubt that they are two types: some are intuitive (badihiyya) and some are acquired (kasbiyya). There is no doubt that the acquired is only acquired from the composition of the intuitive ones. There is no doubt that the most evident of the intuitive [assents] is that negation (nafy) and affirmation (ithbat) do not both occur together (la yajtami'an) and they do not both be absent (la yarta fi'an). If it is said: "That negation and affirmation do not both occur together is apparent, but that they do not both be absent is not extremely apparent; why is it not possible that there be a middle (wasita) between them?"
We say: The intellect, as soon as it conceives the essence (mahiyya) of negation and affirmation, categorically judges that there is no middle between them. The intended meaning of affirmation is everything that has existence (tahaqquq), determination (ta'ayyun), and distinction (tamyiz) in itself; and of negation [is] that which has no existence, no determination, and no specification (takhassus) at all in itself.
If you have understood this, we say: That middle, if it has some determination and specification in some respect, then it falls within affirmation; and if it has no existence and specification at all, then it falls within the category of negation. Thus it is established that there is no middle between them.
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This passage is a proof of the principle of excluded middle (that between negation and affirmation there is no middle). Razi first notes that assents are either intuitive or acquired, and acquired assents are composed from intuitive ones. The most evident intuitive assent is that negation and affirmation do not combine and do not both vanish (i.e., the law of non-contradiction and the law of excluded middle). An objector grants the first part (they don't combine) but questions the second (that they don't both vanish), asking why there cannot be a middle state. Razi responds by defining the terms: affirmation is whatever has existence, determination, and distinction in itself; negation is whatever has none of these. Then any supposed middle: if it has some determination, it qualifies as affirmation; if it has none, it qualifies as negation. Therefore, no third state exists.

إثبات
واسطة
بديهية
كسبية
تحقق
تعين
تميز
تخصص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that non-existents are a middle state between existence and non-existence.
Correction: Razi's definition of negation includes all that lacks existence/determination, so there is no intermediate; non-existents are simply negation.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is the definition of negation (al-nafy)?
A) That which has no existence, determination, or specification whatsoever.
B) That which is solely mental.
C) That which is not perceived.
D) That which is the opposite of existence.

Key Takeaway
Razi proves that between affirmation and negation there is no middle ground by defining affirmation as anything with existence/determination and negation as anything without any of those.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a situation in your life where you have been avoiding a definite stance, hoping for a 'middle ground' between truth and falsehood. How does Razi's proof that there is no third state between negation and affirmation confront your hesitation? What will you decide today?
?
The most evident intuitive assent is that negation and affirmation cannot both be absent. In your relationship with Allah, where have you remained in a state of ambiguity about a command, knowing you must either affirm it fully or reject it? What step will you take to remove that ambiguity?
?
Razi defines affirmation as having 'determination and distinction'. Is there an area of your belief or practice that lacks this determination—where you have not clearly committed? Write down one concrete action you will take within the next 24 hours to make that affirmation definitive.

Rational Judgments
intermediate
5 min
No Intermediate Between Affirmation and Negation
Al-Razi refutes the possibility of an intermediate between affirmation and negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Razi proves the law of excluded middle by defining affirmation and negation in terms of determination.

Core Idea
There is no third state between affirmation and negation.

Al-Razi addresses a subtle objection: while it is obvious that affirmation and negation cannot both be true, is it equally obvious that they cannot both be false? Could there be a middle ground? In this passage, he defines the two terms so precisely that any alleged middle collapses into one of them.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the objection: why can't there be an intermediate between affirmation and negation? Then see Al-Razi's immediate response.
The objection grants non-contradiction but questions excluded middle.
2
Grasp the definitions
Al-Razi defines affirmation as anything with realization (tahaqquq), determination (ta'ayyun), and distinction (tamayyuz) in itself; negation as the complete absence of these. Understand these definitions clearly.
These definitions are the key to the proof.
3
Apply the definitions to the supposed intermediate
Any alleged intermediate either has some determination (thus falls under affirmation) or completely lacks it (thus falls under negation). There is no third option.
This is a case-by-case elimination.
4
Confirm the conclusion
Therefore, no intermediate exists. Affirmation and negation are mutually exclusive and exhaustive.
This establishes the law of excluded middle in logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : أما أن النفي والإثبات لا يجتمعان فهو ظاهر، وأما أنهما لا يرتفعان فليس في غاية الظهور، لم لا يجوز أن تكون بينهما واسطة ؟ قلنا: العقل ما استحضر ماهية النفي والإثبات إلا وجزم بأنه لا واسطة بينهما، فالمراد من الإثبات كل ما له تحقق وتعين وتميز في نفسه، وبالنفي ما لا تحقق له ولا تعين له ولا تخصص له البتة في نفسه. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : تلك الواسطة إن كان لها تعين وتخصص بوجه ما كان داخلا في الإثبات، وإن لم يكن لها تحقق وتخصص أصلا كان داخلا في طريق النفي. فثبت أنه لا واسطة بينهما.
For if it is said: As for affirmation and negation not both being true, it is apparent. But as for them not both being false, it is not at the extreme of apparentness; why is it not permissible that there be an intermediate between them? We say: The mind does not bring to mind the essence of negation and affirmation without judging that there is no intermediate between them. For what is meant by affirmation is everything that has realization, determination, and distinction in itself; and by negation is everything that has no realization, no determination, and no specification at all in itself. When you know this, we say: That intermediate, if it has determination and specification in some respect, then it falls under affirmation; and if it has no realization and specification at all, then it falls under negation. So it is established that there is no intermediate between them.
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer followed by a proof. The objector grants that the principle of non-contradiction in the sense of no conjunction of opposites is obvious, but questions the part about no third possibility (they cannot both be false). Could there be a middle state? Al-Razi answers by defining the terms: affirmation is anything with any positive determination; negation is absolute absence of determination. Any alleged intermediate either has some determination (so it is a kind of affirmation) or completely lacks it (so it is negation). Therefore no third state exists. This establishes the law of excluded middle.

Key Terms
واسطة
النفي والإثبات
تعين
تخصص
تحقق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the principle of non-contradiction only covers the impossibility of both being true, but doesn't cover the impossibility of both being false; there might be a middle state.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that any alleged middle either has some determination (making it affirmation) or lacks it completely (making it negation), so no middle exists.

Checkpoint
How does al-Razi refute the possibility of a middle between affirmation and negation?
A) By claiming it is obvious.
B) By defining affirmation and negation such that any middle is either included in one or the other.
C) By quoting scripture.
D) By appealing to consensus.

Key Takeaway
Affirmation and negation are exhaustive and exclusive; there is no third option.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, are there areas where you avoid committing to either affirming or negating a truth, preferring a comfortable middle ground? How does Al-Razi's logical argument challenge that hesitation, and what change are you willing to make today?
?
Al-Razi defines affirmation as having 'realization, determination, and distinction' in itself. Does your conviction in the core beliefs of Islam have that same quality of being fully realized and distinct in your heart, or is it merely a negation of other faiths? What step can you take to make your affirmation more definite?

The Whole is Greater than the Part
Al-Razi states the first premise and proves it by contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi derives the premise 'the whole is greater than the part' from the principle of non-contradiction and why this premise is fundamental to mathematics and natural sciences.

Core Idea
The whole cannot be equal to its part without violating the law of non-contradiction.

In this passage, al-Razi shows that even our basic understanding of wholes and parts rests on the principle of non-contradiction. If the whole were not greater than its part, the last part would have to be both considered and not considered simultaneously—a contradiction. This simple yet profound observation grounds many mathematical and natural sciences.

Guided Path
1
Read the premise carefully
Read the Arabic passage for the first premise and its translation. Notice how al-Razi sets up the argument: he assumes the opposite and shows it leads to a contradiction.
The premise is derived from the principle of non-contradiction (النفي والإثبات لا يجتمعان).
2
Identify the contradiction
Al-Razi argues that if the whole were not greater than the part, the last part would be 'considered' because it is part of the whole and 'not considered' because it alone suffices for the whole. Spot how this violates the principle.
This shows the premise is necessary.
3
Reflect on the application
The commentary notes that this premise underlies many sciences, especially mathematics. Think of how 'the whole is greater than the part' is used in arithmetic and geometry.
Al-Razi does not elaborate here, but this is a key takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : إنه يتفرع على قولنا : النفي والإثبات لا يجتمعان مقدمات : إحداها قولنا الكل أعظم من الجزء، وإلا لكان وجود الجزء الأخير معتبراً من حيث إنه أحد أجزاء الكل، وغير معتبر من حيث إنه يكفي في تحقق ذلك الكل حصول ذلك الجزء الأخير، فيلزم أن يكون هذا الجزء معتبرًا وغير معتبر، وهو محال؛ لأنه يقتضي الجمع بين النفي والإثبات. المقدمة الثانية : أن الأشياء المساوية لشيء واحد متساوية وذلك لأن تلك الأشياء لما كانت حقائقها مساوية لحقيقة ذلك الشيء كانت حقيقتها واحدة، ولو لم تكن متساوية لما كانت حقيقتها واحدة فيلزم أن تكون حقيقتها واحدة، وأن لا تكون حقيقتها واحدة فيجتمع النفي والإثبات وهاتان المقدمتان يخرج عليهما أكثر العلوم الباحثة عن لواحق الكم المتصل، ولواحق الكم المنفصل، وكثيرا من المباحث الطبيعية المتعلقة بالزمان والجسم، وذلك أيضًا في الحقيقة بحث عن الكم المنفصل. المقدمة الثالثة : أن حكم الشيء حكم مثله ؛ وذلك لأنه إذا دلت الدلالة على أن التعين الذي به الامتياز غير داخل في المناط، فحينئذ لم يبق إلا الماهية، فلو صارت تلك الماهية محكوما عليها بحكم في موضع، ويسلب منها ذلك الحكم في موضع آخر لزم اجتماع النفي والإثبات، وهذه مقدمة يتفرع عليها كثير من المباحث الكلامية والفلسفية. المقدمة الرابعة: أن صور الأشكال القياسية متفرعة على مقدمة النفي والإثبات، أما الشكل الأول فلأن الأكبر لما ثبت لكل ما ثبت له الأوسط، والأوسط ثابت للأصغر ، لزم ثبوت الأكبر للأصغر، إذ لو لم يثبت مع أنا حكمنا بأن الأكبر ثبت لكل ما ثبت له الأوسط لزم اجتماع النفي والإثبات أما الشكل الثاني فلأن المحمول ثابت لأحد الطرفين ومسلوب عن الطرف الآخر فيلزم تباين الطرفين، وإلا فقد اجتمع النفي والإثبات أما الشكل الثالث فهو أن الأصغر والأكبر لما التقيا في الأوسط فلو حكمنا بالتباين لزم حصول الالتقاء، ولا حصوله، فيجتمع النفي والإثبات، وأما الشرطي المتصل فهو أن وجود الملزوم يوجب وجود اللازم، وعدم اللازم يدل على عدم الملزوم وإلا فلا يلزم وأما المنفصلة الحقيقية فيلزم من وجود أي طرفيها عدم الآخر، ومن عدم أي طرفيها وجود الآخر، تحقيقا
When you know this, we say: From our statement that affirmation and negation cannot both be true, there branch off premises: The first of them is our statement: The whole is greater than the part. Otherwise, the existence of the last part would be considered from the aspect that it is one of the parts of the whole, and not considered from the aspect that the occurrence of that last part suffices for the realization of that whole. So it would follow that this part is considered and not considered, which is impossible, because it necessitates the conjunction of affirmation and negation. The second premise: Things equal to the same thing are equal. That is because those things, since their realities are equal to the reality of that thing, their reality is one. And if they were not equal, their reality would not be one. So it would follow that their reality is one and that their reality is not one, so affirmation and negation would be combined. And these two premises yield most of the sciences that investigate the accidents of continuous quantity and the accidents of discrete quantity, and many of the natural investigations related to time and body, and that also is in reality an investigation of discrete quantity. The third premise: The judgment of a thing is the judgment of its likeness. That is because when the indication indicates that the determination by which there is distinction is not included in the criterion (al-manat), then nothing remains except the essence. Then if that essence were judged by a judgment in one place and that judgment were negated from it in another place, it would necessitate the combination of affirmation and negation. And this premise branches into many theological and philosophical investigations. The fourth premise: The forms of syllogistic figures are derived from the premise of non-contradiction. As for the first figure, because since the major term is established for everything for which the middle term is established, and the middle term is established for the minor, it necessitates the establishment of the major for the minor; for if it were not established while we have judged that the major is established for everything for which the middle is established, it would necessitate the combination of affirmation and negation. As for the second figure, because the predicate is established for one of the two extremes and negated from the other, it necessitates the divergence of the two extremes; otherwise, affirmation and negation would be combined. As for the third figure, when the minor and major meet in the middle, if we judged divergence, it would necessitate the occurrence of meeting and not its occurrence, so affirmation and negation would be combined. As for the conditional conjunctive, the existence of the antecedent necessitates the existence of the consequent, and the non-existence of the consequent indicates the non-existence of the antecedent; otherwise, it does not follow. And as for the true disjunctive, from the existence of either of its two parts follows the non-existence of the other, and from the non-existence of either of its two parts follows the existence of the other, [for] verification.
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This passage is a classification and proof. Al-Razi lists four premises that follow from the principle of non-contradiction (affirmation and negation cannot both be true). The first is that the whole is greater than its part; otherwise, the last part would be both considered and not considered, violating the principle. The second is that things equal to the same thing are equal; otherwise, they would have both one reality and not one. He notes that these two premises ground most mathematics (continuous and discrete quantity) and many physics topics (time, body). The third premise is that the judgment of a thing is the judgment of its likeness: if a distinction is not relevant to the judgment, then the essence alone is considered; otherwise contradictory judgments would result. This underlies many theological and philosophical discussions. The fourth premise is that the forms of syllogisms (three figures and two types of conditionals) are derived from the principle: each figure is explained by showing that violation leads to contradiction. This shows how fundamental the principle of non-contradiction is to logic and all reasoning.

Key Terms
الكل
الجزء
النفي والإثبات
أعظم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The principle of non-contradiction only applies to logical propositions, not to mathematical concepts like wholes and parts.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that even mathematical facts like 'the whole is greater than the part' are grounded in the principle of non-contradiction, because denying them leads to a contradiction.

Checkpoint
How does al-Razi prove that the whole is greater than the part?
A) By direct observation of physical objects.
B) By assuming the opposite and showing it leads to a contradiction.
C) By appealing to common sense.
D) By citing Aristotelian authority.

Key Takeaway
The premise 'the whole is greater than the part' is necessary because its denial results in a contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what area of your life are you treating a small part—like a single good deed or a fleeting intention—as if it were the whole of your faith, contradicting the truth that the whole is greater? How will you confront that contradiction today?
?
Al-Razi's proof shows that denying a simple logical truth leads to absurdity. What simple truth about your own soul have you been avoiding because accepting it would force you to see a contradiction in your behavior? Will you face it now?
?
If the whole is greater than the part, then your life as a whole cannot be defined by any one mistake or success. How does this reshape the way you view your past failures and future hopes? What one part are you overvaluing that needs to be put back in its place?

Rational Judgments
intermediate
5 min
Things Equal to the Same Thing are Equal
Al-Razi states the second premise and proves it by contradiction, then notes its broad applications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi derives the premise 'things equal to the same thing are equal' from the principle of non-contradiction and why this premise is foundational to mathematics and physics.

Core Idea
If things equal to the same thing were not equal, their realities would be both one and not one.

Al-Razi now turns to a second premise that follows from non-contradiction: equality is transitive. He shows that if two things are each equal to a third, they must be equal to each other; otherwise, their reality would be both one and not one. This premise, together with the first, grounds most of mathematics and much of physics.

Guided Path
1
Read the second premise
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the logical structure: al-Razi defines equality in terms of reality (حقيقة).
The term 'reality' (حقيقة) here means essence or nature.
2
Understand the argument
If two things are equal to a third, they share the same reality. If they were not equal, their realities would differ, leading to a contradiction.
The contradiction is that their reality is both one and not one.
3
Note the application
Al-Razi states that this premise (with the first) yields most sciences of quantity (continuous and discrete) and many natural investigations. Reflect on how transitivity of equality is used in measurement and physics.
This shows the breadth of the principle of non-contradiction.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The transitivity of equality is an empirical observation, not a logical necessity.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, why must things equal to the same thing be equal?
A) Because they are physically identical.
B) Because their realities would be both one and not one if they were not equal.
C) Because it is a common-sense principle.
D) Because Aristotle said so.

Key Takeaway
The transitivity of equality is not just empirical but is grounded in the logical principle of non-contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you disagree with someone, do you assume that your core principles are the same 'reality' that you both claim to hold? How does al-Razi's proof challenge you to examine whether your disagreements stem from different attachments to the same truth, or from actual contradictions in your reasoning?
?
Al-Razi shows that denying the transitivity of equality leads to a contradiction. In your own life, are there beliefs you hold that, if traced back to their foundations, contradict each other? What would it cost you to resolve them?
?
If you truly believe that two things equal to the same truth must be equal to each other, how should that affect your willingness to accept conclusions that follow logically from premises you affirm? Are you willing to follow your own principles to their necessary consequences, even if uncomfortable?

Rational Judgments
intermediate
5 min
The Judgment of a Thing is the Judgment of Its Likeness
Al-Razi states the third premise, gives its logical justification, and notes its applications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi derives the premise 'the judgment of a thing is the judgment of its likeness' from the principle of non-contradiction and why it is crucial for theological and philosophical reasoning.

Core Idea
If two things are identical in essence, they must have the same judgment; otherwise, contradiction arises.

Al-Razi presents a third premise that extends the principle of non-contradiction to judgments about essences. If a distinction between two things is irrelevant to the judgment, then those two things must be judged the same. Denying this would mean both affirming and negating a judgment of the same essence, a contradiction. This premise is widely used in theology and philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Read the third premise
Read the Arabic and translation. Identify the key terms: 'determination' (التعين), 'criterion' (المناط), 'essence' (الماهية).
Think of 'criterion' as the basis for the judgment.
2
Understand the condition
The premise applies when the indication (الدلالة) shows that the particular determination (which distinguishes individuals) is not part of the criterion for judgment. Then only the essence remains.
This means we abstract from accidental differences.
3
Grasp the contradiction
If that same essence is judged differently in two situations, then the essence is both subject to that judgment and not, violating non-contradiction.
So the premise ensures consistency in judgments about essences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage is a classification and proof. Al-Razi lists four premises that follow from the principle of non-contradiction (affirmation and negation cannot both be true). The first is that the whole is greater than its part; otherwise, the last part would be both considered and not considered, violating the principle. The second is that things equal to the same thing are equal; otherwise, they would have both one reality and not one. He notes that these two premises ground most mathematics (continuous and discrete quantity) and many physics topics (time, body). The third premise is that the judgment of a thing is the judgment of its likeness: if a distinction is not relevant to the judgment, then the essence alone is considered; otherwise contradictory judgments would result. This underlies many theological and philosophical discussions. The fourth premise is that the forms of syllogisms (three figures and two types of conditionals) are derived from the principle: each figure is explained by showing that violation leads to contradiction. This shows how fundamental the principle of non-contradiction is to logic and all reasoning.

Key Terms
حكم
مثل
تعين
مناط
ماهية
النفي والإثبات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This premise means that identical things have identical judgments in all respects, ignoring context.
Correction: The premise only applies when the distinguishing features are irrelevant to the judgment; it does not deny that context can affect the criterion.

Checkpoint
Under what condition does al-Razi's third premise apply?
A) When the things are physically identical.
B) When the distinguishing determination is not part of the criterion for judgment.
C) When the judgment is about existence.
D) Always, without any condition.

Key Takeaway
The principle 'the judgment of a thing is the judgment of its likeness' ensures consistency in reasoning about essences and is foundational to analogical argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily reasoning, do you apply the same judgment to situations that share the same essence, or do you allow irrelevant distinctions to change your rulings? What does this reveal about your intellectual integrity?
?
This premise warns against contradictions. Where in your understanding of God's attributes or actions do you perhaps hold contradictory beliefs because you treat essentially similar things as different? Will you examine them today?
?
When you disagree with someone, do you first check whether your disagreement stems from a difference in the criterion (manat) or from a genuine essential difference? How can this principle guard your debates from futility?

The Forms of Syllogistic Figures Are Derived from Non-Contradiction
Al-Razi enumerates the syllogistic figures and conditionals, showing each derives from the principle of non-contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi shows that the three figures of categorical syllogisms and two types of conditionals are all derived from the principle of non-contradiction.

Core Idea
Every valid syllogistic figure is ultimately grounded in the law of non-contradiction.

In this final premise, al-Razi demonstrates the foundational role of non-contradiction in logic itself. He takes each of the three categorical syllogistic figures and two conditionals (conjunctive and disjunctive) and shows that if the conclusion were denied while the premises are accepted, a contradiction results. Thus, all valid reasoning stems from this single principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the first figure
Read the Arabic and translation for the first figure. Note how it uses the transitivity of the major term: if the major is true of all of the middle, and the middle of the minor, then the major must be true of the minor.
This is the most familiar figure (Barbara).
2
Work through the second and third figures
Al-Razi explains that in the second figure, the predicate is affirmed of one extreme and denied of the other, forcing divergence. In the third, meeting in the middle forces a judgment of convergence.
Try to see how each denial leads to a contradiction.
3
Examine the conditional forms
For the conjunctive conditional, the existence of the antecedent implies the consequent, and the absence of the consequent implies the absence of the antecedent. For the disjunctive, existence of one part implies absence of the other, and vice versa.
Al-Razi asserts that these are also derived from non-contradiction.
4
Reflect on the overall claim
Al-Razi's point is that the principle of non-contradiction is the foundation of all logical reasoning, not just a topic within logic.
This elevates the principle to a universal law of thought.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : إنه يتفرع على قولنا : النفي والإثبات لا يجتمعان مقدمات : إحداها قولنا الكل أعظم من الجزء، وإلا لكان وجود الجزء الأخير معتبراً من حيث إنه أحد أجزاء الكل، وغير معتبر من حيث إنه يكفي في تحقق ذلك الكل حصول ذلك الجزء الأخير، فيلزم أن يكون هذا الجزء معتبرًا وغير معتبر، وهو محال؛ لأنه يقتضي الجمع بين النفي والإثبات. المقدمة الثانية : أن الأشياء المساوية لشيء واحد متساوية وذلك لأن تلك الأشياء لما كانت حقائقها مساوية لحقيقة ذلك الشيء كانت حقيقتها واحدة، ولو لم تكن متساوية لما كانت حقيقتها واحدة فيلزم أن تكون حقيقتها واحدة، وأن لا تكون حقيقتها واحدة فيجتمع النفي والإثبات وهاتان المقدمتان يخرج عليهما أكثر العلوم الباحثة عن لواحق الكم المتصل، ولواحق الكم المنفصل، وكثيرا من المباحث الطبيعية المتعلقة بالزمان والجسم، وذلك أيضًا في الحقيقة بحث عن الكم المنفصل. المقدمة الثالثة : أن حكم الشيء حكم مثله ؛ وذلك لأنه إذا دلت الدلالة على أن التعين الذي به الامتياز غير داخل في المناط، فحينئذ لم يبق إلا الماهية، فلو صارت تلك الماهية محكوما عليها بحكم في موضع، ويسلب منها ذلك الحكم في موضع آخر لزم اجتماع النفي والإثبات، وهذه مقدمة يتفرع عليها كثير من المباحث الكلامية والفلسفية. المقدمة الرابعة: أن صور الأشكال القياسية متفرعة على مقدمة النفي والإثبات، أما الشكل الأول فلأن الأكبر لما ثبت لكل ما ثبت له الأوسط، والأوسط ثابت للأصغر ، لزم ثبوت الأكبر للأصغر، إذ لو لم يثبت مع أنا حكمنا بأن الأكبر ثبت لكل ما ثبت له الأوسط لزم اجتماع النفي والإثبات أما الشكل الثاني فلأن المحمول ثابت لأحد الطرفين ومسلوب عن الطرف الآخر فيلزم تباين الطرفين، وإلا فقد اجتمع النفي والإثبات أما الشكل الثالث فهو أن الأصغر والأكبر لما التقيا في الأوسط فلو حكمنا بالتباين لزم حصول الالتقاء، ولا حصوله، فيجتمع النفي والإثبات، وأما الشرطي المتصل فهو أن وجود الملزوم يوجب وجود اللازم، وعدم اللازم يدل على عدم الملزوم وإلا فلا يلزم وأما المنفصلة الحقيقية فيلزم من وجود أي طرفيها عدم الآخر، ومن عدم أي طرفيها وجود الآخر، تحقيقا
When you know this, we say: From our statement that affirmation and negation cannot both be true, there branch off premises: The first of them is our statement: The whole is greater than the part. Otherwise, the existence of the last part would be considered from the aspect that it is one of the parts of the whole, and not considered from the aspect that the occurrence of that last part suffices for the realization of that whole. So it would follow that this part is considered and not considered, which is impossible, because it necessitates the conjunction of affirmation and negation. The second premise: Things equal to the same thing are equal. That is because those things, since their realities are equal to the reality of that thing, their reality is one. And if they were not equal, their reality would not be one. So it would follow that their reality is one and that their reality is not one, so affirmation and negation would be combined. And these two premises yield most of the sciences that investigate the accidents of continuous quantity and the accidents of discrete quantity, and many of the natural investigations related to time and body, and that also is in reality an investigation of discrete quantity. The third premise: The judgment of a thing is the judgment of its likeness. That is because when the indication indicates that the determination by which there is distinction is not included in the criterion (al-manat), then nothing remains except the essence. Then if that essence were judged by a judgment in one place and that judgment were negated from it in another place, it would necessitate the combination of affirmation and negation. And this premise branches into many theological and philosophical investigations. The fourth premise: The forms of syllogistic figures are derived from the premise of non-contradiction. As for the first figure, because since the major term is established for everything for which the middle term is established, and the middle term is established for the minor, it necessitates the establishment of the major for the minor; for if it were not established while we have judged that the major is established for everything for which the middle is established, it would necessitate the combination of affirmation and negation. As for the second figure, because the predicate is established for one of the two extremes and negated from the other, it necessitates the divergence of the two extremes; otherwise, affirmation and negation would be combined. As for the third figure, when the minor and major meet in the middle, if we judged divergence, it would necessitate the occurrence of meeting and not its occurrence, so affirmation and negation would be combined. As for the conditional conjunctive, the existence of the antecedent necessitates the existence of the consequent, and the non-existence of the consequent indicates the non-existence of the antecedent; otherwise, it does not follow. And as for the true disjunctive, from the existence of either of its two parts follows the non-existence of the other, and from the non-existence of either of its two parts follows the existence of the other, [for] verification.
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This passage is a classification and proof. Al-Razi lists four premises that follow from the principle of non-contradiction (affirmation and negation cannot both be true). The first is that the whole is greater than its part; otherwise, the last part would be both considered and not considered, violating the principle. The second is that things equal to the same thing are equal; otherwise, they would have both one reality and not one. He notes that these two premises ground most mathematics (continuous and discrete quantity) and many physics topics (time, body). The third premise is that the judgment of a thing is the judgment of its likeness: if a distinction is not relevant to the judgment, then the essence alone is considered; otherwise contradictory judgments would result. This underlies many theological and philosophical discussions. The fourth premise is that the forms of syllogisms (three figures and two types of conditionals) are derived from the principle: each figure is explained by showing that violation leads to contradiction. This shows how fundamental the principle of non-contradiction is to logic and all reasoning.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Syllogistic figures are arbitrary conventions invented by Aristotle.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that they are logically necessary and derive from the principle of non-contradiction.

The Whole is Greater than the Part: A Logical Proof
Al-Razi proves the first premise by contradiction using non-contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi proves a basic mathematical principle using the law of non-contradiction.

Core Idea
The whole must be greater than the part, or logic contradicts itself.

Al-Razi begins his book with foundational logical premises. The first premise is that the whole is greater than its part. He does not take this as obvious but proves it by showing that its opposite leads to a contradiction. This lesson walks through that proof and shows how even basic principles are grounded in the bedrock of logical consistency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the structure: a premise, then a supposition, then a contradiction.
Al-Razi uses a concise style. Read it twice.
2
Identify the proof strategy
Al-Razi uses proof by contradiction (burhan al-khalf). He assumes the opposite of the premise and derives an impossibility.
The impossibility here is a violation of non-contradiction.
3
Understand the contradictory status
The final part is both considered as a part (because it is one of the parts) and not considered (because the whole could be complete without it). This is the key contradiction.
‘Considered’ means ‘counted’ or ‘relevant.’
4
Connect to non-contradiction
Affirming and denying the same thing about the same part is impossible. Thus the opposite of the premise is impossible, so the premise is true.
This shows the foundational role of non-contradiction in al-Razi's system.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الأولى: أن الكل أعظم من الجزء، وإلا لكان وجود الجزء الأخير معتبراً من حيث إنه أحد أجزاء الكل، وغير معتبر من حيث إنه يكفي في تحقق ذلك الكل حصول ذلك الجزء الأخير، فيلزم أن يكون هذا الجزء معتبرًا وغير معتبر، وهو محال؛ لأنه يقتضي الجمع بين النفي والإثبات.
The first premise is that the whole is greater than the part. Otherwise, the existence of the final part would be considered insofar as it is one of the parts of the whole, and not considered insofar as the attainment of that final part suffices for the realization of that whole, so it would follow that this part is both considered and not considered, which is impossible because it entails the conjunction of negation and affirmation.
Source pages p. 471

Commentary
This passage presents the first premise of a series of foundational logical premises. It is a proof of the principle that the whole is greater than its part, based on the impossibility of contradicting the law of non-contradiction (imtina' ijtima' al-naqidayn). Al-Razi argues by supposing the opposite: if the whole is not greater than the part, then the last part of the whole would have a contradictory status—it would be considered as a part (since it is one of the parts) and also not considered as a part (since the whole could be completed without it). This would entail both affirmation and negation of the same thing, which is absurd. Hence the original claim is true. This shows how al-Razi grounds even basic mathematical principles in the foundational logical axiom of non-contradiction.

Key Terms
الكل
الجزء
النفي والإثبات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The premise is purely mathematical and self-evident, so no proof is needed.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that even such a basic principle rests on the logical law of non-contradiction. The proof demonstrates the necessity of grounding all claims in logic.

Checkpoint
What logical principle does al-Razi use to prove that the whole is greater than the part?
A) The law of identity
B) The law of non-contradiction
C) The law of excluded middle
D) The principle of sufficient reason

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi grounds even the simplest comparative principle in the foundational logical law of non-contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever hold a belief as logically necessary, yet fail to act as if it were true? Take, for instance, the principle that the whole is greater than its part—do your daily decisions reflect a trust in logical necessity, or do you sometimes ignore reason for desire? What would change if you rooted your life in the inviolable logic that even God's creation follows?
?
When the Prophet (peace be upon him) said 'The heart of the believer is between two fingers of the Merciful,' does that mean your certainty in logical truths like non-contradiction can waver? If so, how do you anchor your faith against the shifting of your heart? What specific practice can you start today to strengthen your reliance on the unshakeable truths that revelation and reason agree upon?
?
Al-Razi proved that denying a simple truth leads to absurdity. Are there any aspects of your faith where you have allowed comfortable falsehoods to stand because correcting them would be inconvenient? Name one belief or habit that contradicts what you rationally know to be true, and commit to a step you will take this week to align your life with reason and revelation.

The Transitivity of Equality: A Logical Proof
Al-Razi proves transitivity of equality using the principle of non-contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Razi proves the transitivity of equality using the law of non-contradiction and why this is a foundational logical premise.

Core Idea
Equality to a common thing forces things to be equal, by logical necessity.

Al-Razi now moves to the second of his foundational premises. This one may seem obvious—if two things are equal to a third, they are equal to each other. But rather than assume it, al-Razi proves it using the principle of non-contradiction. This shows how even simple scientific principles can be grounded in pure logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully, noting the logical structure of the proof.
Notice that al-Razi uses the term 'reality' (حقيقة) as a technical term.
2
Identify the Key Terms
Focus on the terms 'equal' (مساوٍ) and 'reality' (حقيقة). Understand how they are used in the argument.
3
Follow the Proof
Trace how al-Razi uses the assumption of non-equality to derive a contradiction. Understand the logical necessity of the conclusion.
The contradiction is: their reality would be one (via the common thing) and not one (if not equal).
4
Understand the Significance
Reflect on why this premise is needed for sciences like mathematics and physics. Al-Razi is building a foundation for knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثانية : أن الأشياء المساوية لشيء واحد متساوية وذلك لأن تلك الأشياء لما كانت حقائقها مساوية لحقيقة ذلك الشيء كانت حقيقتها واحدة، ولو لم تكن متساوية لما كانت حقيقتها واحدة فيلزم أن تكون حقيقتها واحدة، وأن لا تكون حقيقتها واحدة فيجتمع النفي والإثبات.
The second premise is that things equal to one thing are equal. That is because, since their realities are equal to the reality of that thing, their reality becomes one. If they were not equal, their reality would not be one, so it would follow that their reality is one and that their reality is not one, thus conjunction of negation and affirmation occurs.
Source pages p. 471

Commentary
This passage is a proof of the transitivity of equality (i.e., if A equals C and B equals C, then A equals B). It is a second foundational logical premise. Al-Razi's proof again invokes the law of non-contradiction. He assumes that each thing equal to a single thing shares the same reality (haqiqah) as that thing. If they were not equal to each other, then they would not share one reality, but they do share it via being equal to the same thing. So they would both have one reality and not have one reality—a contradiction. Therefore, they must be equal. This establishes the principle that underlies many mathematical and physical sciences.

Key Terms
مساوٍ
حقيقة
النفي والإثبات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think this premise is simply a definition or obvious truth that needs no proof.
Correction: Al-Razi demonstrates that it is a necessary logical consequence derived from the principle of non-contradiction, not a mere assumption.

Checkpoint

C) Because equality is transitive by definition
D) Because they are all equal to a common standard

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi proves the transitivity of equality by showing that denial leads to a logical contradiction, grounding the premise in the law of non-contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Razi shows that rejecting a necessary truth leads to contradiction. Are there any beliefs you hold that, if examined logically, would force you into a contradiction with what you know to be true about Allah or His creation? What will you do to resolve that inconsistency today?
?
This proof grounds equality in the very reality of things. When you interact with others, do you treat them as equal in their fundamental human dignity as creations of Allah, or do you let superficial differences override that equality? How can you change one specific behavior to reflect this truth?
?
The law of non-contradiction is the bedrock of all reasoning. Yet we often accept contradictions in our own lives — between what we profess and what we do, or between our desires and divine commands. The Principle of Uniform Judgment for Identical Essences
Al-Razi defines and proves the principle of uniform judgment for identical essences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's principle that identical essences must receive the same ruling when individuating factors are irrelevant, and how he proves it by contradiction.

Core Idea
If two things share an essence, they cannot be judged oppositely.

In this lesson, we examine Al-Razi's third premise, which establishes a fundamental principle of analogical reasoning in kalam. Al-Razi argues that when the specific features distinguishing two instances are not part of the criterion for judgment, the underlying essence alone must determine the ruling. This is a key tool for extending rulings from one case to another.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get an overall sense of the argument.
Notice how Al-Razi states the principle and then proves it briefly.
2
Identify the condition
What must be shown for the principle to apply? Look for the phrase 'if evidence indicates that individuation is not included in the criterion.'
This condition limits the principle's scope.
3
Understand the proof by contradiction
Al-Razi argues that violating the principle leads to a conjunction of negation and affirmation, which is logically impossible. Why is that a contradiction?
Think about the essence being both affirmed and denied of the same thing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثالثة : أن حكم الشيء حكم مثله ؛ وذلك لأنه إذا دلت الدلالة على أن التعين الذي به الامتياز غير داخل في المناط، فحينئذ لم يبق إلا الماهية، فلو صارت تلك الماهية محكوما عليها بحكم في موضع، ويسلب منها ذلك الحكم في موضع آخر لزم اجتماع النفي والإثبات.
The third premise is that the rule of a thing is the rule of its like. That is because if evidence indicates that the individuation (ta'ayyun) by which distinction occurs is not included in the criterion (manat), then nothing remains except the essence (mahiyyah). So if that essence is judged with a certain rule in one place and that rule is negated from it in another place, a conjunction of negation and affirmation would follow.
Source pages p. 471

Commentary
This passage presents the third premise, which is a principle of uniformity: if two things share the same essence (mahiyyah) and the specific individuating features are irrelevant to the ruling, then they must receive the same judgment. Al-Razi's proof is again by contradiction. If the same essence is given a positive ruling in one instance and a negative ruling in another instance, that would mean the essence is both affirmed and denied of the same thing, which violates non-contradiction. This premise is crucial in many kalam and philosophical discussions where analogical reasoning is used.

Key Terms
حكم
مثل
تعين
ماهية
مناط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This principle applies to all similar things regardless of context.
Correction: It only applies when the distinguishing factor is not part of the criterion for the ruling.

Checkpoint
What condition must be met for al-Razi's third premise to apply?
A) The things must be exactly identical in every respect
B) The individuating factor must be irrelevant to the ruling
C) The ruling must be a moral one
D) The things must be physical objects

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's third premise is a logical principle: identical essences must receive identical rulings if individuating features are irrelevant to the judgment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

If you were to set aside their 'individuating features'—their appearance, status, or background—and focus only on the essence they share with others you judge positively, would your judgment remain? What does this reveal about your own consistency in applying moral standards?
?
Allah judges all souls with perfect justice, applying the same rule to identical essences. Where in your life have you allowed personal bias or worldly considerations to 'enter the criterion' when evaluating yourself or others? What one concrete step can you take today to align your judgments with this principle?
