The First Three Categorical Syllogistic Figures
Al-Razi explains how the first three categorical syllogistic figures depend on non-contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the first three categorical syllogistic figures depend on the principle of non-contradiction.

Core Idea
Syllogistic validity is grounded in the impossibility of affirming and denying the same thing.

Al-Razi's fourth premise demonstrates that all valid syllogistic forms are derived from the principle of non-contradiction. In this lesson, we explore how the first three figures of categorical syllogisms rely on this fundamental logical law. Each figure is shown to entail a contradiction if its conclusion is denied.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp how each figure is presented.
Notice the repetitive structure: 'أما الشكل الأول... أما الشكل الثاني... أما الشكل الثالث'.
2
Identify the three figures
The passage describes the first, second, and third categorical syllogistic figures. For each, find the reasoning that ties validity to non-contradiction.
3
Understand the logical mechanism
For each figure, Al-Razi shows that if we deny the conclusion, a contradiction arises. Trace how this works for each case.
For example, in the first figure, denying the conclusion would mean the major term both applies and does not apply to the middle term.
4
Reflect on the underlying principle
Ask yourself: Why is non-contradiction the ultimate foundation for syllogistic validity? How does this connect to the broader theme of the course?
This links back to the fourth premise as a whole.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الرابعة: أن صور الأشكال القياسية متفرعة على مقدمة النفي والإثبات، أما الشكل الأول فلأن الأكبر لما ثبت لكل ما ثبت له الأوسط، والأوسط ثابت للأصغر، لزم ثبوت الأكبر للأصغر، إذ لو لم يثبت مع أنا حكمنا بأن الأكبر ثبت لكل ما ثبت له الأوسط لزم اجتماع النفي والإثبات. أما الشكل الثاني فلأن المحمول ثابت لأحد الطرفين ومسلوب عن الطرف الآخر فيلزم تباين الطرفين، وإلا فقد اجتمع النفي والإثبات. أما الشكل الثالث فهو أن الأصغر والأكبر لما التقيا في الأوسط فلو حكمنا بالتباين لزم حصول الالتقاء، ولا حصوله، فيجتمع النفي والإثبات. وأما الشرطي المتصل فهو أن وجود الملزوم يوجب وجود اللازم، وعدم اللازم يدل على عدم الملزوم وإلا فلا يلزم. وأما المنفصلة الحقيقية فيلزم من وجود أي طرفيها عدم الآخر، ومن عدم أي طرفيها وجود الآخر، تحقيقا لما ذكرنا من امتناع اجتماع النقيضين وارتفاعهما. وإذا عرفت هذه المقدمات ظهر لك أن بها يتبين جميع العلوم. صح
The fourth premise is that the forms of syllogistic figures are derived from the premise of negation and affirmation. As for the first figure: since the major term is established for everything for which the middle term is established, and the middle term is established for the minor term, it follows that the major term is established for the minor term; for if it were not established while we judged that the major term is established for everything for which the middle term is established, a conjunction of negation and affirmation would follow. As for the second figure: the predicate is established for one of the two extremes and denied of the other, so it necessitates that the two extremes are mutually exclusive; otherwise, negation and affirmation would have conjoined. As for the third figure: since the minor and major terms meet in the middle term, if we judge that they are mutually exclusive, then meeting would occur and not occur, so negation and affirmation would conjoin. As for the conditional conjunctive: the existence of the antecedent necessitates the existence of the consequent, and the non-existence of the consequent indicates the non-existence of the antecedent; otherwise, it would not follow. As for the true disjunctive: it necessitates that from the existence of either of its two parts, the non-existence of the other follows, and from the non-existence of either part, the existence of the other follows, in confirmation of what we mentioned about the impossibility of the conjunction of the two contradictories and their absence. When you understand these premises, it becomes clear to you that all sciences are elucidated by them.
Source pages p. 471

Commentary
This passage is the fourth premise, but it is a long and comprehensive demonstration that all forms of valid syllogism in logic (categorical and conditional) are ultimately derived from the principle of non-contradiction (imtina' ijtima' al-naqidayn). Al-Razi goes through each figure: the first figure works because if the major does not apply to the minor despite being true of everything the middle applies to, then the middle would both have and not have the major—contradiction. The second figure works because if the two extremes are not mutually exclusive (i.e., both could have the predicate), then the predicate would be simultaneously affirmed and denied of one thing. The third figure works because if the minor and major are not both true of the middle, they would both meet and not meet in the middle. For conditionals, al-Razi shows that the conjunctive conditional (if p then q) relies on the impossibility of p being true and q false; the true disjunctive (either p or q exclusively) relies on the impossibility of both being true or both false. He concludes that all sciences become clear through these premises, emphasizing the central role of non-contradiction.

Key Terms
شكل
قياس
النفي والإثبات
أكبر
أوسط
أصغر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Syllogistic validity is purely formal and independent of material logical principles.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that validity ultimately rests on the law of non-contradiction, a material principle that cannot be violated.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, what follows if we deny the conclusion of a valid first figure syllogism?
A) The middle term disappears
B) The major term becomes false
C) A contradiction arises
D) The minor term becomes false

Key Takeaway
The validity of categorical syllogisms depends on the impossibility of affirming and denying the same thing simultaneously.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held two beliefs that contradict each other, and did you try to harmonize them or ignore the contradiction? How does al-Razi’s demonstration that all logical validity rests on non-contradiction compel you to examine your own inconsistent beliefs? This week, identify one contradiction in your thinking and resolve it by seeking the truth.
?
In your daily arguments, do you rely on hidden contradictions to persuade others, or do you base your reasoning on the impossibility of contradiction? How can you ensure that your reasoning is grounded in truth rather than rhetorical tricks? Commit to using syllogistic clarity in your next discussion.
?
If the law of non-contradiction is the foundation of all sound reasoning, how seriously do you take the command to avoid hypocrisy (contradiction between your words and deeds)? Reflect on one area where your actions contradict your stated beliefs and make a plan to align them within the next week.

Conditional Syllogisms and Non-Contradiction
Al-Razi explains how conjunctive conditional and true disjunctive syllogisms derive from non-contradiction and concludes that all sciences depend on these premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how conjunctive conditional and disjunctive syllogisms are grounded in the principle of non-contradiction.

Core Idea
Conditional and disjunctive syllogisms also derive their validity from non-contradiction.

In this continuation of the fourth premise, Al-Razi extends the application of non-contradiction to conditional syllogisms. He shows that the conjunctive conditional (if p then q) and the true disjunctive (either p or q exclusively) both rely on the impossibility of affirming and denying simultaneously. The lesson closes with his assertion that all sciences become clear through these premises.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for conditional forms
Read the Arabic text for the conjunctive conditional and the true disjunctive. Pay attention to how each form is defined.
Note the terms 'شرطي متصل' and 'منفصلة حقيقية'.
2
Understand the conjunctive conditional
Al-Razi explains that if the antecedent (ملزوم) exists, the consequent (لازم) must exist; and if the consequent is absent, the antecedent must be absent. Otherwise, the implication fails. This depends on non-contradiction: if both p and not-q could be true, the implication would be false.
3
Understand the true disjunctive
For the true disjunctive, if one part exists, the other cannot exist; and if one part is absent, the other must exist. This is based on the impossibility of both being true or both false (i.e., non-contradiction and excluded middle).
Al-Razi explicitly mentions the impossibility of 'اجتماع النقيضين وارتفاعهما' (conjunction and absence of the two contradictories).
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Al-Razi concludes that when you understand these premises, all sciences become clear. Why does he claim such a foundational role for these logical principles?
This underscores the importance of logic for all knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الرابعة: أن صور الأشكال القياسية متفرعة على مقدمة النفي والإثبات، أما الشكل الأول فلأن الأكبر لما ثبت لكل ما ثبت له الأوسط، والأوسط ثابت للأصغر، لزم ثبوت الأكبر للأصغر، إذ لو لم يثبت مع أنا حكمنا بأن الأكبر ثبت لكل ما ثبت له الأوسط لزم اجتماع النفي والإثبات. أما الشكل الثاني فلأن المحمول ثابت لأحد الطرفين ومسلوب عن الطرف الآخر فيلزم تباين الطرفين، وإلا فقد اجتمع النفي والإثبات. أما الشكل الثالث فهو أن الأصغر والأكبر لما التقيا في الأوسط فلو حكمنا بالتباين لزم حصول الالتقاء، ولا حصوله، فيجتمع النفي والإثبات. وأما الشرطي المتصل فهو أن وجود الملزوم يوجب وجود اللازم، وعدم اللازم يدل على عدم الملزوم وإلا فلا يلزم. وأما المنفصلة الحقيقية فيلزم من وجود أي طرفيها عدم الآخر، ومن عدم أي طرفيها وجود الآخر، تحقيقا لما ذكرنا من امتناع اجتماع النقيضين وارتفاعهما. وإذا عرفت هذه المقدمات ظهر لك أن بها يتبين جميع العلوم. صح
The fourth premise is that the forms of syllogistic figures are derived from the premise of negation and affirmation. As for the first figure: since the major term is established for everything for which the middle term is established, and the middle term is established for the minor term, it follows that the major term is established for the minor term; for if it were not established while we judged that the major term is established for everything for which the middle term is established, a conjunction of negation and affirmation would follow. As for the third figure: since the minor and major terms meet in the middle term, if we judge that they are mutually exclusive, then meeting would occur and not occur, so negation and affirmation would conjoin. As for the conditional conjunctive: the existence of the antecedent necessitates the existence of the consequent, and the non-existence of the consequent indicates the non-existence of the antecedent; otherwise, it would not follow. As for the true disjunctive: it necessitates that from the existence of either of its two parts, the non-existence of the other follows, and from the non-existence of either part, the existence of the other follows, in confirmation of what we mentioned about the impossibility of the conjunction of the two contradictories and their absence. When you understand these premises, it becomes clear to you that all sciences are elucidated by them.

Commentary
This passage is the fourth premise, but it is a long and comprehensive demonstration that all forms of valid syllogism in logic (categorical and conditional) are ultimately derived from the principle of non-contradiction (imtina' ijtima' al-naqidayn). ?

?
Al-Razi argues that these logical principles underlie all sciences. Think of one belief you hold about your faith or life that you have never examined for consistency. Are you willing today to apply the test of non-contradiction to that belief, even if it unsettles you?
?
The principle of non-contradiction is not just an abstract rule—it reveals the impossibility of combining truth and falsehood in your heart. Is there any area where you are trying to hold both a truth and its opposite? What would it cost you to let go of one?

The Fifth Premise: Distinct Letters Have Distinct Concepts
Al-Razi proves distinctness of concepts via the impossibility of simultaneous knowledge and ignorance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's argument that the mental concepts of distinct letters are different, based on the impossibility of simultaneously knowing and not knowing the same thing.

Core Idea
Two letters cannot share a single concept because we can think of one while forgetting the other.

Al-Razi continues building his premises for arguments about the divine attributes. Here he establishes a key principle about concepts: if two expressions (like 'alif' and 'ba') produce distinct mental understandings, those understandings must be different entities. The proof is straightforward but powerful.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Notice that al-Razi uses a conditional argument: if the concepts were one, then a contradiction would follow.
Pay attention to the key terms: مفهوم (concept) and مغاير (different).
2
Understand the proof structure
Identify the core logical move: we can conceive one letter while being heedless of the other. If the two concepts were identical, then the same thing would be both known (when conceived) and not known (when heedless), which is impossible. Therefore, they are distinct.
This type of argument is called 'reductio ad absurdum' (إبطال اللازم).
3
Reflect on the significance
Al-Razi says this premise clarifies many theological and philosophical investigations. Think about why distinguishing concepts is important when discussing God's attributes.
For example, if God's knowledge and power were the same concept, we couldn't distinguish them rationally.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الخامسة: أن المفهوم من الألف مغاير للمفهوم من الباء، فهما شيئان. وتقريره يرجع إلى تقرير تلك المقدمة، فإنه لما تعقل أحدهما الذهول عن الآخر فلو كانا واحدًا لكان قد صدق عليه أنه معلوم وغير معلوم فيجتمع النفي والإثبات. وبهذه المقدمة تبين كثير من المباحث الكلامية والفلسفية.
The fifth premise is that the concept understood from 'alif' is different from the concept understood from 'ba', so they are two things. Its explanation goes back to the explanation of that premise. For when one of them is conceived while being heedless of the other, if they were one, then it would be true that it is both known and not known, so negation and affirmation would conjoin. And by this premise many theological and philosophical investigations are clarified.
Source pages p. 472

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth premise, which establishes the distinctness of concepts (e.g., the concept of 'A' and the concept of 'B'). Al-Razi proves this by appealing to experience: we can think of one letter while being oblivious to another. If the two concepts were identical, thinking of one would necessarily mean thinking of the other; but we can be conscious of one and not the other. Hence, they must be distinct. If they were the same, the same thing would be both known (when we think of it) and not known (when we do not), which is a contradiction. This premise is said to clarify many discussions in kalam and philosophy, likely including those about divine attributes and their distinction from the essence.

Key Terms
مفهوم
مغاير
الذهول
معلوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The premise is about the physical shapes of letters being different.
Correction: It is about the mental concepts derived from the letters, not the physical forms. The proof relies on mental awareness, not visual differences.

Checkpoint
How does al-Razi prove that the concept of 'alif' is different from the concept of 'ba'?
A) By citing scriptural passages
B) By noting that we can think of one while ignoring the other
C) By measuring brain activity
D) By logical definition of difference

Key Takeaway
The concepts of distinct letters are necessarily different because we can be conscious of one while oblivious to the other, otherwise a contradiction would arise.

The Sixth Premise: Motion and Rest Are Additional to the Essence
Al-Razi proves accidents are superadded to the substance using non-contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that motion and rest must be distinct attributes added to the substance, and why they cannot be identical to the essence itself.

Core Idea
If motion were the substance itself, a moving substance would cease when it rests — but it doesn't.

Al-Razi presents a concise logical proof that motion and rest are not identical to the substance but are additional accidents inhering in it. This argument is foundational for kalam metaphysics, establishing the reality of change without undermining the continuity of the substance. The proof rests on a simple observation and the principle of non-contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's words sink in before interpreting.
Notice the flow: observation → implication → reasoning → conclusion.
2
Identify the key observation
Al-Razi begins with an empirical observation: we see a substance move after being at rest. What does this imply about the relationship between the substance and its states?
3
Understand the hypothetical contradiction
If the substance were identical to motion, then when it becomes at rest, the substance would cease to exist. Since it persists, the identification is impossible. Similarly for rest.
This uses the principle of non-contradiction: the same thing cannot both exist and not exist.
4
Appreciate the conclusion
Therefore, motion and rest must be additional attributes (a'rad) that inhere in the substance, not the substance itself. Al-Razi endorses this as a strong premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة السادسة قولنا رأينا الذات تحركت بعد أن كانت ساكنة فلا بد وأن تكون الحركة والسكون زائدين على الذات وهذه أيضًا ترجع إلى ما ذكرناه؛ لأن الذات باقية في الحالتين وكل واحدة من هاتين الحالتين غير باقية في الحالتين، فلو كانت الذات هي نفس هاتين الحالتين لزم اجتماع النفي والإثبات. وهذه المقدمة الجيدة القوية.
The sixth premise is our statement: we see the essence (dhat) moved after it was at rest, so it is necessary that motion and rest are additional to the essence. This also goes back to what we mentioned, because the essence remains in both states, but each of these two states does not remain in both states. So if the essence were identical to these two states, a conjunction of negation and affirmation would follow. And this is a good, strong premise.
Source pages p. 472

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth premise, a key argument in kalam for the reality of accidents (a'rad) as distinct from the substance (jawhar). Al-Razi argues from observation: we see a substance (dhat) move after being at rest. The substance itself persists through both states, but the states of motion and rest themselves change. If the substance were identical to motion, then when it becomes at rest, the substance would cease to exist (since motion no longer exists). But we know the substance continues. Therefore, motion and rest must be additional attributes superadded to the substance. If they were identical, the substance would have to be both motion and rest simultaneously, a contradiction. Al-Razi endorses this premise as sound and strong.

Key Terms
ذات
حركة
سكون
زائد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument proves that substances are composed of atoms.
Correction: This argument does not address atomism; it only shows that motion and rest are distinct from the substance, not that the substance is composed of discrete parts.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Razi conclude that motion and rest must be additional to the essence?
A) Because motion is a separate physical entity
B) Because the essence remains while motion and rest replace each other
C) Because motion cannot exist without rest
D) Because the senses tell us so

Key Takeaway
Motion and rest are not identical to the substance but are superadded accidents, as shown by the persistence of the substance through change of states.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your essence (your soul) remains constant while your states—like anger, calm, action, rest—change, what does that mean for your accountability before Allah? Are your deeds merely passing accidents, or do they define who you are? How does this passage force you to reconsider the weight of every action you choose?
?
You claim to have a stable faith, yet your state of iman fluctuates. Are you willing to admit that your faith is an accident that can increase and decrease, requiring constant renewal? Or do you mistakenly identify your essence with your current state, risking despair when weak and pride when strong?
?
If motion and rest are additional to the substance, then what you do is not you—but it is yours to choose. Does this thought terrify you or liberate you? What state will you choose to attach to your essence today, knowing it passes but leaves a mark on your soul?
